In C. elegans germl cells can be reprogrammed into somatic cells through chromatin perturbation, and this process is facilitated by the GL-1/Notch signaling pathway, which is typically known for maintaining undifferentiated germline stem/proitor cells. A combination of genetics, tissue-specific transcript analysis, and functional studies of candidate genes revealed that GL-1/Not promotes reprogramming by activating genes silenced by the Polycomb repressive complex 2 (PRC2) and identified the conserved histone demethylase UTX-1 as a crucial GL-1/Not target facilitating reprogramming. GL-1/Not and PR2 have an antagonistic effects on germ cell fate decisions and co-regulate their common target UTX-1. UTX is a histone demethylase that erases gene silencing methylation of histone H3 dependent PR2. GL-1/Not-dependent induction of UTX facilitates reprogramming by alleviating PR2-mediated repression of alternative fates. GL-1/Not and PR2 interact function in a cell-autonomous manner and PR2-repressed genes overlap with Not-activated, particularly those linked to the X chromosome. GL-1/Not induces expression of PR-repressed genes, which is not due to global loss of repressive trimethylation of histone H3 lysine 27 (H3me3). Germ conversion to neurons can be triggered by LIN-53 depletion or PR2 depletion. GL-1/Not and PR2 have antagonistic effects on cell proliferation and gene regulation, and Ge triggered by PR depletion is sensitive to Not signaling. UTX orthologs in species contribute to tissue-specific chromatin signatures and are implicated in germ and somatic reprogramming.
A study investigated the role of the adaptor protein SK2 (SK2) in defense against Klebsiella pneumoniae (K. pneumonia) infection in a murine model of pulmonary infection. SK2 mice had a 100-fold higher bacterial burden compared to wild-type mice, which was controlled by SK2 expression in innate immune cells. SK- neutrophils and monocytes were present in lungs and neutils degranulated normally in response to K. pneumonia infection; however, K pneumonia-stimulated reactive oxygen species (ROS) production was abolished in vitro. K pneumonia-induced neutil ROS required the activity of Src family kinases (SFs), Syk, B, PLC2, and PK. The loss of SK2 significantly hindered K pneumonia-induced phosphorylation of SFs, Sy, and Py2, implicating SK2 as proximal to their activation signaling pathways. SK2 signaling in neutils is essential for K pneumonia-activated ROS production and promoting bacterial clearance during infection.

 SK2 plays a key role in host defense against K pneumonia in the hematopoietic compartment in a murine lung model of infection. SK was indispensable for pneumonia-stimulated neutil ROS, not degranulation phagocytosis, suggesting the increased burden in SK- mice is affected by loss of ROS. Sensitivity of pneumonia to ROS is supported by increased burden in Cyb- mice during lung infection and observations in CG patients. In murine neutils, SK was required full phosphorylation of SFs, Sy, and Py, demonstrating critical in pneumonia-induced pathways for ROS. Consistent previous findings, pneumonia was resistant internalization indicating phocytosis is likely not primary mechanism by neutils to this strain.
A mathematical model of the hypnoite reservoir in Plasmod vivax transmission was developed and validated against data from tropical strains of P vivax. The within-host model of liver-stage hypnoites was embedded in a P viv transmission model to demonstrate the build-up of the hypzo reservoir following new infections and its depletion through hypzo activation and death. The hypzo reservoir was predicted to be dispersed with individuals having few or no hypnoites and some intensely infected livers. Individuals with more hypnoites were predicted to experience relapses and contribute to P viv transmission. Incorporating hypite drugs such as prima into first-line treatment regimens was predicted to cause reductions in P viv transmission as individuals with most hypites were likely to relapse and targeted for treatment. Relapses from hypites in the liver constitute a third parasite reservoir in addition to reservoirs in circulation and mosquitoes for P falcum. Relapses can be incorporated into Ross-Mdon models of transmission through addition of state to represent hypite reservoir. The intensity of hypite infection is crucial for understanding patterns of relapses and evaluating effect of interventions such as prima treatment that target hypite. Hypites are assumed to be subjected to processes activation at constant and death. Considering batches of hypites these processes can explain complex patterns in P viv rel. A key assumption is constant activation of hypites implying rel can occur after primary P viv infection in contrast to first rel occurring 2-3 weeks. Evidence of early rel comes from efficacy studies where treated for P viv followed recurrent for 42 days. In cases with chloro resistance recurrent are rarely observed prior 14 but inclusion long-lasting anti-mals provides prophylaxis making detection unlikely. A study of eliminated dihydroartemipquine showed most relapses were due to hypite activation. Existing models do not account liver reservoir but hypite has been incorporated some P viv transmission as state to denote hypite or two broods. Rel patterns and implications have been investigated using distributions for time to rel. This work advances by considering number of hypites contributes to rel infections and epidemiology of P viv.
Amazonian forests are disturbed by selective logging, releasing 90g of carbon (C) into the atmosphere each year, which is then balanced by forest regrowth. A study of 133 forest plots across Amazonia links climate, soil, and initial biomass with survivors and recruits' carbon fluxes to provide Amazon-wide predictions of post-logging recovery. Aboveground carbon recovery over 10 years is higher in the Guiana Shield and west (21 Mg ha-1) than in the south (12 Mg-1) where environmental stress is high. Survivors play a key role in regrowth and a comprehensive map of post-disturbance recovery in Amazonia is provided.

 Logging losses are determined by harvest intensity and care, affecting collateral damage. After logging, losses continue due to mortality of injured trees. Forests recover above carbon via growth of survivors and recruits. Recovery of pre-disturbance in logged stands requires up to 125 years, depending on intensity. Processes vary with geographical patterns in structure and dynamics across Amazon and Guiana. Northeast-south gradients have been reported for carbon stocks, productivity, density, and floristic composition, coinciding with climate and edaphic conditions.

We partition contributions to post-disturb gain from growth and loss of trees to detect drivers and patterns of recovery in forests by selective logging across Amazonia. Long-term data from 13 sites across Amazon were used to model post-dist changes in a Bayesian framework. Effects of pre-dist ecosystem, disturbance, and environment on post-dist changes are quantified. Global maps of carbon, climate, and soil density are used to upscale results to Amazon, providing predictive maps of changes over 10 years under 40% loss, a common intensity in Amazon. Summing changes gives net post-dist rate of accumulation.

Amazon is a transition zone for environmental and biotic gradients: competition between dense and nutrient-poor northeastern and nutrient-rich western forests, and environmental between northern and southern forests. Survivors contribute to post-dist recovery. Regions with high survivors' gain have high net recovery: annual recovery 1-3 Mg ha-1 in first 10 after logging, lower in secondary forests. Recruits have low variations post- changes: 10 after disturbance store similar amounts almost everywhere Amazon.
Researchers have identified two distinct populations of cells in the adult mouse brain's subventricular zone (SV): pre-GEOT cells and GEOT cells. Pre-GEOT cells are quiescent, lack the ability to form neuropheres, and are long-lived, while GEOT cells are highly mitotically active, form neuropheres, and short-lived. GEOT cells are enriched for neuro-initiating cells (NICs), accounting for 3.2% of SV cells, and include type C cells based on marker expression and morphology. Pre-GE cells account for 6% of SV cells, are quies, lack neurophere formation, and include type 1 cells based on marker and morphology. Conditional deletion of the polycomb protein Bmi-1 depletes both pre-GEOT and GEOT cells, but pre-GE cells are more dependent on Bmi for Cdk2 (16ink4) repression. Data distinguish quies NSCs from NICs and make it possible to study their properties in vivo.

Researchers investigated the influence of volatility on decision-making in perception by introducing a novel manipulation that mimics the addition of noise to motion-selective regions of the visual cortex. They recorded neuronal activity from macaque areas MT/MST and found that increasing noise led to faster decisions and greater confidence over a range of stimuli with minimal accuracy impairment. The effect was consistent with predictions of a bounded accumulation model, which explains the effect of signal-to-noise ratio on decision speed, accuracy, and confidence. The model quantitatively accounted for observed choices, reaction times, and confidence in a coherent way, even predicting confidence ratings based on reaction time data from a separate experiment.

 The manipulation involved changing the statistical properties of a dynamic stimulus, differing from previous studies that varied reliability through stimulus manipulations. The approach applied the manipulation to a well-studied motion task, verified the effect by recording from neurons in the visual cortex, and showed a framework based on accumulation could explain the effect on choice, reaction, and confidence. The framework predicted the pattern of choices, reaction times, and confidence in a quantitatively way, even confidence ratings based on reaction time from a separate experiment.

 The study tested the influence of noise on three observables of behavior - accuracy, reaction time, and confidence - in humans and monkeys. A dissociation between accuracy and confidence led to conclude that transcranial stimulation increased the noise associated with the representation of a pattern, and a dissociation led to conclude that cortical stimulation might affect the mean and variance of the representation of motion in extrastriate visual areas. However, characterization of these effects was inferred from behavior. Psych studies attempted to increase noise through changes in visual or attentional state did not characterize the influence on neural that accumulates to a decision.

 The study sought to manipulate variance associated with representation without affecting mean, achieved with a motion in random motion display, adding a level which increased volatility was unbiased. Verified the manipulation by recording from selective neurons in middle, medial temporal areas of macaque cortex. Neurons in these areas represent momentary in tasks identical to the study. Traces differed when waves on sure trials when responses stronger. Results indicate early evidence informs both choice and confidence, inconsistent with proposal that choice and confidence are resolved in succession.

Researchers studied how fruit flies groom their bodies using genetically targeted activation of neural subsets to drive distinct motor programs. They found that motor programs are organized into a hierarchy, with programs that occur first suppressing those that occur later. Cleaning one body part reduces the drive to its motor, relieving suppression of the next, allowing the sequence to progress down the hierarchy. A model featuring evoked movements in parallel, but selected through hierarchical, was successful in reproducing the sequence. This provides the first example of an innate sequence implemented by a model for generating action.

The study demonstrated that hierarchical suppression among behaviors leads to emergence of a sequence, lending support to the view that hierarchical is a mechanism for selecting actions. Grooming may be considered a series of 'choices' among competing modules selected in order through suppression. Each part cleaned, suppression of other modules is lifted, and new competition between options occurs. Activation and parallel models describe mechanisms for generating behavior, with activation chains through feedforward and parallel through winner-take inhibition.

The researchers used Drosila melanogaster to identify and manipulate subdivisions of behavior in freely moving flies by expressing activators in subsets of neurons. This revealed neural can trigger specific behaviors and components, such as abdominal and courtship. They used this approach to study the organization of serial behavior through control of individual actions in an innate behavior, grooming. Grooming consists of discrete movements in sequences, but mechanisms governing transitions are unknown. Local stimulation causes flies to perform targeted sweeps with front or hind legs, while coated flies coordinate movements to groom whole bodies.

The researchers exploited behavior of dustated flies to determine how actions are prioritized and ordered when stimulated simultaneously. Cleaning progresses to next hierarchically superior. They found hierarchical mediates selection among stimulated modules in competition. The model for motor strategy consists of three layers: sensory, hierarchical, and winner-take. Sensory inputs activate specific modules, hierarchical establishes differences, and winnerake selects the highest suppressing others. Once selected, reduced drive lowers activation, allowing to proceed to next. Groom proceeds from highest to lowest until dust removed.

The study provides a framework for understanding neurons and circuits mediate serial, and basis for extrapolation to organizational logic in network sequences. GAL lines target specific modules without inducing phenotypes. Sensory and command neurons are implicated in initiating movements. Sensory can activate stimulus-response circuits, while command are necessary and sufficient for specific programs. The GAL-targeted neurons show sufficiency for cleaning, may be 'command' or higher interneurons.
Researchers have identified a protein called Shuglin-like 2 (Sg2) that plays a crucial role in coordinating various processes during meiosis I in oocytes, including chromosome segregation, spindle assembly, and kinetochore-microtub interactions. S2 is a member of the shugin family and has a conserved C-terminal region and N-terminal homimer parallel coiled domain, which interacts with protein phosphatase 2 (PPA) and Mad2, a component of the mitotic checkpoint complex (MCC). S2 protects centromeric cohes from separase at the first meiotic division by recruiting PP to centromeres, and silences the spindle assembly checkpoint (SAC) by binding Mad2 and PP. It also moderates chromosome stretching by recruiting kineto the kines MCA and downregulating Aurora B kinase activity at kinetores. S2's ability to protect cohes depends on its interaction with PP, while its ability to silence SAC is mediated by direct binding to Mad2. S's effect on bivalent stretching and kinet formation is independent of PP and mediated by recruitment of MCA and inhibition of Aurora kinase activity, respectively. S2's multiple interactions link many processes for faithful chromosome segregation. In S2 deficient oocytes, chromosome and microtub dynamics are altered, with delayed bi-orientation of bivalents, increased stretching, and increased Aurora B kinase activity at kinetores, accompanied by increased lateral and decreased end kinetoch-microub attachments. S2 is required to shut off SAC, as evidenced by delayed APC/CC20 activation. S's role in meiosis is pleiotropic, affecting multiple processes including SAC, chromosome stretching, and kinet-microub interactions. S's multiple domains regulate different processes, including cohesion, SAC, and kin-micro interactions.
Neuron-wide RNA transport combines with netrin-mediated local translation to spatially regulate the synaptic proteome. Studies have shown that experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity require RNA and protein localization, and local translation plays a role in altering synapse structure and function during formation and plasticity. This study investigates whether local stimulation triggers RNA transport from the nucleus to synapses. Imaging in cultured Aplyia sensory-motor neurons revealed RNAs are delivered throughout the neuron, but translation is enriched at sites of synaptic contact and stimulation. Mechanisms of local translation reveal a role for calcium-dependent netrin-1/DCC signaling. Spatial regulation of gene expression is maximized by local translation rather than RNA delivery from nucleus to synapses. Transcripts, ribosomes, and translational machinery are delivered throughout the neuron, but translation is selectively activated in response to stimuli such as synapse formation. Studies do not rule out localized subcell trafficking within processes, but indicate synapse and stimulation do not regulate trafficking from soma to synapse. Transcripts induced by stimuli are delivered throughout the neuron, with local translation regulated by localized cues. Netrin-1 is investigated as retrograde signal driving translation due to its role in axonal growth, synaptogenesis, and transmembrane regulation in dendrites and axons. Anti-DCC antibodies decrease sensorin immunoreactivity in paired MNs but not isolated SN, consistent with netrin released from MN binding DCC on pre-synaptic SN to promote translation at synapses. Neurotrophins regulate specific RN in axons of regenerating sensory neurons. Activity induces transcription of genes transported into dendrites, such as Arc. In situ hybridization indicates mRNA localized peripheral but not central axons DRG neurons. Findings are consistent with delivering transcripts and transl machinery throughout neuron, with synaptically netrin-dependent triggering translation at synapses.

A normative theory of set effects in human working memory proposes that encoding precision decreases with the number of items due to a trade-off between behavioral cost and neural cost. A model based on this theory predicts size effects, which are mediated by item probability, consistent with previous findings. The model accounts for effects of size and probability on precision in delayed-estimation experiments and shows that sometimes it's optimal to encode a subset or none of relevant items. This raises the possibility that cognitive limitations arise from cost minimization rather than constraints. Descriptive models of working memory lack a principled explanation for size effects, so a normative model was examined. It found that size effects are fully mediated by item probability, consistent with earlier work. The model suggests that as items are added, relevance decreases, making it less efficient to spend resource on encoding. It's optimal to encode a subset of items, implying rationality as a bridge between resource and slot models. The model accounts for data from nine experiments with few parameters and a non-monotonic between size and total resource invested. Implications are that limitations may be driven by cost minimization, not constraints. The theory makes assumptions for further investigation, such as item probability and neural activity. It may apply to attention tasks, explaining size effects. Developmental studies raise the question of optimal trade-off with age. A speculative answer is efficiency improves with age, shifting optimal resource to larger values. Neuroimaging may provide insight into efficiency changes. The theory fits a tradition of normative in psychology and neuroscience, analyzing systems in ecological context.
Researchers investigated how the potato pathogen Phytophthora infest interferes with plant autophagy, a process that helps plants defend against diseases. They found that the pathogen's effector protein Pex54 binds to the autoph protein AT8 to stimulate autag formation and depletes the autagy receptor J2 out of AT8 complexes, counteracting host defenses. This is the first time a pathogen effector has been shown to antagonize a host autagy receptor. Autagy plays a role in plant stress, senescence, development, and defense against pathogens, and is implicated in the accumulation of defense hormones and hypersensitive response, a form of cell death that prevents microbial infection. However, the mechanisms of defense-related autagy and selective autagy are poorly understood. Phytophthora infest is a serious threat to food security, causing losses that could feed hundreds of millions of people. The pathogen delivers RX-type effector proteins inside cells to enable parasitism. RX effectors alter host processes and serve as probes to dissect pathways for pathogen invasion. Pex54 showed higher affinity to AT8 compared to ATIL. Variation between AT proteins may contribute to selective autagy. The study highlights intricate endomembrane formation during plant-micro interactions. Future studies will consider path-directed modulation of autagy and subcell trafficking during infection. Other pathogens may secrete effectors with AT interacting motif or target autagy in ways, serving as tools to dissect mechanisms of defense-related autagy.
Researchers have developed a novel in vitro model to study human cardiac fibroblast (CF) responses to combined mechanical strain and oxygen changes, mimicking pathological conditions such as ischemia. They used a multi-stimulus microdevice to apply cyclic strain and controlled oxygen changes to primary human CFs, revealing differential responses to individual and combined stimuli. Cyclic strain caused proliferative effects (PH3 mitotic cells, YAP translocation, PD secretion) or collagen presence, while hypoxia and loss of contractility (2% strain) induced inflammatory and fibrotic responses. The combination of hypox and reduced contractility elicited increased release of inflammatory cytokines and matrix metalloproteinases. This model recreates a pathological environment for understanding CF responses during cardiac injury, replicating the combination of oxygen and mechanical cues that trigger pathological responses and emerging cellular mechanisms. Insights have impact on future studies of myocardial remodeling and the in vitro model provides a tool for understanding pathological mechanisms and reparative strategies.

Researchers have developed a method to quantify the dynamics of force transmission at cell-cell junctions in epithelial clusters with sub-cell and multi-cellular resolution. They applied this technology to spontaneously forming adherent epithelial cell clusters and found that basal force fluctuations were coupled to E-cadher localization at the level of individual-cell junctions. At the multi-cell scale, cell force depended on the position within the cluster and was adaptive to reconfigurations due to cell divisions or positional rearrangements. Importantly, transmission through a cell required modulation of cell-matrix adhesion and actomy contractility in the cell and its neighbors. These data provide insights into mechanisms that control mechanical homeostasis in epithelial tissues and highlight the method as a resource for study mechanotrans in cell adhesions.

The study found that on softer substrates, mechanisms are less active, preventing force transmission to the center of the cluster, whereas on stiffer substrates, mechanisms are more active, allowing force through the center. Substrate stiffness regulates cell-matrix, cytoskeleton, and traction exertion in clusters, extending multiple layers into a sheet. Understanding mechanical properties influence force in epithelial groups has implications for developmental processes and pathologies such as cancer where stiffness is altered. The method allowed correlation, in quantitative, cell-cell transmission to intracellular dynamics, showing variations in junction forces coupled to Eadher-G turnover at junctions. Future studies can apply the approach for analyses other cell-junction molecules, including force-responsive proteins, such as alpha-caten, vinculin, and Epl.

The method represents a toolkit for investigation of complex homeost mechanisms required for maintenance of stress tissue. Spatial activation of actomy contract above basal levels yields force between cells, or deactivation of actomy and cell-matrix may increase transmission beyond one diameter. The notion of mechanical across tissues via myos-II activation is supported by epithelial migration and invasion, asymmetric actomy contractility promoting cohesion. Analysis of 90 clusters and 250 junctions showed intercell transmission distinct patterns on soft vs stiffer substrates, transmitted along periphery on stiffer, through center on soft. This may result from interplay cell-cell, cell-matrix, basalomy contractility to anchor, scramble forces. Focal adhesion and traction are higher periphery, suggesting forces derived cell-matrix occur mainly periphery.

The organization of the lateral prefrontal cortex (LP) is crucial for higher-level cognition, but its functional organization has remained elusive. A prominent proposal is that the LP is hierarchically organized, with progressively rostral areas processing increasingly abstract information, facilitating efficient and flexible cognition. However, support for this theory has been limited. Human fMRI data revealed rostral/caud gradients of abstraction in LP, and dynamic modeling showed asymmetrical LP interactions indicative of hierarchical processing. Contrary to assumptions, the strength of efferent versus afferent connections positioned midLP as the apex of the hierarchy. Cognitive demands modulations towards LP consistent with a role in integrating information control. The strengths of these dynamics were related to trait-measured higher-level cognitive ability. The study investigated the role of ubiquitination in the transcriptional activity of the immune coactivator NPR1 in Arabidopsis thaliana. It was found that ubiquination of NPR is a stepwise event, with initial modification by the Cullin-RING E3 ligase (CRL) promoting chromatin association and expression of target genes, while polyubiquination by the E4 ligase U4 leads to proteasomal degradation. Conversely, ubiquitin ligase activities by UBP/7, proteas-associated deubitinases, enhance NPR longevity. Immune transcriptome reprogramming requires sequential actions of E and E ligases balanced by opposing deubitinases to fine-tune activity of NPR without sacrificial turnover. The ubiquitin proteasome plays a vital role in regulating eukotic gene expression by controlling the abundance of transcription regulators. Paradoxically, proteome-dependent instability of potent eukotic transcription activators is necessary for expression of their target genes, ensuring continuous supply of fresh activators to initiate transcription. This sacrificial process is energy-expensive, raising a dilemma of why such mechanisms evolved to regulate transcription activators. The study indicates that ubiqu chain and trimming activities can fineune transcription outputs of unstable eukotic activators without strict requirement for sacr turnover. CRL-mediated ubiquination of NPR is important for preventing autoimmunity in absence of pathogen, while C3-mediated ubiquination induced NPR coactiv activity in presence of SA. Polyubiquination by U4 blocked activity and led to proteas-mediated turnover. Initial ubiquination boosts NPR transcription coactiv activity by enhancing promoter occupancy and genomic mobility. The study proposes stepwise ubiquination by sequential E and E ligases generates a transcription timer controlling activity and lifetime of unstable coactivators. The complexity of ubiqu-dependent timer was revealed by identification of UBP and U7 deubiquinating NPR, regulating activity and lifetime.

Researchers investigated the progression of large-scale and cellular activity patterns across development in the brain using high-resolution in vivo electrophysiology in immature mice. They found that mature cortical networks emerge rapidly and simultaneously after a discrete but volatile period at the beginning of the second postnatal week of rodent development. This transition is characterized by neural quiescence, after which spatially distributed, temporally precise, and organized activity occurs. A similar developmental trajectory is observed in humans, suggesting an evolutionarily conserved mechanism that facilitates transition in network operation. The researchers hypothesize that this quiescent period is requisite for the emergence of coordinated cortical networks.

The study demonstrates that maturation of cortical dynamics is preceded by a discrete, relatively quies, evolutionarily conserved period during early development. During this period, oscillations and neural spiking display a nad that signals shift from local, loosely, correlated, putatively-driven patterns to internally, spatially distributed, and temporally activity. A developmental inflection may reflect a rapid engagement of mechanisms that enable computational processes within networks. Self-organization in systems is mediated by processes that facilitate sudden transitions in behavior. Results provide evidence for a transition in developing cortex of rodents and humans. Rodent development is temporally compressed relative to humans, an inflection that lasts 1-2 days in mouse could conceivably extend weeks in humans.

Prior to inflection, oscillatory activity was intermittent, non-uniform-cycle waveforms and high frequency. Oscill activities after transition became nearly, exhibiting discrete bands and waveform. Based on similarities oscillatory patterns, neural spiking features observed through recordings in mouse could extend to developing human infants. Neural spiking was recruited to oscillatory with strong, phase-lock, and temporal. These features are characteristic of networks engaged in processing. Multiple functions emerge during early development, enabling precise spatiemporal coordination of synaptic and cellular in brain functions. Immature networks develop into form for complex computations underlying cognition remains poorly understood.

The organized pattern of activity in circuits across species is spindle oscillation (10-20 Hz) that intermittently on background inactivity. Spindle bursts in rodents and delta in humans, immature activity commonly triggered peripheral stimuli. Cortical columns and pre-columns linked neuronal survival, establishing ensembles, and plasticity. Spindle bursts characteristic postnatal rodent, delta disappear term human neonates, emphasizing transient in network. Mature exhibits perpetual patterns interact wide frequencies, creating precise spatiemp windows for synchronization, plasticity generation sequences.
Researchers have developed an agent-based model that extends earlier strain theory to address the effect of immune selection on multi-copy genes in Plasium falcum, a pathogen responsible for the deadliest type of malaria in humans. The model considers the diversity of multi-copy genes that undergo antigenic variation via sequential, mutually exclusive expression and tracks the dynamics of unique reperto in a population of hosts. It shows that even under high levels of sexual recombination, competition through cross-immunity structures the population into a subset of coexisting repires of genes whose degree of antigen overlap depends on transmission intensity. Empirical comparison of genetic variation at antigenic and neutral sites supports the role of immune in structuring parasite diversity.

The model considers the shaping enormous diversity of genomic reperto of genes by tracking all possible reperto, given a pool of genetic variants. It explores structured populations resulting from selection exerted by competition distinct repires for hosts, mediated cross-immunity. The results show that decreasing transmission provokes nonlinear changes in dominant configurations. Importantly, there is not a simple relationship between diversity and repertoire. The model suggests that control strategies reducing transmission will not drastically reduce gene diversity because a reservoir of parasite with diverse, non-overlapping repires will remain. This provides a plausible explanation for why it is difficult to eliminate malaria: non-overlapping diversity creates inherent difficulties in reducing potential to reinfect same hosts.

The model demonstrates that strain structure rather than diversity determines epidemiological patterns and response to intervention. Reducing antigen between strains will result in infection at older age and gradual acquisition of protection, even in absence of changes gene. The model does not differentiate infection from disease, thus cannot address impact on severity. However, if number of genes a host encountered relates to degree against disease, expect more symptoms occur younger under high settings and homogeneous incidents over broader age-classes when transmission lowered.

The model constrains repertoire to orders lower levels than possible given diversity. Reduction occurs coexisting into variants with discordant repires. Departure from theory predicts dominant can exhibit partial overlap; extent increases with transmission and influences epidemiological patterns, particularly ageology. Immune each host structures population into distinct patches that define environment, causing selection act on parasite. These patches conserved even immunity alleles short-lived.
Researchers investigated the mechanism by which the bacterial effector protein OI from Shigella flexner inactivates the inflammatory NF-κ signaling pathway by deamating a glutamine residue in the ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme UBC, converting it to glutamate (Q100). This modification inhibits the interaction between UBC and the RING domain of TRAF6, a ubiquitin ligase, leading to a decrease in polyubiquination activity and subsequent NF-κ activation. The study found that deamation creates an intramolecular saltbridge in U13 that competes with an intermolecular saltbridge at the U13/TR6 interface, destabilizing the complex and reducing binding energy by 1.69 kcal/mol. Additionally, repulsive interactions between E100 and the negatively charged surface of TR6 destabilize transient interactions. The effect of deation was also observed in another U13/R complex, reducing Ub-discharge and Ub synthesis by several folds. The study highlights a mechanism by which post-translational modification perturbs conformation and stability of complexes to inhibit protein-protein association.
Researchers used optogenetic manipulations to activate and inactivate somatostatin-positive (S+) and parvalbum-positive (Pb+) interneurons in the auditory cortex of awake mice and recorded their responses to tones. They found that inactivation of S+ interneurons increased response gain, while inactivation of P+ interneurons weakened tuning and decreased information transfer, suggesting these neurons support different computational functions. However, activation of S+ and P interneurons showed no differences. A simple model was used to understand this asymmetry and showed that small changes in parameters, such as spontaneous activity or strength of light, determined whether activation or inactivation would produce consistent or paradoxical conclusions about neurons' functions.

The study aimed to test the assumption that differences in function between interne populations can be directly read and interpreted by dialing activity up or down. Three aspects of sensory processing were tested: regulation of gain, tuning, and information of responses. Activation of S and P interne produced no differences, even where activation caused strong suppression. In contrast, inactivation of S and Pne produced differences in all domains: P appeared to increase transfer through subtractive inhibition, while S provided divisive inhibition.

Putative S (n=24) and P+ (n=27) units were identified by increased sound-evoked rates with light. Optogen activation had larger effect on spontaneous than sound-evoked rates. Units not active without sound often showed rates above 30Hz with light. This suggests activation increases stimulus-independent spikes, potentially masking normal function. Activation heterogeneous populations may strengthen different functions than weakened by inactivation, leading to inconsistent conclusions.

Temporal organization of activity, such as response delays or synchrony, and stimulus preferences are important for output. Pneurons have been reported rapidly activated by broad tones, while S respond slowly to narrower tones. These features not replicated in indiscriminately tonically increased or decreased. A linear framework was applied to FTCs of units whose responses were tuned and suppressed by activation. This explained effects well, with median R values around 0.8. Activating S and Pne produced types of linear transformations. This suggests optogen manipulations can reveal linear or nonlinear operating, beyond previous work identifying discrepancies between single and network consequences.

Specifically, synchronous ACS during WM increased parietal activity, which correlated with behavioral performance. Functional results indicate that relative frontopetal stimulation influences information flow within WM. The study demonstrates a link between performance in demanding WM and large-scale brain synchronization.

Researchers have identified the transcription factor Pitx as a key regulator of embryonic axis formation and twinning in the chick embryo. In contrast to invertebrates and amphibians, where embryonic polarity is largely specified by maternal determinants, amni embryos remain plastic and can form multiple individuals until gastrulation. A molecular screen to find upstream regulators of cV1 in normal and manipulated embryos uncovers Pit2 as a candidate. Pit2 is essential for axis formation and acts as a direct regulator of c1 by binding to enhancers within neighbouring genes. V1, GDF and Nodal are also key in left-right asymmetry, suggesting the same polarity mechanism has been co-opted to different functions during evolution. When a blastoderm is cut at right to the primitive axis, c1 spontaneously initiates in the marginal zone adjacent to the edge, either right or left at frequency, foreshadowing the appearance of the streak a few hours later. This shows mechanisms position c1 active in the blasterm stage. Researchers investigated how fruit flies (Drosophila melanogaster) process temporal features of olfactory stimuli to enable odor-guided behaviors. They subjected flies to precisely defined odor waveforms and examined spike patterns of olfactory sensory neurons (OS) and projection neurons (PN). A significant transformation between OS and PN spike patterns was found, with PN strongly signaling OS spike rate and rate of change. A two-dimensional model admitting OS spike and rate and rate as inputs predicted PN output. When cascaded with rate-change encoding by OS, PNs signal the rate and acceleration of dynamic stimuli to higher centers, enabling reliable response to odor onsets.

The study tested first and second-order neurons in the Dophila olfactory system with varying concentrations and constructed a 2D model for temporal processing olfactory information. The shape of 2 nonlinearity and low prediction supported that PN rate is dependent on rate change of feed OS input. Combined with dynamic encoding by OS, PNs signal rate change and acceleration of odor to brain. An example of acceleration by PNs is found in OS/PN responses to parabolic input, where onset is amplified to step-like in P. This encoding is distinguished from other chemosensory systems, such as bacterial chemotaxis and nematodeenorhabditis elegans.

Fruit flies compute acceleration of odor concentrations and acceleration is relevant to chemaxis. Efficient chemaxis depends on odor distribution in behaviorally relevant, quantified by Reynolds number. Natural plumes are spatiotemporal and dynamics influence encoding. Drosila OS connect P in olfactory glomeruli, which relay to higher centers like mushroom and horn. Recent studies reported OS encode concentration and rate as a function of time. Building on this, the study asked how PNs contribute to internal representations of dynamic environments. Tested OS and P with plume stimuli and analyzed correlation of input signals in odor-OS-P pathway. A 2D linear model of OS-P transformation was constructed with triangle-shaped OS rates via systematic stimuli.

Odor distribution is intermittent and dynamic, and animals detect and respond to variation. Fruit flies exhibit robust odor-guid behaviors like turning and staying within specific zones. To enable these tasks, olfactory must process timeary features and supply brain. Several studies investigated dynamic stimuli in early olfactory systems, observing temporal processing. Most used step-pulse stimuli without measuring actual concentrations. This study rigorously measures input and explores space, confirming for vision and audition. The hypothesis is that acceleration in Dila allows higher centers to respond to onsets of rising stimuli.
In flowering plants, seed development is initiated by the fusion of the maternal egg and central cells with paternal sperm cells, leading to the formation of the embryo and endosperm, respectively. This study shows that fertilization of the central cell results in the production of auxin, likely exported to the maternal tissues, which drives coat development by removing Pc function. Mutants of the MADS transcription factor AGL have impaired auxin transport from endos to integuments, resulting in seed abortion. It is proposed that AGL regulates auxin transport from endos to integ, leading to removal of Pc block on seed development.

Researchers used a high-throughput method called Optimized Recombination, Accumulation, and Library Engineering (ORACLE) to comprehensively map the functional landscape of the receptor binding domain of T bacteriophage T7 (T7) by creating a library of 166 variants with single amino acid substitutions at every position. The study aimed to understand how mutations in the domain influence phage activity and host range. The domain is the distal region of the tail that makes primary contact with the host receptor. ORACLE generated a large, unbiased library of T7 variants to characterize the mutational landscape of the tip of the fiber. The study identified hundreds of function-enhancing substitutions not previously characterized and mapped regions of function-enh substitutions to the crystal structure to rationalize sequence and structure influence activity and host. Results showed that single amino acid substitutions are sufficient to enhance activity and host range, including dramatic increases in activity or specificity. The landscape each host is unique, reflecting different molecular preferences of adsorption and fitness of wildtype T7 on hosts. Hydrophilic substitutions were enriched in E. coli K-12 while hydrophobic were in BW13. Notably, substitutions on E. coli-12 lacking LPS components mirrored those recovered on BW13 mutants with truncated LPS, showing convergence of selection. Function-enhancing were concentrated in exterior loops, indicating orientational preference receptor recognition, but also found on other residues, less, suggesting alternative modes of tip for host recognition and intolerant residues located exterior. Results highlight the functional potential of the domain and rational the use of this fold for molecular recognition. The study demonstrated the utility of ORACLE by engineering highly active T variants against urinary tract pathogen E. coli. The approach provides a framework for characterizing sequence-function in ph-bacterial systems.
Individuals with congenital amus, a rare condition characterized by impaired perception and memory for pitch, rely on other dimensions such as duration during speech perception. This study investigated the neural basis of this strategy using functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) in 15 individuals with amus and 15 controls. Participants read sentences where a comma indicated a grammatical phrase, then heard sentences that differed in pitch and/or duration cues and selected the best match for the written. Prominent reductions in functional connectivity were detected in the amia group between left frontal language-related regions and right pitch-related regions, reflecting between-group differences in cue weights. Connectivity between these regions were not present during a control task. Results indicate the reliability of perceptual dimensions is linked to functional between frontal and perceptual regions, suggesting a compensatory mechanism.

The study found individuals with amia, who rely less on pitch than controls to process language, exhibited decreased connectivity between left frontal and right pitch regions. Using a data-driven approach, four regions in left and right frontal cortex were identified for the am group with decreased connectivity to several sites in frontal, temporal, and occipital cortex. Most results were decreased connectivity between frontal language processing regions (left IFG, DLPFC) and right regions implicated in pitch (superior gyrus, sulcus, Hesch gyrus, insula). This may relate to unreliability of amics' perception and memory for pitch, similar to weighted connections model of multisensory integration.

Congenital amia is described as a disorder of structural and functional within right hemisphere, between inferior cortices and posterior cortex. Consistent with this, right inferior cortex exhibited decreased connectivity in amia, and seed testing revealed auditory areas involved. However, this can be explained by pitch account, as suprasegment task detection and memory for patterns, whereas segment does not. Account of amia suggests structural differences cannot explain finding functional patterns do not differ amics controls during verbal task, and amics controls show patterns during passive listening. Suggest amics neglect pitch because learned memory is unreliable, down-weighting pitch reflected in connectivity between auditory and task-relevant areas.

Previous study on amia detected differences functional during passive listening, used seed voxels bilateral cortex, found amia increased between left and right, but decreased right inferior gyrus. Congital amia is characterized impaired perception and memory pitch, although auditory responses normal, subcort encoding pitch. Researchers have used optogenetic mapping in mice to investigate the neural circuits of the granular retrosplenial cortex (RSG), a region critical for both spatial and non-spatial behaviors. They found a dissociation between parallel circuits in superficial SG to process disparate inputs, with the thalamus and subiculum sources of spatial information strongly recruiting low-rhe (LR) pyramidal cells, while neighboring-sp (RS) cells are preferentially controlled by clastral and cingulate inputs, sources of nonpatial information. Precise subamin axonal and dendritic arbor within SG layer 1 permits parallel processing. Observed thalamortical dynamics enable LR neurons to compute speed of rotation despite head inputs not explicitly encoding speed. Thalamort synapses onto LR cells are depressing, allowing them to compute angular speed from direction inputs. Modeling demonstrates depressing synapses enable robust encoding of angular speed in postsynaptic LR rate.

LR cells are unique among pyram cells in SG, with no evidence of similar cells in other regions. Traumatic brain injury (TBI) is a significant risk factor for dementias, including tauopathies such as chronic traumatic encephalopathy (CTE). The mechanisms that promote prion-like spreading of Tau after T are not fully understood, in part due to lack of tractable animal models. This study tests the putative role of seizures in the spread of tauopathy. Zebraf larvae expressing a genetically encoded Tau biosensor report Tau accumulation when seeded via intraventricular brain injections. Subjecting zraf to a novel T paradigm produced TBI features, including cell death, post-traumatic seizures, and Tau inclusions. Application of dynamin or anticonvulsant drugs rescued T-induced tauopathy and cell death. Data suggest a role for seizure in prion seeding and spreading of tau following T. Further work is warranted regarding anti-convants that dampen postumatic seizures as a route to moderating tauopathy.

Researchers investigated the relationship between the spatial configuration and functional connectivity of brain regions using resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (rfMRI). They found that the spatial arrangement of functional regions strongly interacts with measures of brain connectivity and is predictive of non-imaging measures of behavior and lifestyle. The study suggests that cross-subject variations in the configuration of functional brain regions are often interpreted as changes in functional connectivity. The findings have implications for the interpretation of functional imaging data and cognitive traits.

 The organization of the brain into large-scale networks has been extensively investigated using rfMRI. Spontaneous fluctuations in brain regions as measured by rf show correlations with each other, revealing patterns of functional connectivity (FC). Extensive connectivity between cortical and subcortical regions is a feature of brain anatomy and function, and dysfunctional is associated with neurological and psychiatric disorders. The study aimed to determine which aspects of rf data most sensitively reflect trait variability across subjects. Neural sources of variability include the strength of coupling between populations and spatial organisation of regions. The study identified variability in the topography of functional across subjects and suggest this is primary driver of cross-sub trait variability in FC measures.

 Variability in spatial features capture a direct representation of subject than temporal correlations between regions. The presence of behaviorally cross-sub variance in maps is not surprising, but the fact that these features capture unique information is unexpected. The implication is that cross-sub information in adopted 'connectivity' reflects variability in location of regions rather than coupling. Partial results show network matrices contain little unique subject information not reflected in spatial organisation. The conceptualisation of brain operationally defined is relevant to interpretation. Some paration models assume piecewise constant connectivity within parcel, while others presume uniformity and recognises heterogeneity.

The study found that spatial in topography of functional is strongly associated with behavior and lifestyle. Network matrices reflect little unique information not captured by spatial variability. The interpretation of FC representing cross-sub trait variability in coupling may not be valid. Up to 62% of variance in matrices can be explained by spatial. The findings have implications for interpretation and contribute to understanding the role of FC in cognition and disease. Consistent with previous research highlighting structured-sub variance in functional and anatomical networks. Recent work shows resting state maps can predict activation accurately and highly variable subnetworks can be identified.

Softcell models allow multiple modes of organisation, representing information through interactions and shape. Both PROFOM and multimodal models capture cross-sub spatial variability, but location overlaps modestly. The need for research into optimal representation organisation is highlighted. Anatomical features were not investigated, but correlations between surface size and C weights suggest variability may contribute. Further research into structural and functional measures and trait variability is needed. The findings are relevant to approaches to study connectivity.
This study investigates the role of TBP (TATA-binding protein) in skeletal muscle cell differentiation and regeneration. TBP is a component of the transcription initiation complex (PIC) that promotes muscle gene expression in differentiated muscles, whereas TBP2 is not expressed in myotubes derived from C2C12 myoblasts or primary mouse satellite cells (MuSC). TBP null mice have intact regeneration potential and MuSC isolated from TBP deficient muscles differentiate into myot with same efficiency as wild type Mu. This suggests TBP does not replace TBP during muscle differentiation as previously proposed. TFI subunits (TBP and TAF) are downregulated at RNA and protein levels in myotubes compared to myoblast, forming a complex detectable on muscle promoters essential for expression. TBP is essential for muscle expression in differentiated muscles, not T2 is not. TBP null mice have intact regeneration potential and MuSC isolated from T deficient muscles differentiate into myot with same efficiency as wild type Mu. TFI subunits are downregulated in myoblast and myot at RNA and protein levels, forming complex detectable on muscle promoters essential for expression.
Researchers used a large-scale optogenetic interface to stimulate excitatory neurons and record electrocortic activity across primary somatosensory (S1) and motor (M) cortex in macaques. They tracked two measures of network connectivity, the response to stimulation and baseline between pairs of electrodes, which were strongly correlated before stimulation. Within minutes, stimulation in S or M significantly strengthened functional connectivity between areas. At a finer scale, stimulation led to heterogeneous changes across the network, reflecting correlations introduced by stimulation-evoked activity, consistent with Hebbian plasticity models. This work extends Hebb plasticity models to large circuits, with implications for stimulation-based neurorehabilitation.

In a rare case of a Rhes macaque monkey born without primary visual cortex (V1), the animal's behavior was unremarkable, but visual task performance was impaired. Multi-modal magnetic resonance imaging revealed that structures outside the lesion were normal. Visual stimulation with checkerboards activated the lateral geniculate nucleus, while full-field dots activated pulvin. Visual cortical activation was sparse but included patches. Consistently across lesion and control monkeys, functional analysis revealed an intact network of bilateral visual areas temporally correlated with V/MT activation, even without V. Despite subcortical responses to visual, little evidence was found for subortical input to V/MT supporting residual function or blindsight phenomena.

The activation of face patches in temporal cortex must be driven by subortical input other than V/MT. A pattern of activation underpins awareness and behavior, supported by a pattern of weak subort inputs to extrastate visual structures. Cortical and subort outside the lesion remained intact in monkey with little atrophy, suggesting a role in residual function. An intact network of extrate visual areas was comparable to controls. Connectivity between subort regions and V/MT was weak, but microstructure intact. Unlike acquired lesions, neonatal damage maintained structural integrity of the system. This may explain residual function in monkey and children with early visual cortex damage.

The dorsal network was intact and potential to support vision, seen in human patients with damage. Retinal information may reach dorsal areas via weak pathways LG and pular, but lack of consistent activation due to anesthesia makes difficult. Lack of differences between monkey and controls in pathways LG-V/MT and pular-V/MT suggests either could support residual abilities. Source of input supporting function may be preserved V connectivity or parallel inputs. Earlier studies in V-lesioned monkeys shown activation in early areas V2, V3 V4 and V/ as VMT. V might be crucial to function and awareness. These areas receive subort input. Activation in pular to dots was as in controls, consistent with perin nature of lesion.
The innate immune system's ability to recognize foreign double-stranded (ds) DNA of pathogenic origin in the intracellular environment is crucial for defense. However, the mechanisms distinguishing between foreign and host DNA in the nucleus are not understood. Researchers used biochemical and single-molecule techniques to show that the innate sensor IF16 one-dimensionally scans long stretches of exposed dsDNA to assemble supramolecular signaling platforms. Nucleosomes prevent IF16 from targeting host DNA by interfering with one-dimensional movements. This unique scanning-assisted mechanism allows IF16 to distinguish from foe and assemble oligomers efficiently on foreign DNA.

 IF16 is part of the AIM-like family, which includes other nuclear and cytosolic ds-sensors. It will be interesting to determine how these related sensors use ds as a 1 'digital' ruler' to regulate signaling assembly. This family is implicated in autoimmune disorders such as Sjög's syndrome. The mechanisms of IF16 targeting ds remain unknown. To establish a signaling platform, IF16 must locate other molecules on large pathogen genomes and overcome host DNA. Here, researchers report a unifying mechanism explaining how IF16 resolves these issues in its foreign-dsensing pathways.

IF16 is composed of three domains flanked by unstructured linkers, one pyrin (PY) and two DNA-binding (HinA and HinB). It detects dsDNA invading pathogens in the nucleus and cytoplasm. A scheme for FRET experiments shows time-dependent changes in emission between donor and acceptor-labeled IF16 on dsDNA. Assembly rates increased non-linearly with dsDNA sizes above 60-200bps, staying constant up to 600bps. About 4-10 IF16 molecules are required for optimal assembly. Assembly constants scaled with IF16 concentration, indicating a non-cooperative mechanism. Single-molecule imaging shows IF16 one-dimensionally diffuses on dsDNA while bound.
Researchers have developed a novel proteomics technique called Spatially Targeted Optical Microproteomics (STOMP) that allows for the interrogation of micron-scale regions of interest (ROIs) in mammalian tissue without genetic manipulation. Methanol or formalin-fixed specimens are stained with fluorescent dyes or antibodies to visualize ROIs, then soaked in a solution containing photoactivatable benzylbenzglycylhistidine (6BP). Confocal imaging with two-photon excitation is used to covalently photo-tag all proteins within ROI to a resolution of 0.67 µm in the xy-plane and 1.48 µm axially. After solubilization, photo-tagged proteins are isolated by mass spectrometry. As a test, amyloid plaques in Alzheimer's disease (AD) mouse and post-mortem case were examined, confirming known constituents and discovering new ones. STOMP can be applied to various samples including cell lines, primary cultures, ex vivo, specimens, biopsy, and postmort tissue.

The study used TCR8 mice expressing human amyloid precursor protein with mutations associated with familial AD, producing amyloid plaques and spatial impairments by 3 months age. Frozen sections of brains with plaques were treated with DEPC, stained with Th, and soaked in 6BP solution. Slides were imaged by confocal to identify Th-positive amyloid. Conf images were used to construct masks. The technique hinges on selective photoabeling and purification, assessing non-specific labeling 6BP in light and immunofluorescence excitation, and nonspecific binding affinity beads. Adjacent sections were 'dark controls to assess non-labeling 6BP and nonspecific binding. ST delivered two-ph excitation to regions corresponding pixels, photoactivating 6BP and photo-tagging proteins, and photo-bleaching Th present regions. Immunofluores staining with anti-H6 antibody superposed on mask, highlighting targeting accuracy.

Lower stringency increases hits but may introduce false positives. Two TCR8 brain sections produced photo-tagged tissue for ST, requiring 12-16 hr microscope time per sample. Regions as small as 0.38 µ can be analyzed. Protein identification via massrometry involved digestion and LCMS. Technical and biological replicates demonstrate high correlation and overlap, illustrating reproducibility. Identified proteins in TCR plaques are summarized, with A being most abundant. A software selectively delivers two-ph excitation to deposits, leaving untouched. Unlike LCM, manual tracing is not required.

ST provides a semi-automated tool for photo-tagging pathological deposits. Importantly, unlike LCM, manual tracing is not required. The smallest photo-tag volume is less 0.5 µ3. A voxel in TCR tissue was photo-tagged. Volume was measured by confocal imaging. Fluorescence profiles are shown. The width is 0.67m in x and 1.48 along z, corresponding 0.38 ellipsoid volume. ST can be applied to various samples, including cell lines, primary, cultures, ex vivo, specimens, biopsy, and post tissue.

A meta-analysis of 18 lineages containing species that use C3, C, or intermediate C–C forms of photosynthesis was conducted to parameterise a 16-dimensional phenotypic landscape. A novel Bayesian approach based on a hidden Markov model was developed and experimentally verified to predict the evolution of the C4 phenotype. The evolutionary histories underlying the appearance of C photosynthesis were determined by ancestral and phenotypic alterations unrelated to photosynthesis. The order of C trait acquisition is flexible and driven by non-photosynthetic drivers. This flexibility facilitated the convergent evolution of the complex trait. Several studies demonstrated that convergence of a phenotype can be specified by diverse genotypes and non-hologous molecular mechanisms in lineages. Our data indicate that flexibility in the series of evolutionary events also facilitated convergence of this complex. Four distinct trajectories underlie the evolution of C lineages. Researchers reengineered the nucleotide-binding pocket of SpoIVA, a bacterial cytoskeletal protein, to mimic its ancestral GTPase activity and found that the protein functioned properly as a GTPase but failed to polymerize because it did not form an NDP intermediate required for polymerization. Incubation of SpoAGTP with ATP did not promote polymerization, revealing that the nucle base, in addition to energy from hydrolysis, is critical for specific functions. The study suggests that increased levels of ATP relative to GTP at the end of sporulation drove the change in nucleotide preference in SpoIVA. SpoIVA is a cytoskeletal protein that hydrozes ATP to form static polymers and is ancestrally derived from Era-like GTP, a pan-bacterial ribosomal maturation protein belonging to the TRAC class of-loop GTPases. To understand the selective pressure underlying the switch in nucle specificity of extant Spo and mechanistic governing function, researchers restored its enzymatic GTP activity by altering amino acids in loops near the bound NTP. The altered protein hydrolyzed GTP with preference in vitro and was able to exploit energy from hydrolysis to drive conformation change required for polymerization. However, it only polymerized in presence of ATP, suggesting the nucle base, not energy, was required for function. The study proposes that scarcity of G during late sporulation drove evolution of Spo to preferentially utilize ATP. Amino acid and stationary phase result in reduction of G and GTP levels, coinciding with increase in production of nucle almones. The drop in G has been implicated in initiation of spor in B. subtilis, possibly derepressing activity of Cod transcription factor repressing sporulation genes directly sensing G via lig-binding GAF domain.
A study investigated the long-term consequences of the absence of leptin signaling in early life by restoring leptin signaling at the fourth or tenth week of age in mice null for the leptin receptor (Lep) gene. The results showed that defects previously considered irreversible due to neonatal deficiency of leptin signaling, including poor development of arcuate neural projections, were recovered by Lep reactivation in adulthood. However, Lep deficiency in early life led to irreversible obesity via suppression of energy expenditure. Lep reactivation in adulthood led to persistent reduction in hypothalamic Pomc, Cart, and Prlh expression and defects in reproductive and brain development. The findings revealed that early defects in leptin signaling cause permanent metabolic, neuroendocrine, and developmental problems. Lept or Lep deficiency led to reduced brain mass, disruption of projections from arcuate neurons to paraventricular nucleus of hypothalamus, and behavioral and cognitive problems. Lep reactivation in adulthood partially recovered brain mass, but remained lower in Lep-null mice compared to wild-type. Hypothalamic expression of neurotrophic factors was reduced in Lep-null and Lep-null mice, including Bdn, Ig1 and D4, and glial fibrillary protein. Brain mass was determined in mice recovered Lep expression before onset. Lep-null males exhibited reduced mass compared to wild-type, but no difference was observed between wild and Lep-null females. Behavioral tests showed Lep-null showed reduced exploration, Lep reactivation normalized. Spatial learning and memory were analyzed in maze.
Arabidopsis heterotrimer G proteins regulate immunity by directly coupling to the FLS receptor. The Arabidopsis immune receptor F2 perceives bacterial flagellin epitope fl22 to activate defenses through the cytoplasmic kinase B1. Heterotrimeric proteins composed of non-canonical G protein XL2, G protein A1, and G proteins G1 and G2 are required for F2-mediated responses through an unknown mechanism. We show that in preactivation, XL2 directly interacts with F2 and B1, and functions with A1 and G1/2 to attenuate proteas-mediated degradation of B1, allowing immune activation. Following activation by fl22, XL dissociates from A and is phosphorylated by B in the termin. Phosphorylated XL enhances production of reactive species (ROS) by modulating NAD oxidase Rho. The study demonstrates the proteins are directly coupled to the F receptor and regulate signaling through pre- and post-activation mechanisms.

This study examines the Arabidopsis circadian clock at the single cell level across the plant over several days under constant environmental conditions. Single cell measurements reveal robust oscillations, albeit desynchronised, with two waves of clock activity, one down and up the root. Cell-to-cell coupling is observed, especially in the root tip, and a simple model shows that cell-to-cell coupling and period differences can generate the observed waves. The results suggest a decentralised structure of the plant clock, unlike the centralised mammalian clock, with multiple coordination points. Tissue-specific differences in phases and robustness of the clock are observed, not restricted to one cell type, suggesting cells respond based on position. The observed rhythms in hypocotyl peak before cotyledon and top root, with a wave of clock expression down the root, fit a proposed hierarchical structure, but the shootap clock drives rhythms in root. However, results suggest a more complicated structure, as hierarchical model does not explain short oscillations in root or wave clock expression from tip. Studies argue cellular oscillations are uncoupled or weakly coupled, but coupling detected in Lemna gibbons and Arabidopsis. Single cell approach reveals weak coupling across plant but regions with strong local between cells in root, sufficient to drive synchrony. Damping is characterised by reduction of oscillations through time, at plant level due to circuit in cells or desising due different periods or phases or stochasticity. Decentral model of coordination in plants supported.
Researchers investigated the neural basis of multi-talker speech perception in adults with normal hearing. They found that multi-talker intelligibility varied widely in normal adults, but could be predicted from neural-lock to modulation (FM) cues measured with ear EEG recordings. Combining temporal processing, pupil-indexed listening, and FM accounted for 78% of variability in multialker intelligibility. Disordered-up and top-down markers of poor multialker perception identified could inform next-generation clinical tests for hearing disorders.

The study used a combination of psychophysical tests and ear EEG to characterize FM processing. They looked at changes in phase-lock to carrier, not rate of FM, emphasizing neural coding of timing in early system. FM was correlated with behavioral detection and digits task performance, suggesting representation of fine timing cues contributes multialker intelligibility. Subjects with lowest FM thresholds had increased FM amplitudes at broad excursion depths, indicating transition from timing to place code. In contrast, IPD transfer in subjects with lowest thresholds increased steadily for all IPDs.

Adding higher-order stream markers to multivariate model captured more variability in multialker intelligibility, but offered less insight into sensory failure. Findings suggest individuals struggling to follow conversations might be identified by poor-up processing rapid cues and overutilization of top-down resources. Interplay between up-down is complex, not a simple tradeoff between poor FS and pupil-index effort. Understanding balance-up and-down strategies encode signals is important in human perception.

The study consisted of self-reported questionnaires, behavioral measures, and EEG of auditory processing. Audiograms were clinically normal, but many reported difficulties with intelligibility, particularly in multiple speakers. Individuals steeper recruitment had difficulty in multialker task, significant correlation between min-max pupil and intelligibility.

Objective markers explained complaints of poor communication with clinically normal. Examined poor-noise intellig associated with nerve integrity, encoding cues, fast processing, and effort. Researchers have developed a non-invasive imaging technique to measure single-cell blood flow in the smallest to largest vessels in the living retina, a part of the central nervous system. The mammalian eye's transparency allows for a non-invasive view of microvess, but imperfections in optics blur microscopic retinal capillaries and blood cells. To break this barrier, they used 15 kHz adaptive optics imaging to measure blood flow in C57/6 mice without surgery or contrast. Measured flow ranged four orders of magnitude (0.2-1.55 µL/min) across retinal diameters (3.2-45.8 µm). The ultrafast imaging pipeline and automated analysis enabled measurement of millions of blood speeds. This study provides a new, automated label-free way to determine vascular perfusion and flow in mammals, conditions of health and disease. A functional map of the arteriolar and venular tree was characterized, with absolute measurements across vessel sizes. Pulsatile flow was measured in all retinal vessels, from capill to arterioles and vles, contradicting conventional wisdom that capill lack pulsatile flow. Quantification of vascular indices like pulsatility and profile showed utility in measuring temporal and spatial flow fluctuations, not just mean flow, to identify signatures disrupted in disease.
Symbiotic Receptor Kin (SYK) is crucial for the development of phosphate-acquiring arbuscular mycorrhiza (AM) and nitrogen-fixing root nodule symbiosis. However, the mechanisms that discriminate between the two symbiotic developmental fates have been enigmatic. This study shows that ectopic expression of receptor-like genes Nod Receptor 1 (N1), N5, and SYMR initiates nodule organogenesis and nodulation gene expression in the absence of rhizobia. Overexpression of N1 or N5 associated with endogenous SYMR in Lotus japonicus revealed that dominant SYK initiates signalling independently of N1 or N5 and upstream a set of genes required for generation of calcium-spiking in both symbes. Only SYK but not N overexpression triggered expression of AM-related genes, indicating the receptors play a role in decision between AM or root nodule symbiosis development.

To achieve overexpression, constructs were generated expressing SYK, N5, or N1 under the control of L japon ubiquitin promoter and C-terminal fluorescent tags for detection. The functionality of N was confirmed by ability to restore nodulation in corresponding mutant roots to level of japon wild-type. Intriguingly, transgenic expression of any of three symb RL versions in japon roots was sufficient to activate entire nodule pathway as evidenced by formation of nodule structures in absence rhobia. Presence of vascular bundles identified lateral organs as nodules. Spontaneous nodule primia were present on 90% of roots with SYK, 23% with N5, 11 with N1, and 0 with empty vector.

Plants establish mutualistic symbes with AM fungi or nitrogen-fix rhobia to circumvent nutrient deficiencies. One step in recognition between rhobia and legume is perception of bacterialipoitigosacides, called nodulation, by lys motif receptor kinases N1 and N5. Nod factor induces genetically separable calcium signatures in root cells; early influx and periodic oscillations.
