A new factor called mRNA:RNA avoidance has been identified, which increases translational efficiency and is selected for across prokaryotes. The avoidance of stochastic mRNA:RNA interactions has a greater impact on protein abundance than mRNA structure or codon usage. By generating variant green fluorescent protein (GFP) mRNAs with different potential for mRNA:RNA interactions, it was demonstrated that GFP levels correlate with avoidance. Taking mRNA:RNA interactions into account enables precise modulation of protein abundance. The specificity of prokotic RNAs for mRNAs is the result of selection for functional and against stochastic interactions. Experimental results support that stochastic interactions are selected due to deleterious outcomes on expression. Avoidance of mRNA:RNA interactions has several evolutionary consequences: transcription outputs become more diverse, the probability of stochastic interactions for new RNAs and mRN increases, impacting emergence of new high abundance RN, and selection for abundance may be mitigated by deleterious cstalk. Interactions limit the number of transcribed RN, combinatorics of RNA:RNA imply stochastic interactions cannot be avoided, driving selection for spatial or temporal segregation of transcripts. Taking cod usage, mRNA structure, and mRNA:RNA into account allows prediction of proteome from genomic data and precise control of protein via manipulation of synonymous sequences.

 A correlation analysis between protein abundances and avoidance, 5 end mRNA structure, and cod usage was computed. Avoidance significantly correlated in four datasets, while 5 end structure in two, and cod usage in four. An outlier analysis on E. coli showed avoidance measures shifted for bottom expressed genes and highest for top genes. A heatmap summarized the effect of four attributes on protein expression: avoidance, 5 end structure, cod bias, and abundance. GFP correlates with cod selection, 5 end structure, and avoidance. Tests can be divided into three parts: evolutionary analyses, proteomics, transcriptomics, and GFP data to predict effect of avoidance on expression, and application to design synonymous mRN for high or low protein.

Conservation analyses detected shifts in bacterial and archaeal genomes relative to shuffled controls, 73% of proary phyla underrepresented stochastic interactions. A test of G composition revealed difference between mRN and ncRN for 95% bacteria and archaea. Selection against interactions is universal for proary. The relationship between potential interactions and variation between mRNA and protein was examined for four published datasets E. coli and Pseudomonas aeruginosa.
Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS) is a fatal neurodegenerative disease characterized by upper and lower motor neuron (MN) loss. Loss of neuromuscular junctions (NJs) is a key event in ALS patients and animal models of the disease. Denervation of NJs is observed before symptom onset and significant axonal or cell loss. However, whether NM denervation arises from branch-specific degeneration or manifestation globally degenerating motor remains unclear. In vivo imaging of single axons and NJs over three months in SODG37 mice reveals motor-units dismantled asynchronously. Weeks prior complete ax degeneration, axonal dismantling is accompanied by contemporaneous axonal sprouting in synapse formation onto NJs. Den events propagate from first NM, consistent contribution neuromus factors extrinsic motor, dist branches susceptible. Results show NM denervation ALS complex process continuous denervation new rather manifestation global neuron degeneration.
Researchers investigated the effects of tamoxifen, a selective estrogen receptor modulator used in breast cancer treatment, on mice to understand its side effects on physiology. Tamox treatment in mice resulted in changes in temperature, bone density, and movement, similar to those experienced by humans undergoing tamox therapy. Single-cell RNA sequencing revealed tamox-induced gene changes in the hypothalamus and preoptic area (hypothalamus-PO), a region rich in estrogen receptor expression. These changes were dependent on estrogen receptor alpha (ER) as conditional knockout of ER in the hypothalam-PO ablated or reversed tamox-induced gene expression. ER-deficient mice did not exhibit tamox-induced changes in temperature, bone, or movement. The findings provide mechanistic insight into effects of tamox on the hypothalam-PO and indicate ER mediates several physiological effects of tamox treatment in mice.

Tamox is known to exert antiprolative effects on cancer cells through ER but agonistic or antagonistic effects on different receptors depending on cell type and context. The hypothalam-PO is a target of tamifen, leading to changes in food intake and body weight, and the hypothamic-puitaryovarian and hypothamic-puit-adrenal axes. Tamifen also affects gene expression in the hypothalam, blocking estrogen induction of progesterone receptor and increasing estrogen receptor beta. Researchers hypothesized tam alters estrogen signaling in the hypothalam-PO to mediate negative effects of tamox therapy. They modeled tamox treatment in mice with 28-day course based on human dosage and measured movement, bone, temperature, and thermogenic adipose tissue. Profiling genome expression of cells in hypothalam-PO revealed tam-induced changes in multiple types, most strongly in neurons and ependal cells.

Conditional of ER in hypothalamPO blocked majority of changes in wild-type mice tamox, indicating pivotal role ER in mediating homeost effects of tamox. Data indicate potent effects on gene in neuronal subtypes and ependal cells, suggesting pleotropic effects of tam involve various responsive cells and brain. Determining regions of hypoth-PO respond to tam and mediate effects require region and cell-specific studies.
IS15 is an interferon-stimulated, ubiquitin-like protein with antiviral activity, but its role during bacterial infection remains elusive. Researchers show that IS15 expression in nonphagocytic cells is dramatically induced upon Listeria monocytogen infection. This induction is type I interferon-independent and depends on cytosolic surveillance, which senses bacterial DNA signals through STING, TBK, IRF3, and IR7. IS15 restricts Lister infection in vitro and in vivo. Stable isotope labeling in culture (SILAC) analysis identifies ISylated proteins responsible for the effect. Overexpression of IS15 leads to ISylation of ER and Golgi proteins, correlating with increased secretion of cytokines counteracting infection. Data reveal a previously uncharacterized IS15-dependent restriction of Lister infection, reinforcing IS as a key component of innate response.

IS15 is unique as it modifies specific and non-specific proteins cotranslationally. It is induced by type I interfer, produced during infection, so researchers aimed to decipher IS induction during Lister and ISylation as host defense. IS15 is dramatically induced upon Lister infection, and surprisingly, early induction is type I-independent. Lister-mediated IS induction depends on CSP, sensing bacterial DNA through STING, TBK, IR3, IR7. IS counteracts Lister infection in vitro and in vivo. Protein targets of ISylation following IS overexpression were identified using SILAC analysis. ISylated proteins are primarily integral membrane, localized to endoplasmic reticulum and Golgi apparatus, critical for glycosylation, ER, morphology, and trafficking.

Cytokine secretion was assessed in MEF lacking IS compared to wild-type following Lister infection. Despite higher bacteria in IS-/- MEF, they secreted less IL-6 than wild-type. IS appears to modulate cytokine secretion following Lister. Data reveal IS modification of ER and Gol proteins increases secretion cytokines counteracting infection in vivo. IS's intracellular role modulating cytokine secretion in nonphocytic cells may be responsible for reduced interferon production. Increased cytokine secretion by IS is a previously uncharacterized host mechanism engaging immune to combat bacterial and viral infection. Cells encountering Lister, nonphocytic, can induce IS early, raising alarm against pathogen and alerting innate cells to infection. IL-6 and IL-8 mediate neutroph and monocyte recruitment, IL-6 is critical innate response Lister. Cytokine secretion without IS is less efficient, explaining moderate susceptibility IS/- to systemic Lister infection.
Researchers have developed DeepRET, an open-source software for automated analysis of single-molecule Förster Resonance energy transfer (smRET) data using deep learning. SmRET is a method for studying the structure and dynamics of biomolecules, but high-throughput methodologies and commercial instrumentation have outpaced the development of rapid, standardized, and automated analysis methods. DeepRET integrates features of smRET analysis and outputs common kinetic metrics. Its accuracy on ground truth data reached >95%, outperforming human operators and requiring ~1% of time. Its precise and rapid operation on data demonstrates DeepRET's capacity to quantify biomolecular dynamics and contribute to benchmarking smRET for structural biology.

DeepRET's neural network is trained for smRET but can be extended to other single-molecule trajectories, including tracking, constant measurements, and blinking using simulated or high-quality data. The network is expected to enable fully automated methodologies for biomolecular recognition, folding, and resolution. The strength of deep neural networks (DNN) is to learn complex functions and recognize aspects of input and model nonlinear relationships. DNN classify data into classes based on provided labels. The training of D is computationally intensive, but the final model can be shared and used for predictions at no cost.

DeepRET is an all-inclusive software with a pre-trained D at its core for rapid, objective, and assessment of smRET for quantifying biomolecular. It operates with minimal human intervention and requires a threshold on data quality as an initial step to detailed quantification of structural from images. This is attained through an intuitive interface that automates common smRET procedures from image analysis, intensity extraction, classification, and production of figures. DeepRET comes as a standalone executable for Windows and Mac users with limited skills to operate. A script version implemented in Python enables experts to adjust analysis for needs.

DeepRET is trained and tested on 2-ALEX and non-A smRET data. Other techniques such as 3- and 4 F, protein fluorescence enhancement, metal transfer, and intrap surface transfer could be implemented through additional training and software. The simulation in DeepRET is based on protein dynamics observed by smRET follows a Mark process. Any data can be simulated using the trace to meet requirements. In current, DeepRET operates immobilized particles, but implementing single-particle tracking would allow extraction dynamics on freely diffusing particles.

This study shows that SC2, an isoform associated with chromatin, contributes to PR localization and repression of certain Poly target genes. These point to a role for RN in regulating SC2 function and suggest SC participates in epigen control directly in cooperation with PR1. Researchers have investigated the mechanism of ribosome stalling by SecM, a regulatory peptide that controls the Sec-dependent secretion pathway in Escheria coli. They used cryo-EM structures of Sec-stalled ribosomes at 3.3-3.7 resolution to reveal two distinct stalling mechanisms at elongation steps: inactivation of the ribosomal peptid-transfer center and prolonged peptyl-RNA residence at the A site. These results demonstrate control of the translation cycle by regulatory peptides through dynamic reshaping of the ribosomal center. Previous studies suggested interactions between Sec and the tunnel induce conformation changes of the PTC, leading to arrest at distinct elong steps, including peptide-bond and tRNA translocation. However, previous structures were not sufficient for visualization of atomic interactions between the tunnel and nascent peptide. The current study provides an alternative mechanism of Sec-induced stalling, where interactions within the rib tunnel promote conformation rearrangements of 23 rRNA nucleides, locking the P in an inactive state. Another mechanism involves altered interaction between A*-RNA and the P-loop, increasing the life of A*-RNA and slowing its translocation to the P site. The study used in vitro translation system and single cryoEM to analyze Sec-stalled ribosome nascent complexes, detecting arrested at the 3-end of Sec coding sequence. Biochemical and structural data indicate that Sec stalling is a dynamic process involving elongation at multiple positions on Sec mRNA, with G as the predominant site of stalling.
Researchers used an Adaptive Scanning Ophthalmoscope to image the fovea in 28 eyes of 16 subjects and measured cone density and metrics in units of visual and linear retinal units. The fove cone mosaic in longer eyes is expanded at the fove, but not proportionally to eye length. Despite retinal stretching, myopes have higher angular density and around the fove compared to emmetropes, offering better visual acuity. Reports of deficits in best-correct fove vision in myopes cannot be explained by increased spacing caused by retinal stretching during my progression. The study aimed to investigate the effect of eye length on cone density at the fove center. Two types of densities were discussed: linear, quantifying cones within a fixed area, and angular, quantifying cones per degree. The study found that linear density decreases with length, but angular increases with length, indicating that stretching is not a simple expansion. Myopes have higher angular density around the fove, suggesting better visual acuity. The study found that axial length accounts for 39% of variance in changes in linear density and 32% in angular density.
Researchers have developed a model to calculate the duration of pneumocococcal carriage in the nasopharynx, combining longitudinal swab data with whole genome sequence data. They found that pneumococcal genomic variation accounted for 63% of the variation in carriage duration, while host traits such as age and previous carriage accounted for less than 5%. The heritability was partitioned into lineage and locus effects, with serotype contributing 17%, drug resistance 9%, and other locus 7%. A pan-genome-wide association study identified prophage sequences associated with decreased carriage independent of serotype, potentially disrupting the competence mechanism. These findings support theoretical models of pneumoccal competition and antibiotic resistance.

The study quantified and mapped genetic contribution to carriage of S. pneumoniae, finding a range of factors such as host age known to cause duration differ, sequence of pneumoccal genome explains most variability. Researchers have investigated the stoichiometry of the IKs channel, a tetrameric voltage-gated ion channel composed of KCQ and its β-subunit KC1. The stoichi between α and β-subunits has been controversial, with studies reporting either a strict 2:1 or variable up to 4:4 ratio. To address this, they created IK fusion proteins linking KC1 to one, two, or four KCQ subunits to reproduce 4:4, 2:4, or 1:4 stoichiometries. Whole and single-channel recordings showed EQ and EQQ to have hyperpolarized activation, reduced conductance, and shorter latency compared to EQ, abolished by the addition of KC1. Using unnatural amino acid crosslinking, they found EQQ and EQ crosslink rates to be slowed compared to KCQ, demonstrating no intrinsic mechanism limits the association of up four β-subunits within the IK complex. The results suggest that under conditions, many IK complexes have β-sub configuration exceeding 2:4. A variable stoichi model for IK has implications, including a depolar shift in the current-voltage relationship, slowed activation, and increased conductance. However, during a cardiac action potential, this would result in limited activation. The stoichi can be regulated based on availability, altering expression could change the distribution of composition, shifting overall kinetics. There is evidence for association of other β-subunits, five KC genes can be expressed in cardiomyocytes, each altering KC1 gating in distinct ways. KCQ alone had V of -15.5m, presence of tethered-sub resulted in progressive depolar of V to maximum 30m, indicating linked-sub contribute complex regulation. The number of β-sub in IK complex is critical for regulation, shown up four KC1 can associate KCQ tetramer. Variable allows flexibility modulation IK but increases heterogeneity in membrane. Development models, IK structure, activity, screens, and transgenic animals should consider diversity IK configuration to reflect physiology.
This study investigated the role of interleukin-1 beta (Ilb) in zebraf fin regeneration. Myoid cells were previously thought to be the primary source of Ilb, but this study found that epithelial cells express Ilb in response to tissue injury and initiate the inflammatory response. Macrophages are necessary for survival of regenerative cells by attenuating Ilb expression. Ilb plays an essential role in normal fin regeneration by regulating expression of regeneration-induced genes. The study reveals that proper levels of Ilb and inflammation, tuned by macrophages, are crucial for tissue regeneration. Myoid cells produce pro-inflammatory cytokines such as Ilb, but epithelial cells express Ilb in response to tissue injury. Macrophages attenuate Ilb expression and resolve inflammation. In the absence of macroph, apoptosis occurs in primed regenerative cells. Ilb is a double-edged sword in tissue, required for regeneration but excess impairs it. The study found that Ilb is expressed by epithelial cells around injury and quenched by macroph. Macrophages play a role in fin regeneration by attenuating Ilb expression and supporting regenerative cells. Ilb induces apoptosis in primed regenerative cells through Tnf signaling or ER stress. Excessive Ilb causes apoptosis and suppresses fin extension. Inflammation is essential for tissue regeneration, but excessive Ilb impairs it. The study used Dex and Ilb MO to assess the role of inflammation in fin regeneration. Dex-treated larvae displayed retardation and reduction in cell proliferation. Expression of regeneration genes was downregulated in Dex-treated larvae.

Lynch syndrome is responsible 2-5% of endometrial (EM) or colorectal (CRC) tumors. Epigenetic silencing of ML1 contributes another 15-28% of these tumors. Deficiency of MMR leads to DNA errors in tumor tissue, not normal tissue. Errors accumulate as ind mutations in mono and di-nucle repeats, microsatellite (MSI). Researchers have engineered an optimized version of a light-inducible dimer protein called Magnets, which enables precise and spatial control of biological processes in a non-invasive fashion. The original Magnets require concatization and preincubation at 28°C to be functional, but these limitations have been overcome by engineering an 'enhanced' Magn (eags) pair. eags have greater thermal stability, dimer efficiency, and faster association and dissociation kinetics than the original Magnets. They were used to rapidly and reversibly recruit proteins to subcellular organelles, induce organel contacts, and reconstitute OS-VAP-ER-Gol tether implicated in phosphidyositol-4-phosphate transport metabolism. eags represent an effective tool to optogenetically manipulate physiological processes over cells or in subcell volumes. 

A cell-culture screen was established to capture dimer efficiency, association, dissociation, and folding and maturation. Mutations were selected across the protein, focusing on the dimer interface. Mutations were based on alignments with thermophil fungalid domains and structure-guided design. Several rounds of mutagenesis and screening resulted in final eags variants with mutations over the starting scaffolds. eags were used in a variety of applications, including protein recruitment to different organel, generation of organel contacts, and reconstitution of V-dependent organel teth that have regulatory functions in lipid transport. eags were compared to other optogen dizer reagents, including Cry/C1 and iIDs, and showed advantages in several assays, such as protein recruitment to mitochondria, lysosomes, and activity of OC phosphat to the membrane. eags dimer occurs only in the irradiated region, while iIDs extend distance and Cry/C1 penetrate the cell. e recovery of Cry1 after offset is slower than iIDs and eags. e efficiency of light-driven recruitment e is greater than iIDs but lower than Cry1 in cell conditions.
Ribosome stalling during translation has been shown to cause neurodegeneration, but the signaling pathways triggered by stalled elongation complexes are unknown. To investigate these pathways, we analyzed the brain of CBL/6-Gbp2f-/- mice, which have elongation complexes stalled at AG codons due to deficiencies in tRNAUC and GTP2, a mammalian ribosome factor. Increased phosphorylation of e2 (Ser) was detected prior to neurodegeneration in these mice, and transcript analysis demonstrated activation of ATF, a key factor in the integrated stress response (ISR) pathway. Genetic experiments showed this pathway was activated by e2 kinase, GC2, in a deacylated tRNA-independent fashion. We found that ISR attenuates neurogeneration in CBL-G2/- mice, underscoring the importance of cellular context on the outcome of activation this pathway. These results demonstrate the interplay between elongation and initiation in regulating neuron survival during stress.

 Activation of GC2 results in e2 phosphorylation and induction of ATF-targets in the cerebellum and hippocampus of B-G2- mice. Genetic studies demonstrate GC2 functions to attenuate neurogeneration in B-G brain. These suggest rib stalling activates GC2 as a previously unknown mechanism linking elong and initiation of translation. A study analyzed 147 population-based studies with measurements of over 18.6 million participants to estimate adult height for people born between 1896 and 1996 in 200 countries. The largest gain in adult height over the past century occurred in South Korean women and Iranian men, who became 20.2 cm (95% credible interval 17.5-22.7) and 16.5 (13.3-19.7) taller, respectively. In contrast, there was little change in adult height in some sub-Saharan countries and South Asia over the century of analysis. The tallest over 100 years were men in the Netherlands, born in the last quarter of the 20th century, whose heights surpassed 182.5 cm, and shortest were women in Guatemala in 189 (140.3; 135.8-144.8). The differential between the tallest and shortest was 19-20 cm a century ago and remained the same for women and increased for men a century later despite changes in ranking countries.

The study found that people in 189 were shortest in Asia and Central and Andean America. The 189 male cohort measured 152.9 (147.9-157.9) in Laos, the same as a well-nourished 12.5-year boy, followed by Timor-Leste and Guatemala. Women in same in Guatemala were 140.3 (135.8) the same a-nourished 10 girl. Salvador, Peru, Bangladesh, South Korea, and Japan had next women. The populations a century ago lived Central and Northern Europe, North and some islands. The of men in Sweden, Norway, and USA surpassed 171, 18-19 taller men in Laos. Swedish women with height 160.3 (158.2-162.4) were tallest a century and 20 taller women in Guatemala. Women also 158 in Norway, Iceland, USA, and American Samoa.

Changes in height over century varied across countries. Notably, large increases in European men heights in 19 and 20 century highlighted, found largest since 189 cohort in Korean and Iranian, who 20.2 (17.5-22.7) and 16.5 (13.3-19.7) taller, respectively. Researchers investigated the brain regions and cells responsible for opioid-induced respiratory depression, a major cause of opioid overdose deaths. Between 1999 and 2017, opioid overdose rates in the US quadrupled, resulting in 130 deaths per day. Opioids depress breathing through two brainstem sites, specifically the preBöt Complex, where breathing originates, and glutamateric neurons in this region are sensitive to opioids. Characterizing these neurons may lead to novel therapies preventing respiratory depression while sparing analgesia. Opioids depress breathing by slowing inspiratory airflow and delaying initiation, matching opioid depression of amplitude and frequency in the preöt slice. Glutamateric preöt neurons mediate this effect, and half of these neurons are sufficient to rescue opioid depression of the preöt rhythm. Opioids may act by silencing presynaptic release, removing neurons from the network or by hyperpolarizing rhythmogenic neurons. Two metrics define opioid-induced respiratory depression: decreased inspiratory airflow and delayed initiation. Locally eliminating the µ-opoid receptor in mice disambiguates pre and postyn effects, and defines two brain sites mediating opioid-induced respiratory depression. One site is the preöt, driven by 140 neurons, and these are not required for opioid-induced analgesia, a potential target for novel therapies. The preöt autonomously generates rhythmic activity in vitro, depressed by opioid agonists, and glutater neurons mediate this depression. Opi mRNA is expressed in glyergic, gabaergic, and glutater neurons, not exclusive to any rhythmogenic subpopulation. Glutater neurons mediate opioid-induced depression in vitro, 50% of preöt Opr1-expressing neurons. The preöt has been proposed to play a role in opioid-induced respiratory depression, localized injection results in depression and naloxone reverses breathing after systemic opioids. However, experiments fail to distinguish action on presyn terminals versus direct on preöt neurons. Genetically eliminating Opr1 from preöt cells, sparing inputs, shows no effect on breathing after saline, suggesting opioids do not exert endogenous effect in this context.
Researchers have identified sodium taurochotransport polypeptide (NTCP) as a functional receptor for human hepatitis B virus (HBV) and its satellite virus, hepatitis D (HD). The study used a novel approach of zero-distance photo-crosslinking and tandem purification to reveal that the pre1 region of the HB envelope protein interacts with NT, a transmembrane transporter predominantly expressed in the liver. Silencing NT inhibited HB and HD infection, while exogenous NT expression rendered nonsusceptible hepatarcinoma cells susceptible to viral infections. Amino acids 157-165 of nonfunctional monkey NT were replaced with human counterparts, conferring ability to support both viral infections. NT is conserved in mammals, but sequences vary among species, contributing to narrow tropism of viral infection. Despite high sequence homology between human and monkey NT, the latter did not support HB and HD infection. Replacing small motif of 157-165 of mkNT with hNT residues converted mkNT to receptor for pre1 as well as HD and HB infection. Further studies are warranted to determine NT's contribution to species specificity of HD and HB infection in other species and if other molecules contribute to entry as coreceptors or components. NT expression and subcellular distribution are regulated under physiological conditions, accounting for most hepatic Na-dependent acid transport. NT is inversely correlated with dedifferentiation of cancer in hepatocellular carcinoma and severity of HB-related cirrhosis. The role of NT as entry for HB and HD raises questions in viral pathogenesis.
Sepsis leads to lasting changes in the phenotype and function of memory CD8 T cells. In patients who survive the cytokine storm associated with seps, a state of immunopar characterized by lymphopenia and functional deficits in surviving CD8 cells is observed. Memory CD8 cells provide rapid cytolysis and cytokine production following reencounter with cognate antigen to promote long-term immunity, and CD8 cell impairment due seps can predispose individuals to refection. This study demonstrates that central CD8 cells (TCM) from septic patients proliferate more than those from healthy individuals. Utilizing LCMV mice and a CL model to induce seps, it is shown that TC proliferation is associated with recovery of path-specific memory CD8 cells following seps-induced lymphopen. Increased proliferation leads to changes in the composition of memory8 cell and altered localization. Further, memory8 cells from seps have an altered transcription profile and chromatin accessibility indicating lasting cell changes. Seps-induced changes the composition of the memory8 pool and transcription landscape culminate in altered cell function and reduced capacity to control L monocogen infection.
Researchers have investigated the mechanism by which APOB3A3, a cytidine deaminase, inhibits LINE-1 (L1) retroposition in human cells. L1 retroposition is a mutagenic threat to human genomes, and cells have evolved strategies to regulate it. APO3 is a family of enzymes that deaminate cytosine to uridine in single-strand DNA substrates. A3A is the most potent inhibitor of L1 retroposition in cultured assays, but previous characterization of L retroposition events in presence of A3 did not yield evidence of deamination. This study used in vitro and in vivo assays to demonstrate A3 inhibits L1 by deaminating transiently exposed single-str DNA during L integration. Data provide mechanistic explanation of A3 cytidine deamin protein inhibiting L retroposition. A3 can deaminate L cDNA in presence of RNase H, leading to C-to-T mutations on L non-coding strand and G-A on L-c strand. Deamination of 5 flanking-str genomic DNA in presence U leads to C-T in 5 T relative to 3 T. Deamination 3 flanking-str genomic in U results in G-A in 3 T relative 5. Overexpression A3 does not abolish L retroposition but reduces to 30 of control levels. Some L events in presence A and U lack evidence deamination. A can deam single-str L cDN in vitro, suggesting newly synthesized L remain annealed to mRNA after transcription, protecting from deamination. Engineered L events from cells co-expressing A and urac DNA glycosylase inhibitor U contain strand-specific A deamination. Data indicate A can inhibit L by deamin transient single-str DNA during L integration.
Researchers investigated the role of the activity-regulated non-canonical Ras-like GTPase Rem2 in visual circuit plasticity. They found that Rem2- mice exhibit a deficit in ocular dominance plasticity during the critical period, but not in early-phase or adult plasticity. At the cellular level, Rem2 regulates intrinsic excitability of layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons, prior to changes in synaptic function. Consistent with this, in vitro and in vivo recordings show increased firing in the absence of Rem2. Rem is a key molecule that regulates excitability and function in the context of sensory experience. Rem is a member of the Rad/Rem/Gem/K family, regulated by neuronal activity in response to calcium influx through voltage-gated channels. Gene knockdown in cultured neurons and Xenopus optic tect established Rem as a positive regulator of synapse and negative of dendritic complexity, suggesting activity-dependent plasticity. Rem expression and signaling is sensitive to neuronal levels and regulates changes in gene expression, leading to the hypothesis that Rem could regulate cortical plasticity in the nervous system. To test this, Rem knockout mice were generated to assess Rem's influence on cortical plasticity in the mammalian visual system. Rem- mice display impaired synaptic scaling, altered excitability, and increased eye responses. These results are similar to binocular deprivation, leading to increased cortical responsiveness. The combination of impaired scaling and increased excitability lead to increased firing in vivo. Rem is a non-canonical-like GTPase, unlikely regulated by nucleotide state, and associates with VGCC subunits and CaMK. Deficits depend on Rem deletion from excitatory neurons in cortex. At cellular level, Rem- exhibit altered function and increased excitability. Using sparse methods, effects on excit are cell-autonomous and precede synaptic. Consistent with increased excit, Rem induces firing in vitro and vivo. Rem provides understanding of molecular processes that coordinate excitability, firing, synapse, and circuit function.
The article discusses the limitations of magnetogenetics, a field that aims to make cells and neurons responsive to magnetic fields for controlling their activity, and magnetosensation, the ability of organisms to sense the Earth's magnetic field for navigation. The authors argue that recent high-profile articles proposing mechanisms for magnetos and magnetogen conflict with basic laws of physics and are not plausible. Calculations show that torques and temperatures produced by proposed biophysical schemes are too small by orders of magnitude for desired effects on molecular orientation and membrane channels. If phenomena occur, they must rely on different mechanisms. Ferritin-based magnetogen proposals have no chance of success due to paramagnetic nature of complexes. Authors and referees failed to perform back-of-the-envelope calculations to bolster claims. Claims violating laws of physics often turn out to be wrong, and understanding old physics is necessary to recognize discrepancies. Calculations are useful to guide experiment design. A new proposal for magnetotactic bacteria introduces a macromolecular compass that aligns with magnetic field, but is not plausible based on principles. A molecular system to couple magnetic fields to ionic current across cell membrane is proposed, but ferritin's paramagnetic nature limits its utility. Heating ferritin with magnetic fields is unlikely to activate TRPV channels. Ferritin is a large complex with 24 subunits forming a spherical shell 12 nm diameter. High-frequency magnetic fields can heat ferritin, but temperature increase is unlikely to open channels. Heating nanoparticles is an active area of research, but ferritin's size is too small for efficient heat transfer. Ferritin's paramagnetic nature limits its utility for magnetogen.
Researchers have studied the process of quiescence in Drosila neuroblasts, a type of stem cell that can self-renew, differentiate, or enter quies. They found that when neurobl enter quies, the factor Prosper is transiently detected in the neuroblast, followed by a unique molecular profile lacking progenitor and differentiation markers. A low level of nuclear Prosper precedes entry into neuroblast quies, even when timing is advanced or delayed by temporal factors. Loss of Prosper prevents entry into quies, whereas a pulse of low nuclear Prosper drives proliferating larval neurobl into quies. The study proposes that Prosper levels distinguish three progen fates: absent self-renew, low quies, and high differentiation. Quies stem cells preserve the cell pool for activation following disease or injury, and cancer cells can enter remission during treatment, possibly using similar mechanisms to stem quies. Studying quies may help design approaches to prevent dormancy and recurrence, as well as activate cells for tissue repair. Dila neurobl have served as a model for conserved pathways regulating stem proliferation and quies. They delinate from neuroecterm during embryogenesis, go through asymmetric division, and exit cycle at end embryogenesis. Upon exit, neurobl can undergo apoptosis or quies. During division, scaffolding Miranda is partitioned into GMC, carrying cargo, including Prosper, which is released into cytoplasm or nucleus. High vertebrate Prox can trigger differentiation, but low levels promote quies without differentiation. Prosper activates some genes at high levels, repress others at low. A model proposes that low Prosper repress progenitor genes in neurobl, but not deadpan, whereas high repress dead and activate differentiation. Temporal factors P and schedule quies timing, but regulate Prosper import unknown. In lar neurobl, Gr prevents Prosper accumulation, inducing exit and differentiation. Caliban promotes Prosper export in S cells, but unknown in neurobl. Prosper is an atypical homeodomain repressing progenitor and cycle genes. The study shows transient Prosper in neurobl prior to quies, loss prevents quies, and pulse low drives proliferating neuro into quies. A model proposes low Prosper repress all cycle and progen except dead, high repress dead for differentiation.
Researchers have developed a single-molecule microscopy method to study the kinetics of HIV-1 capsid unco, a tightly regulated disassembly process required for viral DNA release. They observed that opening the first defect in the lattice is the rate-limiting step of unco, followed by rapid collapse. Capsid inhibitor PF74 accelerates opening but stabilizes the lattice, while binding to a polyan to a conserved arginine cluster delays initiation but does not prevent disassembly. Observations suggest different stages of unco can be controlled by the interplay between capsid regulators.

The study measured single-particle unco kinetics of authentic capsids after membrane removal, observing effects of caps-binding molecules on discrete steps. The majority of capsids contain defects or uncoat immediately. In absence of binders, unating is an-all process that proceeds after first defect created. Capsid molecules exhibit differential effects on initiation and continuation of unating. The heterogeneity of caps is described, with assembly yielding conical, tubular shapes often containing structural defects. Researchers identified functionally classes of caps based on GFP kinetics, quantifying heterogeneity and relating to assembly states.

Using a pore-form to permeabilize the membrane, they observed early events upon exposure to change conditions, capturing unstable states. Results show assembly range from incomplete to fully closed caps, exhibiting differences in unco kinetics. They quantified fraction of particles lacking envelope and GFP, which may affect maturation and assembly or misclassification. 60-80% of caps are not fully closed and unable to retain GFP upon permeabilization, including early intermediates and fully assembled yet unable to close, assigned to leaky structures observed by cryo-electron microscopy and GFP in vivo.

The observation of Cyp binding at low concentrations is neutral to caps opening, enabling kinetic studies of disassembly. Defective caps collapsed after permeabil, stable in presence of high CA. Lattice disassembly of intact caps is rapid after first gap appeared, unating primarily controlled by rate removing first CAunits. PF accelerates opening but stabilizes lattice, while hexarboxbenene strongly inhibits step.
A study investigated the role of the JUN-terminal kinase (J) signaling pathway in breast cancer development. Loss-of-function mutations in J signaling components, such as MAPK1, MAP2, and MAP7, are implicated in breast cancer etiology. To examine the effect of J deficiency in the mammary epithelium, control and J-deficient mice were compared to control mice. J-deficiency caused breast cancer and accelerated tumor formation in a mouse model. The tumor-suppressive function of J was not mediated by its role in established tumors, but by its requirement to prevent initiation. Data identify J pathway defects as 'driver mutations that promote instability and initiation. The J pathway promotes genome stability and tumor suppression, distinct from TP53, which is known for stability and suppression. J-deficiency caused more SNVs and indels than TP-def, but similar CNVs. J signaling can promote proliferation through AP transcription factors, but its major role in the epithelium is suppression. Frequent J pathway ablation and mutation in human breast indicate its tumor suppressor function.
Researchers have identified peptidylglyc α-amating monxygenase (PAM), an enzyme responsible for generating amidated bioactive peptides, as crucial for cilium assembly in Chlamdomonas and mammalian cells. P is present in the Golgi and cilia of C. reinii and mammalian cells, including tracheal epithelial, fibroblasts, and spermatozoa. In C. reinii, P activity is associated with the axonem superstructure, suggesting a conserved signaling function in eukotic cilia. P plays a conserved role in early ciliogenesis, linking amidation and assembly in multiple cell types. In C. reinii, P knockdown resulted in loss of cilia beyond the transition zone, abnormal Golgi architecture, and altered c assembly components. Decreased P expression reduced motile cilia on the ventral surface of planaria, with cytoplasmic axemes lacking ciliary membrane. Primary cilia on neuroepithelial cells in P-/- mice were also aberrant. P activity and post-Gol trafficking contribute to cili defects, providing a link between amidation and assembly. P is conserved in multiple cell types, required for biogenesis of motile cilia in C. rein and ventral epithelium planaria, and primaryilia neuroepith cells. In C. rein, P reduction did not affect growth, but led to loss ofilia, with c stubs containing electron-dense material. Basal docking and division were comparable, but microtule extension from basal was disrupted in Pami cells, similar to IFT and I46 mutants. PH and bifunctional P in planaria revealed complexities in regulation. PH activity was reduced in S-ph and S-p RNA animals, with predominant role in gliding. Double knockdown resulted in loss motilia and ectopic axemes in cytoplasm.
Class III myos (My3) and actin bundling protein Espin play critical roles in the development and maintenance of stereocilia in vertebrate cells, and their defects cause hereditary impairments. My3 interacts with Espin through its tailology motif (TH), however, it is unclear how My3 specifically acts through Espin to regulate actin assembly and stabilization. This study discovers My3 TH contains a pair of sequences capable of independently and strongly binding to ankyrin repeats of Esp, revealing an unexpected My3-mediated crossing mechanism of Esp. The structures of My3 in complex with Esp elucidate the mechanism of binding and reveal My3-induced Espin autoinhibition release. Cell-based filopodia formation and microscopic actin bundling assays provide evidence that My3 and Esp work together to assemble and promote parallel actin formation in processes such as stereilia. The study reveals the probable molecular mechanism of hearing loss in humans with mutations Mya (DF30) and supports the hypothesis of delayed loss in DF30 subjects due to My compensatory mechanism. The full-length My/Espin complex may have higher actin bundling activity than the My-AB/Espin complex. This study reveals a direct myosin-induced bundling regulation mechanism of actin crosser protein and demonstrates an intimate action between classes of actin proteins controlling actin size and stability.
Voltage-gated calcium channels, such as those found in excitable cells like neurons and heart and skeletal muscles, control various functions by translating membrane depolarization into calcium influx. To accomplish these diverse functions, different channels activate at distinct voltages and kinetics. Researchers combined modeling, mutagenesis, and electrophysiology to analyze the structures, free energy, and kinetics of the activated and resting states of two functionally distinct voltage-sensing domains (VSDs) of the eukotic calcium channel Ca1.1. Both VSDs displayed features of the sliding helix model, but differed in ion-pair formation of outer charges. Stabilization of the activated state enhanced voltage of activation, while stabilization of resting slowed kinetics. This provides a mechanistic model explaining how ionair formation in VSDs realize characteristic properties of voltageated cation channels.

Advanced models of Ca1.1 VSD fill a gap in understanding of voltageensing in eukotic voltageated channels. Results demonstrate two VSD, differing in S helix displacement and ionic bonds between outer charges. These differences correspond to free states and kinetics, correlating with experimentally determined kinetics and voltage dependences of activation. Structure-function combination of simulation and MSM proved high value. Tested interactions showed predicted effects when experimentally by mutagen and electrophys analysis. Alterations recognized experimentally reproduced by model. Structures of lowest states Ca1 VSD display features of sliding helix model and previous simulations.

Voltage-gated channels translate depolarization into calcium influx, contributing to cellular excitability and functions like contraction, secretion, and regulation. With voltageated sodium, they form a structurally related superfamily with four symmetry. Their poreing subunits are composed of homolog non-identical repeats, each six transmembrane helices. S and S6 form the central pore with selectivity and gate. Hel S of each repeat form separate voltageensing domains. S contains positively residues in every position, movement thought to initiate conformation change in opening. High-resolution structures of proary and eukotic channels have been solved. Advances cry-elect microscopy enabled determination of voltageated channel Ca1 at 3.6 resolution, displaying pore and VSD in activated. Recently, cry structures of homoetram channels locked in resting or V-down states have been reported. However, up now resting of eotic and channels remained inaccessible.

The sliding model of voltage action describes negative potential pulling positively S helices toward cytoplasm holding gate closed. Reversal electric upon depolar causes outward S hel by 10. Movement positive through hydrophobic constriction facilitated by ionair with negative in other helices. This describes principal mode action without structure-function, explaining variability between four VSD eotic channels and unique properties. Accumulating evidence indicates differences individual isoforms. Rabbit muscle Ca1 is first of Ca family solved. Based cry structure Ca1, a model study molecular determining specific properties. Researchers investigated the role of matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) in adult brain plasticity, particularly in healthy and stroke-affected brains. They found that optimal MMP activity is crucial for visual plasticity to occur in both healthy and stroke-affected brains. In healthy mice, 7-day application of MMP-inhibitor prevented plasticity, while treatment with MMP-inhib once but not twice after stroke rescued plasticity normally lost under these conditions. The study suggests that MMPactivity has to be within a narrow window to allow plasticity, if MMPactivity is downregulated with inhibitors or upregulated after stroke-induced changes are compromised. In healthy brains, enzymatic activity of MMP has plastic promoting effects, loosening the extracellular matrix and influencing signaling pathways, altering neuronal structure and activity. Involvement of MMP in synaptic remodeling has been studied in the hippocampus, yet their expression in the central nervous system indicates a broader function. The results establish a novel function of MMP in experience enhancements of the optotor reflex of the eye in adult mice, highlighting a critical role of MMP in visual plasticity and suggesting upregulation of MMPactivity after stroke compromises experience plastic, which can be rescued by brief MMP-inhibition. Precise regulation of MMPactivity seems essential for plastic in the brain, offering opportunities in treatment and recovery afterstroke.
Researchers used bifunctional methaniosulfate crossers to calibrate conformations in AMPA receptors in the presence and absence of auxiliary subunit Stargin. Their data indicate that extracellular layer of AMP receptors can get trapped in stable opened conformations, especially upon long exposures to glutamate. In contrast, Stargin limits this conformation flexibility. Under synaptic conditions, where glutamate and auxiliary proteins dominate, extracellular domains of AMP receptors likely stay compact during gating. Cryo-EM structures of AMP receptors in complex with auxiliary subunits show reduced separation of V residues compared to receptors without proteins. For example, sulfhydry groups on subunits are 19 Å apart in activated receptors without St and 15 Å in complex St. In desensitized state, equivalent residues are 21 apart in homolog receptors and 17 in desitized Glu2 associated with G1 proteins. Longest crosser M has less effect on desitized V receptors with St. Trapping with bis-M crossers indicated conformation flexibility of L layer in desitized AMP receptors. Researchers have developed an optimized protocol for isolating immune cells from the intestines of mice infected with the parasitic worm Heligomosoides polygyrus bakeri. The standard digestion protocol was modified to remove excess mucus and improve cell yield, resulting in 3-6 million live cells per duod with 70-80% viability and 20-30% CD45+ cells. The optimized protocol was effective at different stages of infection, including larval development, acute, and chronic infection. The isolated cells were analyzed using a 23-color flow cytometry panel and validated by confocal microscopy, identifying hallmarks of anti-parasite immune responses throughout the course of infection. High-dimensional analysis allowed for single-cell discoveries of local helmin immune responses previously unfeasible. RNA extraction was also possible from isolated cells, enabling single-cell analysis tools like single RNA sequencing. The study highlights the importance of good cell isolation techniques to investigate intestinal responses against helmin directly. Helmin infections represent 50% of parasitic infections in human and livestock, limiting analysis to imaging, phenotypical observations, or secondary locations like lymph nodes or spleen, which may not reflect local immunity.
Researchers have developed a comprehensive mathematical model to describe the dynamics of the 20S proteasome, a key enzyme responsible for protein degradation in cells. The model accounts for interactions between substrates, products, and proteolytic and regulatory sites, and suggests the rate-limiting step of hydrolysis is transport of substrates into the proteome. Efficiency varies between standard and immuno-proteomes, impacting total degradation and substrate cleavage usage. The model helps understand how 20 proteas degrades specific proteins in cells and forms basis for future studies of active proteas in cells, such as bound to regulatory complexes 19 and 28. Observing proteome dynamics over time using purified 20 proteome and homogenates, the model describes core proteome features that reflect dynamics in complex cellular scenarios. The model provides interpretative framework for proteas action, accounting for substrate-specific effects.

 The 20 proteome is dynamic, catalytic activity varies over time, influenced by interactions of substrates and products with proteolytic and regulatory sites. Analysis suggests the cavity is progressively filled by peptides, facilitating other molecules to enter until effective volume is almost complete. Estimated substrate and product numbers inside proteome are close to maxima. Accumulation leads to activation of non-catal modifier sites, located inside proteome. Key part of transport is capacity of proteome-chambers and main chamber, dependent on volume of proteome and substrate. Model estimates for tested substrates agree with predicted numbers inside proteome chamber.

 Different proteome isoforms, standard and immuno, degrade substrates with different rates, generate same pool. Immuno-proteome has higher polype degradation rate than standard. Steps of proteolysis may be responsible for isoform kinetics: transport, binding, peptide-hydrolysis, and release. Question of medical relevance: degradation specific substrates by isoforms or carrying polymorph/mutations involved in pathologies. Proteins, oxidised, can be degraded 20 proteomes without polyubiquination. 20 proteomes regulate host molecules in cell cycle, growth, control, oncogenesis.
Researchers have identified a new β-alanine transporter, named Bala, which plays a crucial role in visual transmission in Drosila. The recycling of neurotransmitters is essential for sustained synaptic transmission, and histamine is required for visual transmission. Histamine is taken up by laminar glia and converted to carcinine, which is then delivered to photoreceptors, where it is hydrolyzed to histamine and βanine. However, the fate of βanine was unknown. Bala is a plasma membrane protein that facilitates β-al uptake in cultured cells and is expressed in retinal pigment cells, rescuing photore synaptic transmission in bal1 mutants. Bala mutants have lower β-al levels and altered distribution, supporting the hypothesis that Bala transports βanine. The hist recycling pathway involves histidine decarboxase in photore cells, releasing histamine into the synaptic cleft, which is taken up by lamin glial cells and conjugated to βanine, inactivating hist and generating carcin. Carine is released and internalized by CarT in photoreceptors. Hist is loaded into synaptic vesicles, but β-al's fate was unknown. Bala transports βanine in retinal cells, recycling it for histamine homeostasis and visual transmission. Retinal cells play a critical role in hist recycling, and Bala is key for β-al trafficking.
Researchers investigated the role of oxidants in aging using chronologically aging yeast as a model. They found that cells undergo a sudden redox collapse, affecting 80% of identified thiol-containing proteins before death. Contrary to the free radical theory of aging, this collapse is not triggered by an increase in endogenous oxidants, but by a drop in NAD levels, which normally maintains redox homeostasis. Caloric restriction, a universal lifespan-extending measure, increases NAD levels and delays the cascade. Studies reveal a timeline of events leading to system-wide oxidation of the proteome before cell death.

A quantitative redox proteomic approach was used to assess physiological redox events in non-dividing cells. Chronologically aging S. cerevisiae underwent an abrupt loss of red homeostasis, indicated by oxidation of majority thiol-containing cytos, nuclear, and mitochondrial proteins. Oxidation is delayed by caloric and hypometabolic conditions, suggesting maintenance of redostasis contributes to lifespan effects. The kinetics of oxidation in 300 different thiols showed two waves: a small subset (10%) oxidized within 24-48hr, and 70% oxidized 24hr later. Cells maintained energy resources and survived several days after collapse.

Bioinformatic analysis revealed early oxidation targets cystines in C2/3 motif. OxAT was used to quantify steady-state oxidation of 400 yeast thiols and identify red-sensitive cystines. Monitoring oxidation during lifespan obtained a spatial-temporal read of oxidation, uncovering targets contributing to physiological alterations. Chronological S. cerevisiae represents a model for aging postmit cells. Lifespan is the time non-dividing cells remain viable in metabolic state. Support the radical theory, lifespan decreases in strains lacking oxid scavenging enzymes, increases glutathione/SOD. Caloric restriction increases lifespan, significantly oxidative resistance.

Monitoring thiol status of 300 thiols showed cells undergo global red collapse, 80% proteins oxidized days before death. Cluster revealed oxidation preceded oxidation of conserved proteins, NAD-dependent thioreductase, key in redostasis. Red metabol and NAD studies suggested oxidation triggered by NAD decrease. Consistent with hypothesis, cal restriction delayed NAD decrease, oxidation, red collapse, and death. Results suggest NAD levels initiate oxidation, system-wide events preceding cell death.
The study presents the mechanistic basis of chirality in the proofreading process during translation of the genetic code, focusing on the enzyme D-aminoacyl-tRNA deacylase (DTD) from Plasium falcum. The crystal structure of DTD in complex with a substrate-mim analog reveals how it uses an invariant crossunit Glyis dipeptide to capture the chiral centre of D-amacyl-tRNA. No protein residues are directly involved in catalysis, but the unique side-independent mode of substrate recognition explains D's ability to act on multiple D-am acids. The chiral specificity ensures the enriched pool of L-amacy-tRNAs escapes this proof step. The study provides insights into fundamental enantio selection and elucidates a chiral mechanism crucial in preventing-am acid infiltration during translational apparatus.

Cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) is a chloride channel belonging to the superfamily of ATP-binding cassette (ABC) proteins. Following phosphorylation of the cytosolic regulatory (R) domain by cAMP-dependent protein kinase (PK), CF channels are gated by cytos ATP. The pore within the transmembrane domains (TMDs) opens upon formation of a head-to-tail nucleotide-binding domain (NBD) dimer stabilized by two molecules of ATP occluded at the interface. Pore closure accompanies NBD disruption by hydrolysis at the interfacial site harbouring the motifs of N2. As evidence of strict coupling N and T movements, a hydrogen bond between the chains of N2 Walker threonine (T6) and N1 arginine (555) was shown to form in open, but not closed channels. These positions have co-evolved as a pair and in structures of nucleotide-bound N dimers the analogous chains also form a bond, arginine as donor and threonine/serine acceptor.

While ATP-driven CFTR openings are coupled to N dimer formation, the mechanism of spontaneous openings in absence is unknown. This study shows spontaneous openings linked to tightening of N interface around site 2, as in presence bound. Strict coupling constrains mechanistic models of ATP-dependent gating and understanding driving forces conserved among ABC proteins. It explains modulatory effects of mutations, physiological or pharmacological regulators. An effect not expected if interfaces not approach in open state, explained by linked N dimer even in G551. Researchers have proposed a new mechanism for the assembly of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers, referred to as the "pre-transition" or "orderobic" effect. This effect arises from the first-order transition between ordered and disordered phases in the membrane. Using large-scale simulations, they show that a protein with hydrophobic thickness equal to that of the disordered phase embedded in an ordered bilayer stabilizes a microscopic–order-dis interface with finite stiffness. When such proteins approach each other, assembly reduces the interfacial energy, analogous to the hydroobic effect. The orderobic effect is mediated by proximity to the order-dis phase and hydroph mismatch of the protein. The strength and range of forces from this effect are significantly larger than those from membrane elasticity for considered.

The orderobic effect is observed in simulations of α-helix proteins in membranes with coexisting liquid-ordered and liquid-disordered domains. A consequence of this effect is the existence of a driving force moving orderobic proteins from ordered to dis phase and creation of disordered domains from clustering orderobic proteins. A dual effect of the orderobic is a transmembrane in dis that favors ordered can nucleate an ordered region and order-dis interface. For example, a protein with positive mismatch would induce in its vicinity. This effect is illustrated in interfaces separating ordered and disordered regions providing a force for assembly, similar to lipidrafts in membranes.

The orderobic effect depends on the protein's hydroph thickness matching the ordered phase, and is maximal for thickness equal to the disordered phase. Proteins with zero mismatch do not induce disordered and the orderobic vanishes. The effect produces excess mobility by melting a surrounding layer and facilitating protein motions. This explains protein reorganization in so-called 'gel' phases. The prediction of enhanced mobility surrounding orderobic proteins may be tested by single molecule techniques. Biological membranes and transmem proteins are more complex than models, with multiple components allowing multiple order-dis transitions.
Researchers investigated the relationship between action potentials (APs) and fluorescence signals in genetically encoded calcium indicators (GECs) in vivo, specifically in layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons of the primary visual cortex. They used transgenic mice expressing GCMP6 or GC6f and simultaneously recorded APs with cell-attached recordings. Most APs were detected under optimal conditions, but only a minority of 1 and 2 AP events were detected under typical population imaging conditions, emphasizing the limits of detection. The study aimed to characterize the AP-fluorescence transfer function and provide ground truth data for spike inference algorithms. The AP-calc fluorescence curves were constructed from isolated events, with APs summed over 250 ms and fluorescence traces aligned to the first AP. For GC6, events were separated by 100 and 500 ms, and for GCf by 300 ms. Only a minority of APs were used to construct curves, and peak fluorescence was calculated by finding the maximum ΔF from 0 to 500 and 300 ms after the AP, respectively. Three spike inference algorithms were tested: non-negative deconvolution (ND), non-negative deconv with L0 constraint, and a biophysical model. All estimated AP numbers approximately recapitulated electrophys, but not integers due to imperfect inference. The study contributes to understanding the relationship between spiking and fluorescence, aiding interpretation of existing and future imaging datasets.
Researchers have discovered a unique biological valve in the female genitalia of the Brazilian insect Neotla that allows it to receive multiple seminal packages from the same or different males. The valve is composed of two sclerites, a closure element, and in- and out canals. Females have a penis-like introment organ to coercively anchor males and obtain voluminous semen. The semen is packed in a capsule, whose formation is initiated by seminal injection. It is not only used for fertilization but also consumed by the female as nutrition. The complex has two slots for insemination, enabling Neotla to mate while the first is occupied. In conjunction with the penis, this valve is a morphological novelty enabling females to compete for seminal in nutrient-poor habitats through copulations and multiple injections. The evolution of this valve may have been a prerequisite for the reversal of the introment organ in Neotla.

Researchers used high-resolution optical tweezers to study the assembly of synaptic SNARE proteins, which are crucial for neurotransmitter release. They found four stages of assembly: N-terminal, middle, C-terminal, and linker domains (NTD, MD, CTD, and LD) with distinct functions. NTD is responsible for vesicle docking, CTD for fusion, and MD regulates assembly and fusion. Munc-1 initiates SN assembly and stabilizes the half-zip SN complex, independent of syntaxin's N-terminal domain. Observations demonstrate distinct functions of SN domains and their assembly is chapered by M-1.

 Synaptic SN assembly underlies strength, speed, and precision of neurotransmission, as well as neurological disorders. Stage SN assembly crosslinking at N-terminal of NTD affects measured energy but not kinetics of other domains. Combining findings with exocysis, confirmed distinct functions of SN stages in exysis. Mutations cause heterogeneity in SN assembly and abolish MD assembly. MD is targeted by regulatory proteins, including complexin and synaptagmin.

Binds to t-SN complex, VAMP, and ternary SN complex. Exact functions in SN assembly and fusion not understood. M-1-mediated SN assembly abolished by V2 mutations L60 and L63, suggesting no effect on accuracy of intrinsic SN assembly.

Researchers have identified a common electrophysiological marker that tracks states of reduced arousal, including sleep and anesthesia in humans. The marker is the 1/f slope of the electrophys power, which reflects the non-oscillatory, scale component of neural activity. This slope differentiates wakefulness from propof anesthesia, non-rapid eye movement (NREM) sleep, and rapid eye (REM) sleep. The study used intracran and surface recordings in four independent data sets to demonstrate that the 1/f slope of the electroph power, estimated from 30 to 45 frequency, reliably differentiates wakefulness from three states of reduced arousal, namely, REM, N sleep, and general anesthesia with propof. The 1 dynamics are observed across various tasks, lifespan, and exhibit state-dependent variations during sleep and anesthesia. The underlying mechanisms giving rise to the 1 decay of the electroph power are not well understood. Recordings from subd grid electrodes showed the same pattern: spectral decreased from wake to anesthesia in majority of sites. The spectral decreased under anesthesia compared to wake in both scalp and intrran EEG. The results indicate the spectral differentiates wake and anesthesia in humans. The effect spanned scalp and majority intrran electrodes, supporting the notion propof anesthesia a global brain-wide change. The study examined if the state modulation generalized to other forms decreased arousal, such as sleep. Analyzed datasets during full night sleep. Obtained polysn recordings from 20 subjects, including EEG, electrocardiography (ECG), electromyography (EM), and electroocography (EOG). Recorded intrran EEG in separate cohort to determine spatial and subc contributions. Combined intrran EEG with polysn (EEG, ECG, EM, EOG) enabled sleep staging across scalp and intrran studies. The time-resolved spectral tracked technician-scored hypogram. Quantified effect by comparing slope across wake, N, and sleep. The results from four studies provide main advances: the 1/f tracks arousal in propof and sleep with high precision, observable range timescales sub-epochs to night; 2/f correlates with stages; 3 mirrored changes excitability; 4 support premise anesthesia brain-wide; 5 spectral can be estimated scalp recordings.
Researchers have discovered that stromules, dynamic tubular extensions of chloroplasts, interact with microtubules (MTs) and actin filaments (AF) to facilitate chloroplast movement during innate immunity. MTs promote stromule extension and stabilization, while AFs anchor stromules and provide points for chloroplast positioning. A correlation between strom direction and chloroplast movement was observed, suggesting strom-directed chloroplast movement is a general function of stromules in epider cells. Results show MTs and AFs facilitate peruclear clustering of chloroplasts during innate response. A model proposes stromules extend along MTs, connect to AF points surrounding nuclei, guiding chloroplast movement to nuclei during immunity.

 Silencing γ-tubulin complex 4 (G4) caused MT bundling and dynamics, resulting in longer stromules with slower extension and retraction. Strom extension does not require AFs, but they function as anchor points stabilizing stromules and chloroplast bodies. AFs play a role in chloroplast movement directed by stromules, which is disrupted by inhibitors. However, strom-directed chloroplast movement was observed when AFs partially disrupted, suggesting chlor anchoring restricts strom movement. A function of stromules is to direct chloroplast movement during innate response. During immunity, a model proposes stromules extend MT towards nuclei, attach to AF points, and peruclear attachments guide chloroplast to nucleus.

Cytoskeletal elements support various cellular functions, including cytoplasm streaming, cell division, elongation, growth, ves trafficking, nuclear positioning, and morphogenesis. This study shows stromules along MT and AFs stabilize strom and chloroplast-nuclear connections during innate response. Stromules direct chlor movement and AF anchoring guide peruclear chlor clustering innate. Previous studies indicated AF and MT during strom formation. Strom visualized by differential contrast interacting with AF labeled GFP-Talin and rearranging AF changed strom morphology. Movement AF implicated by myosin XI domain and small domain localizing chloroplast. Knockdown myos XI disrupted strom or quantitatively decreased plastids with strom. However, dynamics along AF were not examined. Longer myos XI domain and full myos XI do not localize chloroplast. Time-lapse confocal microscopy of strom and AF dynamics was conducted.

In future, molecular components for strom dynamics and movement and importance peruclear clustering during immunity will be investigated. Stromules play a role in innate and programmed cell death. During response, chloroplast move nucleus and chloroplast connections are established. Mechanism behind peruclear clustering and chlor interactions unknown. Results indicate AF and MT during peruclear clustering. MT promote strom extensions, contributing strom movement, while AF position chloroplast towards nucleus. Reinforce AF as points for strom previously shown in Arabidopsis hypocotyl and study showing strom maintaining contact with nuclei. Researchers have developed a method to predict residue-residue interactions across protein interfaces using evolutionary information. They used a pseudo-likelihood statistical method to examine residue-residue covariance in 50 ribosomal and 28 bacterial protein complexes with known structures and found that pairs with high covariance are almost always in contact. The method was used to predict subunit contacts for 36 complexes with unknown structures and models for four complexes, including the tripart ATP-independent periplasm (TR) transporter, tripart efflux system, pyruvate form lyase-activating enzyme, and methine ABC transporter. The results show strong selective pressure at protein interfaces beyond residue conservation and co-evolving pairs are nearly in contact. However, not all contacting residues co-evolve nor all protein interfaces. The method is useful for modeling protein complexes due to poor representation in PDB. The approach is applicable to any taxa, but focused on prokaryotes and mitochondria to avoid paralog problem. The study began with 50S ribosomal subunit, the largest evolutionarily conserved multiprotein complex with atomic structure. Multiple alignments were constructed for each protein in the complex and paired alignments for each pair of proteins. A GREIN statistical model was built for each paired alignment, computing coupling strengths and ranking pairs based on scores. Only a fraction of pairs co-evolve in 50S sub with coupling greater than 1.5, and these are almost within 8Å of each other in structure. The method was used to predict contacts for 36 complexes with unknown structures and models for four complexes, including TR, tripart efflux, pyvate lyase-activ enzyme, and methine ABC. The predicted contacts were used to assemble models of complexes from components and evaluated on a docking set of 18 complexes with at least one component or homolog with known structure. The model with best restraint had interface within 4Å of native in 14 cases and more than half native contacts in 16. The sum of coupling between proteins can distinguish interacting and non-interacting pairs in 50 sub, but not all interactions will be identified. The number of aligned sequences required for accurate prediction was found to be greater than the sum of lengths of two proteins. The method is useful for protein complexes due to poor representation in PDB.
Researchers investigated how humans categorize visual scenes by accumulating information from multiple locations in a scene using a novel gaze-conting paradigm in a categorization task. Participants' scan paths followed an active strategy that incorporates acquired information about the scene and statistical structure of patterns. Intriguingly, categorization was improved when locations were revealed by an optimal Bayesian active algorithm. By combining a Bayesian observer and sensor, a major portion of apparent optimality arose from biases, perceptual noise, and inaccuracies in eye movements. The process of selecting locations was 70% efficient in the task. Results suggest participants select eye movements to maximize information about categories requiring integration from multiple locations.

 In contrast, high-efficiency active sensing is a natural strategy humans adopt without deliberation, evidenced by inter-sacc intervals in naturalistic tasks and near-asympt efficiency in the first session. Using a categorization task ensured no single location was informative, but required integration from multiple locations to select the next movement and solve the task. This is in contrast to studies quantifying informativeness of single locations in a scene, which showed target fixations tend to choose general (Goss and Schs, 2001; Peterson Eckstein,201; Toscan et al,201; Choskie et al,201) or search where the target is fully informative (Najem and Geis,200). Most studies have not addressed the interplay between information and fixation of natural active sensing, where the informative location is dependent on history of fixations.

 Active sensing extends to tasks with arbitrary features, not spatial, such as categories, and provides fixation-fixation analysis under eye movements by matching observer to participants' performance. Maximal efficiency requires a closed interaction between perception, integrating information from fixated locations with prior about prevalence and patterns, and planning movement directing next fixation at potentially relevant. Inspired by this, an experiment was designed with patterns statistically-controlled and simple, ensuring fove vision dominated by a gaze-conting task. In contrast to previous studies (Najem andis,200; Ren et al,2007; Peterson Eck,201; Mor and Maloney,201), required multiple locations to extract abstract categories.

 Participants performed better with revealings chosen by BAS than own paths, but results suggest suboptim was due to biases, perception, noise, and inaccuracies constraining selection rather than active strategy. Movements were more efficient than heuristic strategies employing subset of closed sensing and 70% as optimal with participants' bias, perception, noise, and inaccuracies. Importantly, estimate does not conflate inference with selection, even biased or inconsistent, can measure beliefs select optimally. Inefficiency may be unmodeled constraints, such as biases for directions or fixating centre, apparent in data.
