Researchers used functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to investigate the ventral occipitotemporal cortex (VOT) responses to auditory and visual stimuli in sighted and congenitally blind individuals. The study aimed to determine if vision is necessary for the development of categorical organization in VOT. Results showed that VOT encodes categories in both sight and blind individuals using a representational structure and connectivity similar to the one found in vision. However, sound categories were more reliably encoded in the blind than sight group using a representational format closer to the one in vision. Crucially, VOT in blind represents categorical membership of sounds rather than their acoustic features. The study suggests that sounds trigger categorical responses in VOT of congenally and sight people that match the topography and functional profile of visual responses, despite nuances in categorical organization between modalities.

The study used multivariate analyses to map representational geometry and responses to images and sounds in VOT of sight and blind individuals. Results demonstrate early deprivation triggers extension of intrinsic, non-visual organization VOT, supported by connectivity with large-scale brain networks. Categorical organization of auditory stimuli in VOT of blind and sight exhibits nuances compared to visual organization in sight people. A study showed patterns elicited by sounds in VOT blind predict response to images of categories in VOT sight, suggesting distributed responses for vision and sounds. Together, studies suggest more to development of VOT than meets the eye. However, questions remain unanswered, such as overlap between visual processing and non-visual inputs, and whether V represents similar content in groups.

The study found that VOT shows similar functional profile for visual and auditory stimuli in sight and blind, and encodes categorical dimension across inputs and experiences. Involvement of right occipital for arithmetic in blind relates to intrinsic spatial nature, involved in computation. The study goes beyond previous results in six ways: similar representational structure for sounds and images in sight, higher accuracies and consistency in blind, represent structure closer to auditory in blind than sight, large-scale connectivity profiles for images and sounds, and dimension of stimuli determines response. VOT encodes categorical membership sounds in sight and blind using represent format similar to images in sight, supporting intrinsic organization independent of vision and multisensory scaffolding.
Researchers have discovered a strain of the fungal pathogen Entomophthora muscae (E. mus) that manipulates the behavior of Drosophila melanogaster (D. melanaster) in the laboratory. Infected flies exhibit morund behaviors characteristic of E. mus infection, including climbing, extending their proboscis, and raising their wings, creating a path for fungal spores to launch from their abdomens. The fungus invades the nervous system, suggesting a direct means by which it induces behavioral changes. Given the molecular toolkit available for D. melanaster, this system enables rapid progress in understanding how E mus manipulates host behavior. E mus sensu strictu isolates have been observed infecting house flies (Musca domestica) and have been found to be capable of causing infection in other hosts outside Drosidae. The researchers measured transcriptional changes in host and fungus at 24 time points over 5 days and found that E mus 'Ber' is present in the brain and its titer increases with progression of infection. The molecular toolkit for D melan includes a catalog of neurons, connections, reagents for activating or inactivating specific neurons, and purifying cells, and tracking behaviors, which will be leveraged to explore the molecular basis of this phenomenon.
Researchers have generated transcriptome-wide binding profiles of 30 RNA degradation factors in yeast Saccharomyces cerevisiae using photoactivatable ribonucleide-enhanced crosslink and immunoprecipitation (PARCLIP). The profiles reveal the distribution of degradation factors between different RNA classes and are consistent with canonical degradation pathways for closed-loop forming mRNAs after deadenylation. Modeling of mRNA half-lives suggests most degradation factors bind intact mRNAs, whereas decapping factors are recruited for mRNA degradation, consistent with decapping being a rate-limiting step. Decapping factors preferentially bind mRNAs with non-optimal codons, consistent with degradation of inefficiently translated mRNAs. Global analysis suggests the surveillance machinery, including complexes N1/N3 and TR4, targets aberrant nuclear RNAs and snoRNAs.

The study measured transcript-wide locations of 30 degradation factors involved in deadenylation, decapping, exosome degradation, and RNA pathways including nuclear surveillance and cytoplasmic nonsense decay (NMD). High reproducibility of PARCL experiments was revealed by comparison of two biological replicates with Spearman correlations 0.87-1.00 (mean 0.94). Tens of thousands of factor-RNA cross sites were obtained with p-values ≤ 0.005. Transcript maps represent an extensive dataset of in vivo-binding sites for factors in RNA degradation. Most PARCL reads fall into mRNA class, with many factors showing considerable reads in ncRN, particularly rRN.

Comparisons alongside published profiles of other RNA-binding factors revealed clusters of factors co-occupying RN or localizing RN. Data are consistent with published results and extend to global scale. Results revealed several novel findings, including that Pan/Pan deadlation complex subunits C4/Not bound preferentially pA site, reflecting function in poly tail shortening early degradation. C/ subunits show variations in RNA specificities, suggesting isoforms varying composition or conformation rearrangements. Decapping factors show cross-link near RNA, consistent proposed near p site.

The study provides a resource for future studies of eukotic RNA pathways, mechanisms, and integration metabolism. TR4 adds poly tail aberrant RN, triggering nuclear exosome degradation. Researchers investigated the ecology and evolution of viruses infecting uncultivated SUP bacteria in a marine oxygen minimum zone (OM) using single-cell and metagenomics. They isolated 127 single amplified genomes (SAGs) from uncultivated SUP bacteria and identified 69 viral contigs representing five new genera within dsDNA and ssDNA viruses. Infection frequencies suggest ∼1/3 of SUP bacteria is viral-infected, with higher frequency where oxygen deficiency was most severe. Observed Microviridae clonality suggests recovery of bloom-termin viruses, while co-infection between dsDNA and ss viruses indicates previously unrecognized cooperation. Analyses of microbial and viral metagenomes revealed SUP viruses persisted for years but remained endemic to OM. Identification of virus-encoded dissimulatory sulf reductase suggests SUP viruses reprogram their host's metabolism. The study demonstrates coupled SUP virus-host co-evolution dynamics with potential to modulate biochemical cycling in climate-critical and OMZs.

The researchers used single-cell genomics to link SUP viruses and hosts across gradients of oxygen and sulf over a 3-year interval in a model OM ecosystem, revealing endemic patterns of co-infection between ss and ds viruses and occurrence of AM with potential to modulate electron reactions essential to SUP metabolism. The findings offer novel perspectives on the ecology and evolution of viruses infecting uncult populations. The capacity to formulate link between virus-host systems in nature is recognized, but use of single genomics to explore link uncult communities represents a watershed in illuminating viral matter and its role in modulating interaction in natural and engineered ecosystems.

The study combined metagen and single genomic sequencing to explore virus-host interactions within uncult bacteria inhabiting OM. OMZs are crucial for biochemical cycles in the ocean, representing hotspots for microbial-driven carbon, nitrogen, and sulfur transformations. Given changing physical and chemical conditions and importance of OM to ocean-atmos functioning, understanding biological responses is critical for predictive capacity. In OMZ, microbial drive matter and transformations are dominated by sulfur-oxid Gammaprote related to chemoautrophic symbts of deep-sea clams and mussels. The relative abundance of microbial and viral is indicated by metagen bases recruited contigs. Virus-host ratio was calculated for each SAG as ratio of viral to microbial cont from same S.

The researchers interrogated 74 virom and 112 metagen from Pacific waters, recovering SUP viral sequences in Saichlet but uncommon in other locales, even proximal to Saichlet. The two detections derived from ET OMZ virome and hydrothermal plume met from Guay basin, indicating endemic SUP populations with potential to modulate SUP-mediated biochemical via metabolic reprogramming. The study highlights role of viruses in metabolic reprogramming from photosynthesis to central metabolism via auxiliary genes. Assembled metagen from SUP hydrothermal samples contain cycling genes.
Researchers investigated the function of TTC26/DY13, a putative intraflagellar transport (IFT) protein, in Chlamdomonas reinhardtii and zebraf embryos. Using live imaging and biochemical approaches, they found that TTC26/DY13 is an IFT complex protein in mammalian and C. reinhardtii. Knockdown of TTC26Y13 in zraf embryos or mutation of TTC26Y13 in C reinhardt resulted in short cilia with abnormal motility. Surprisingly, IFT assembly and speed were normal in dy13 mutant flagella, unlike other IFT complex mutants. Proteomic and biochemical analyses indicated a set of proteins involved in motility was depleted in dy13 mutant. These results support the concept that different IFT proteins are responsible for different subsets, providing a possible explanation for the complexity of IFT machinery.

 Similar phenotypes were observed in zraf cilia when TTC26 was depleted. IFT movement seems normal in dy13 mutant flagella, but specific dynein components were reduced in flagella of dy13, indicating a specific role for TTC26Y13 in transport a subset ciliary proteins. Comparative analysis of flagellar proteins in dy13 supports a function for TTC26Y13 in transporting subset cargo involved in machinery flagellar motility. MicroRNA-9 (miR-9) is a conserved non-coding RNA that functions as a post-transcriptional regulator of gene expression and is one of the most abundant miRNAs in the brain. Although the function of mi-9 has been well characterized in neural progenitors, its role in dendritic and synaptic development remains largely unknown. To target mi-9 in vivo, a transgenic mi sponge mouse line was developed allowing conditional inactivation of the mi-9 family in a spatio-temporally controlled manner. Using this approach, mi-9 was found to control dendritic growth and synaptic transmission in vivo. Furthermore, it was demonstrated that mi-9-mediated downregulation of the transcription repressor REST is essential for proper dendritic growth.

The study defines a key role for mi-9 during postmit differentiation of neurons and presents a double mouse line to characterize the activity of mi-9 in different cell types in the brain. Analysis of the mi-9-SNest-C mouse showed inhibition of mi- results in aberrant dendritic growth in vivo accompanied by conitant impairment in transmission. Experiments using primary neurons and utero transfer indicate the dend changes are cell-autonomous. Importantly, an essential function of mi- in dendritic growth as a regulator of the expression of the transcription repressor REST was described. The involvement of miRN in regulation of developmental and physiological processes and disease is well established, but uncovering the function of specific microRNA has been limited by lack of technologies in vivo.

In this study, postmit stages of neuronal differentiation and a critical role for mi- in control of dendritic in vivo was demonstrated. Researchers investigated the neural basis of social engagement difficulties in autism spectrum disorder (ASD) using resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) in a large sample of toddlers with ASD and non-ASD comparison groups. They found that ASD-related hypoconnectivity between social brain circuitry, the default mode network (DM) and visual and attention networks. Eye-tracking identified an ASD subtype with pronounced social engagement difficulties (Geo ASD) was characterized by DM-occipital cortex (OTC) hypoconnectivity. Increased DM-OT hypoconnectivity was related to severity of social-communication difficulties, but only in Geo ASD. Early social-visual circuit hypoconnect is a key neurobiological feature describing Geo ASD and may be critical for future social-communic development and new targets for early intervention.

The study found that within the first 4 years of life, the developing social brain default network is functionally disconnected from visual cortices and attention. This is important for why social difficulties are a symptom in early ASD and shared across most toddlers. In contrast, heterogeneity in social engagement is important for explaining functionalconnect between DM and OTC. Geo ASD is a factor behind DM-OT functionalconnect and this distinction is a better model than traditional-control or transdiagnostic. Geo is behaviorally severe on different domains, but functional difference is specific to Geo. Non-AS toddlers with developmental delay do not show effects. The magnitude of DMOT hypoconnect specific to Geo was associated with individual variability in social-communication measured on ASD diagnostic instrument.

The study identified functionalconnect between social circuit, DM and low-level networks is important in early ASD. DM-P connectivity is reduced across toddlers, but DM-OT is reduced in Geo ASD. In Drosophila melanogaster, the hematopoietic organs produce two types of mature hemocytes: plasocytes and crystal cells. Plasocytes are phagocytic and functionally similar to vertebrate macrophages, while crystal cells are non-phocytic and produce prophenoxidase, a component of the melanization cascade. Both cell types are generated during embryogenesis and persist into larval stages. Hemocytes in the larva are found in circulation and attached to the cuticular epidermis as sessile cells, forming stereotypical clusters in every segment. The integrity of these clusters is necessary for crystal differentiation, as shown by the absence of lymph-derived hemocytes in cluster-disrupted larvae. Notch signaling is required for crystal differentiation and depends on cluster structure. The increase in circulating and sessile crystal numbers during larval development is a paradox, as mature crystal do not divide during larval stages and cells in lymph do not enter circulation under homeostatic conditions. Crystal number may rely on a population of pro-crystal that proliferates in lar before maturation or exists in sufficient numbers at beginning development to mature into cells. The transcription factor loge (L) is upregulated in crystal development, and its induction in hemocytes is Notch-dependent with Serr as ligand. L+ cells increase in sessile population during third instar, excluding a fixed population of L+ matures into crystal. L+ cells have been reported to proliferate embryogenesis, but not seen in larval stages. The study of hematiet organs and function is essential to understand how mature cells arise and their numbers are controlled.
Researchers investigated the effects of aneuploidy, a condition where cells have an abnormal number of chromosomes, on gene expression in yeast. They found that aneupidy is common in wild yeast isolates and that show lower-than-expected expression at many genes. They generated diploid panels of cells with two, three, or four copies of affected chromosomes and showed that dosage compensation functions at 10-30% of amplified genes. Genes subject to dosage compensation have higher expression in wild populations but show elevated rates of amplification, suggesting copy variation is buffered at these genes. Aneidy provides a clear advantage to strain Y100 by amplifying a gene that escapes compensation. The study presents a model where compensation buffers amplification through aneidy to provide a natural, likely, transient route to rapid evolution.

The researchers devised a CN-buffer score for each gene as the phylogenetic sum of copy number across strains divided by Vm for that gene. Genes subject to dosage show higher scores compared to all and amplified with proportionate expression. Amplified with heritable expression showed lower scores than dosage-compensated. Results show genes subject to compensation can be buffered CN in S. cerevis populations. The ability to buffer toxicity of amplification could facilitate evolution through anidy. Aneidy in laboratory strains can be advantageous under adverse conditions when nonos compensated genes are amplified. FT improves with increasing Chr copy in Y100.

Researchers investigated how spatial and temporal factors integrate to specify neuronal diversity in Drosila neuroblasts. They tested two models: independent and sequential specification. In the independent model, spatial and temporal factors bind their targets independently, resulting in unique gene expression and identities. In the sequential model, early spatial factors establish chromatin landscapes, allowing later temporal factors to bind distinct loci. They used Targeted Dam to identify Hunch binding in NB-6 and NB-4 neuroblasts. H targets were different in each neuroblast, ruling out the independent model. Each neuroblast had distinct chromatin domains correlating with H-bound loci. Gsbax, expressed in NB-6 but not NB-4, had sites correlated with open chrom in NB-6 but not NB-4. Data support a model where early spatial factors establish neuro-specific chromatin, leading to temporal factor binding and production of distinct neurons in each lineage. Over 100 loci were differentially bound by H in NB-6 or NB-4, candidates for lineage-specific specification. The study explores the mechanism of spatial and temporal integration in Dila neurogenesis, a conserved in vertebrates.
Researchers used photo-switchable tweezers to investigate the mechanism of pore opening of the P2 receptor, a trimer ion channel that responds to extracellular ATP. They found that the inner and outer ends of adjacent pore-lining helices come closer during opening, likely through a hinge-bending motion, and that the extent of pore expansion is reduced compared to the ATP-bound structure. This study provides insight into the gating mechanism of P2 receptors and establishes a strategy applicable to other membrane proteins. Photoable tweers were used in conjunction with MD simulations to explore the function of the trimer ion channel, showing a decrease in distance between the inner and outer ends of adjacent poreining TM helices, through bending at a critical gating hinge, is essential for activation of the P2 receptor. The strategy is distinct from previous optating approaches and provides distance restraints at physiological conditions, allowing stabilization of the open-channel state for the first time in simulation. The model differs from the X-ray structure of the ATP-bound state of zf2, particularly in the TMD, with at least three major issues arising from the photoable tweers strategy. Disagreement between the new model and the interunit Cd+ site is questionable, further crossing experiments are required, and the strategy may trap distinct open conformations, evidenced by desensit kinetics and NMD permeability. NMD permeability was insignificant in one case and partial in the other, supporting the idea that P2 fluctuates between open conformations. Rearrangement of TM-TM interfaces is sufficient to drive gating, consistent with recent findings that binding two molecules is sufficient to open the channel.
Cytokinesis, the physical division of one cell into two, is powered by constriction of an actomyosin contractile ring. It has been assumed that all cells divide by a similar mechanism, but evidence suggests that cytoketic regulation in cell types has more variation than realized. The four-cell C. elegans embryo, each blastom has distinct fate specified by conserved pathways. Bacterial flagella mediate host–micro interactions through tropism during colonization and immune activation. The flagellar shaft of some bacteria, including human pathogens, is encased in a membranous sheath of unknown function. Here, we demonstrate that rotation of the sheathed flagum in Vibrio fischer and Vibrio cholera promotes release of potent immunogenic lipopolysaccharide, LPS, found in the flagellar sheath. We present a new role for flagellar sheath in triggering, rather than circumventing, host responses in the squid-vibrio symbiosis. This observation has implications for study of bacterial with sheathed flagella and raises biophysical questions of she-flagum function.

Using the squid-vrio symbiosis as a tool, we uncover a previously unrecognized role of sheathed flagella in mediating LPS release in V. fischer and its pathogenic con V. cholera. The flag sheath has been problematic to study, with no mutations resulting in loss of she in sheathed flagella. A few Vibrio species, including human pathogen Vibrio parahemeticus, encode two flagellar structures: sheathed and unshe flagella. However, pleiotropic effects and technical limitations make direct comparison difficult. The role of she, as relates to motility, remains poorly characterized.

Bacterial flagella are virulence factors facilitating tissue tropism, mediating migration, adherence, and invasion. However, in vertebrate models, it's difficult to separate flag motility from immune activation. Flagella are conserved, but display diversity in morphology, number, and subcell localization. One variation is the presence of outer-derived she. The she remains unknown, but posited to allow evasion of innate recognition. This presents a conundrum: effect of coating a spinning structure with LPS, a potent immunostimolecule.

We sought to study the flagellar she in the squid-vrio symbiosis. LPS is produced by non-growing cells at tissue, high-field conditions. If L is shed from flag tip vesicles, other molecules may act synergistically induce responses. Alternatively, L from tip or base may form vesicles smaller than cell-derived, enabling interaction. Work will build upon discoveries, characterizing L released by V. fischer, identifying squid receptors, and role of flagin in host responses. The phenomenon may be conserved in animal bacteria with sheathed flagella.
Researchers have identified a phenomenon of catastrophic chromosomal restructuring during genome elimination in Arabidopsis plants, a process resulting from hybridization of plants expressing different centromere histone H3. Shattered chromosomes are formed from the genome of the haploid inducer, consistent with genomic catastrophes affecting a single lard compartmentalized within a micronucle. Analysis of breakpoint junctions implicates breaks followed by non-homous end joining (NHE) or stalled repair. Furthermore, mutation of NHE factor Ligase 4 results in enhanced haploid recovery. Heritability and stability of rearranged chromosome suggest potential for enduring novelty. These findings provide a tractable system towards investigating causes and mechanisms of genomic rearrangements similar to those associated with human disorders.

The study demonstrates occurrence of chromosome restructuring when diver individuals hybridize, identifying centrom-based mechanism for instability. Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) infection is linked to several human cancers, and the virus expresses oncogenes that promote cell growth and inhibit the apoptotic response, leading to uncontrolled proliferation. The viral oncoprotein LMP1 constitutively activates NF-κ and is critical for survival of EB-immortalized cells. However, during early EB infection, the virus induces rapid cell proliferation with low levels of LMP and little apoptosis. This study sought to define the mechanism of survival in the absence of L1/NFκ early infection. BH profiling was used to query mitochondrial regulation of apoptosis and defined a transition from uninfected cells (B-2) to early-infected (M-1/B-2) and immortalized (1) cells. This change in cell survival mechanisms is unique to virus-infected and relies on regulation of M-1 localization and 1 transcription by the EB3 protein. The study defines a new role for EB3 in suppression of apoptosis with implications for EB lymphomagenesis.

The importance of EB3 in cell transformation and survival was recognized, but this study defines the molecular basis of inhibition. Plants produce diverse metabolites to cope with environmental challenges, driving diversification of specialized metabolites within and between species. This study measures variation in metabolites across 797 Arabidopsis thaliana accessions, showing a combination of geography, environment, demography, and genetics influence specific chemotypes and their distribution. Causal loci in metabolism contain frequent, independently generated alleles, suggesting within-species convergence. This provides new perspective on selective forces shaping allelic variation influencing local adaptation.

 The study uses Arabid glucinolate (GSL) pathway as a model, a diverse class of metabolites with extensive variation across Brassica, including Arabidopsis. GSLs are important for seed defenses against herbivores and pathogens. Combining GSL measurements with whole-genome sequencing in European accessions, three major loci controlling GSL diversity contain multiple derived alleles recreating same phenotypes using single nucleotide polymorphism (SNPs) and structural variation. These genotypes and chemotypes with geographic distribution show GSL diversity across Europe influenced by demography and ecological factors. Ecological relationships suggest variation selective processes across regions.

 Metabolic variation and between species result from structural variation enzymes or expression, influencing plant response to biotic interactions. Genetic variants influencing chemical derived from gene duplication encoding metabolism, both local and whole genome. Apoptotic caspases, previously considered tumor suppressors, can promote tumor growth and proliferation under certain conditions. In Drosila, oncogenic Ras12 mutations maintain tumor cells in an 'und-like state and transform caspases from suppressors to promotors. Casases activate intra and extracellular reactive oxygen species (ROS), which recruit and activate macrophage-like cells that signal back to tumorous epithelial cells to activate oncogenic JNK. J further amplifies casase activity, constituting a feedback loop. Interfering with the loop reduces neoplastic behavior and improves survival. Inhibition of casases blocks tumor growth and invasion in scri−/− Ras12 clones. Casase-induced ROS are essential for recruitment and activation of hemocytes, which signal to tumorous epithelial cells to stimulate J signaling. This feedback loop is necessary for neop activity of scri/− Ras12 cells. This work extends previous models of E and J signaling from anti-tumor to pro-tumor by oncogenic Ras and identifies casases as components of this switch. Amplification loops have been described in apopt and 'ead' cells, ensuring oncogenic signaling in scri/− Ras12. This is in contrast to scri− mutant alone, where J signaling triggers casase activation and apoptosis.
A study investigated the effect of social interaction on subjective time perception and its relationship with social proficiency. Results showed that motion sequences of agents engaging in social interactions were perceived as shorter in duration than those acting non-communicatively. This effect was independent of visual features or biological properties of agents, spatial-temporal correlations, and causality between entities' movements. It relied on observer's autistic tendency, with socially proficient individuals more susceptible to the effect than less proficient. Mechanism involved oxytocin, with less proficient having lower endogenous oxytocin levels, oxytoc administration promoting temporal compression, and atos antagonist diminishing effect in proficient. Findings linked subjective time with personality trait, social proficiency, opening new avenue to probe social processing at individual level.

 Experiments manipulated point-light displays to dissociate social interaction and motion perception, assessed in varying social proficiency, and examined oxyin role. Results reaffirmed influence of social on subjective time, highlighted idiosyncrasy of time for interactions tied to stable trait - social proficiency. This study investigated the effects of common mutations in the SARS-CoV-2 Spike receptor binding domain (RBD) and its ligand, human ACE2, on their binding affinity and kinetics. Mutations in R are present in SARS-CoV variants of concern that have emerged worldwide, such as the B.1.1.1.7 lineage with the N501Y mutation, which enhances binding to ACE2. The study analyzed the effects of individual RBD mutations and combinations in SARS-CoV Alpha (B.1.1.7), Beta (B.1.351), and Gamma (P) variants. Most mutations increased the affinity of R/ACE interaction, except K417/T, which decreased it. The N501, E484, and S477 mutations enhance binding, while K417/T facilitates immune escape and E484 enhances both binding and escape. The study suggests that N501 and S477 mutations enhance transmission by increasing binding, K/T mutations facilitate immune escape, and E484 enhances both binding and escape.

 The study analyzed five RBD mutations (K417, K417, N501, E484, and S477) and two ACE mutations (S19 and K26) on the R/ACE interaction at physiological temperature (37°C). The R mutations selected have emerged independently and become dominant in at least one lineage, suggesting they provide a selective advantage. The N501, E484, and S477 increase the affinity of R for ACE, raising the question whether this contributed to their selection. Epidemiological studies suggest the Alpha variant with N501 has enhanced transmissibility. A SARS-CoV variant with Spike D614, which increases activity stabilizing following furin cleavage, rapidly became dominant globally.

 The study found that most R mutations (501, E484, and S477) increased the affinity of R for ACE, with N501 increasing by ~10-fold. Increased was due to decreases in dissociation constants (501, S) and/or increases in association constants (501, E). The K/T mutations in B.351 and P decreased the affinity, but the affinity-enhancing N and E484 present in both variants confer a ~4-fold increase in affinity. A preliminary study suggests carriers of the K allele may be at risk of severe disease, but findings did not reach significance.
Researchers have developed a method to map directional genetic interactions in metazoan cells by combining combinatorial RNA interference and single-cell phenotyping. They generated a large interaction map for 21 phenotypic features of Drosophila cells and devised a method that combines interactions on multiple phenotypes to reveal relationships. The network reconstructed sequence of protein activities in mitosis and revealed the Ras pathway interacts with the SW/SNF chromatin-remelling complex, an interaction conserved in human cancer. The study presents a powerful approach for reconstructing regulatory networks and provides a resource for interpreting functional consequences of genetic alterations. The method directly captures epist relationships from multivariate phenotypes after-gen perturbations, often effects that cannot be constructed from single-gene perturb or single phenotypes. The interaction map comprises 136 Doph genes in cellular processes including signalling, chromatin and cycle regulation, and quantitatively scored 21 phenotypes in cells using automated imaging. Gene functions were inferred based on statistical interactions, protein complexes clustered by similarity, and multivariate phenotypes computed directional interactions, revealing logical and temporal dependencies between modules. The temporal order of protein complexes during mitosis was reconstructed, and an epist relationship between Ras/MK signalling and SW/S regulators chrom remodelling was confirmed in vivo. The approach can be extended to detect mixed relationships and combined with Bayesian modelling to improve inference networks. Recurrently mutated genes in cancer were predicted to modify each other's phenotypes.
Heterostly is a widespread adaptation that promotes outbreeding in plants, but its genetic and evolutionary origins are poorly understood. In Primula (primros), hetery is controlled by the Slocus supergene that determines reciprocal reproductive organ arrangement and incompatibility between two morphs. However, the identities of the component genes remain unknown. This study identifies the Primula CYP50 gene, a putative brassinoster-degrading enzyme, as the locus that determines style dimorphism. CYP50 is present on the short-styled S-morph haplotype, specifically expressed in S-m styles, and its loss leads to long styles. The gene arose by duplication in the Primaceae lineage and shows accelerated molecular evolution. Results provide a mechanistic explanation for Prim style dimorphism and shed light on the Sloc as a model for a plant supergene. Heterly is a prominent adaptation that promotes outbreeding in plants, with individuals belonging to two or three morphs with reciprocal reproductive arrangement and incompatibility. In Primula has been the model for studying hetery since Darwin's book. Heter in Prim is controlled by the Sloc super, comprising tightly linked genes determining style, anther, pollen, and female incompatibility. Sorphs carry dominant alleles, resulting in short styles and anthers, while Lorphs carry recessive alleles, causing long styles and low anthers. Fertilization only possible in intermorph crosses. Candidate genes have been described from Fagrum and Sloc linked sequences from Prim and Turnera, but no causal gene isolated in Prim. This study identifies CYP50 as the causal gene and provides mechanistic explanation for style dimorphism. C50 is present in Sorphs, but not Lorph, and its loss leads to long styles. Gene arose by duplication in Primaceae and shows accelerated evolution. Heter evolved once in genus Prim, same genes co-segregate with morphotypes in heteryl Prim species. C50 sequenced transcriptomes of distantly related P forii S and Lorph, C50 exclusively expressed in S, not Lorph. Each exon PCR-amplified from Sorph, but not Lorph. Genome pooled S Lorph detected C50 reads, coverage Sorph half a related gene. Presence/absence C50 maintained trans polymorphism linkage G over 20 years, indicating strong selection.
High-frequency stimulation of kisspept (Kiss1) neurons in the arcuate nucleus (AR) of the hypothalamus (KissAR) evokes the release of excitatory neuropeptide neurokinin B (NKB) and inhibitory neurope dynorphin, synchronizing issAR neuronal activity. This synchronized activity robustly excites gonadotrop-releasing hormone 1 (GnRH) neurons through a mechanism involving glutamateric input from issAR to preoptic neurons. issAR neurons also project to kisspe neurons in the anteroventral periventricular nucleus (AVPV) via glutamate, which burst-firing these neurons and transsynaptically depolarize and excite GRH neurons via kisspein activation of G54 and opening of TRPC channels. issAR neurons play a dual role in driving episodic secretion of GRH through differential release of peptide and amino neurotransmitters to coordinate reproductive function. High-frequency photostimulation issAR neurons releases NKB, which depolarizes and recruits other issAR neurons, while dynin is co-released and acts presynaptically to modulate the release of N. The two peptides govern the synchronous activity of issAR and promote kisspein that stimulates GRH in the median eminence. issAR also communicate with issAV/Pe neurons via glutamate, which stimulates burstiring iss/Pe neurons. Activation of rostral iss releases kisspein to excite GR neurons via G54 signaling, stimulating release of GR at the preovulatory surge. iss, G54, N, KB, Tac3, and GR are required for normal fertility.
Bone morphogenic protein (BMP) signalling plays a crucial role in maintaining pluripotency and favouring mesodermal over neural fates during differentiation, but the mechanisms by which BMP controls differentiation are not well understood. Researchers report that BMP regulates differentiation by downregulating Cdh, an event that accompanies the earliest stages of neural and mesermal differentiation. Loss of Cdh is a requirement for differentiation of pluripotent cells, and experimental suppression of Cdh activity rescues the BMP-imposed block to differentiation. BMP acts prior and independently of Cdh to prime plurip cells for mesod differentiation, thus reinforcing the block to neural. Differentiation depends not only on exposure to extrinsic cues but also morphogenetic events control receptivity to those cues, and a key pluripot signal, BMP, feeds into this mechanism.

BMP acts through transcription upregulation of Inhibitor of Differentiation (Id) factors to prevent neural differentiation. Id factors act as dominant inhibitors of bHL family transcription factors, but the mechanism by which Id block neural induction is unknown. The effects of BMP on mesod differentiation relate to its pluripotency and neural effects are separable events or outcomes of one mechanism. This underlines poor understanding of mechanisms which BMP influences neural and mesermal specification. BMP is required for meserm formation in vivo and used to induce meserm from embryonic stem (ES) cells, but how effects of BMP on mesod relate to plurot and neural unclear.

Researchers examined effects of BMP and Id on neural and mes differentiation using an ES cell system, allowing differentiation to be monitored in a well-defined environment. They found direct BMP target Id is expressed in subpopulation Nan+ epiblast prior to mesod differentiation, in addition to previously described in meserm. They asked events regulated by BMP/Id during plurotent differentiation and found BMP maintains Cdh expression during differentiation. BMP blocks neural, maintains pluripot, and has positive and negative effects on mesod differentiation, but mechanisms unclear. Researchers have identified a protein called C120 that plays a critical role in regulating the maturation of centrioles in quiescent cells. Centrioles are asymmetric structures that differ in morphology and function, and their properties are established and maintained during quiescence. Depletion of C120 in mouse and human cells leads to accumulation of pericentri material (PCM) components, including pericentr, C5ap2, nine, and C170. Elevated PCM results in microtub-nucleation activity at the centros, and loss of C120 leads to aberrant dyin-dependent trafficking of centomal proteins, dispersal of centolar satellites, and defective ciliary assembly and signaling. C120 helps maintain centome homeostasis by inhibiting untimely maturation of the centole, and defines a new molecular mechanism underlying the pathogenesis of ciliies such as Jeune Thoracic Dystrophy and Joub syndrome. C120 localizes to the outer wall of centolar microtules and regulates the distribution of PCM proteins at the centome. Loss of C120 initiates a series of changes in localization and abundance that result in defective ciliary assembly. C120 inhibits the maturation of the centole, which is a multi-step process including accumulation of PCM, assembly of appendages, recruitment of proteins for ciliary assembly, and elimination of inhibitory factors. Depletion of C120 does not result in complete maturation of the centole nor its ability to build a primary cilium. Overexpression of Pl1 can induce centole maturation independently of C120, resulting in assembly of appendages and formation of a secondary cil. C120-depleted cells show decrease in Of1 foci at centoles, indicating defect in centolar targeting of protein.
This study investigated neuron-glia signaling in the developing retina, focusing on the role of neurotransmitters in mediating communication between neurons and Müller glial cells (MCs). The researchers found that retinal waves induce calcium transients in MCs, which were blocked by muscarinic acetylcholine antagonists during cholinergic waves and ionotropic glutamate antagonists during glutamaterg waves, indicating MC responsiveness to neurotransmitters match retinal waves. Using an optical glutamate sensor, the decline in MC transients was caused by a reduction in glutamate reaching MCs. The study elucidated spatio-temporal and molecular pathways of neuron-MC communication in the retina, demonstrating spontaneous activity encompasses both neuronal and glial networks at this age. Physiological results indicate MC express multiple neurotransmitter types, allowing control of MC activity to be sustained as neuronal shifts dependent on cholinergic to glutamg signaling. The proportion of MC responding to waves decreased from P9 to P11, despite retinal waves, due to an increase in glutamate expression restricting glut spillo from nearby synapses. Data indicate MCs in the retina use multiple pathways to participate in correlated activity with neurons during retinal development.
Researchers have developed a novel method to estimate the postmortem interval (PM) in a mouse model system by analyzing microbial changes in the decomposing corpse. Traditional methods for estimating PM, such as forensic entomology, are susceptible to errors and biases. The study utilized high-throughput DNA sequencing to characterize bacterial, archaeal, and eukaryotic communities in the abdominal, skin, and soil of 40 mouse corpses over 48 days. The results show that postmort microbial changes are dramatic, measurable, and repeatable, allowing PM to be estimated within 3-48 days. The study provides a detailed understanding of the microbial ecology of decomposition and suggests that community data can be developed into a forensic tool for estimating PM. A mouse model was used to perform a highly replicated experiment with destructive sampling, accessing the surface and interior of each corpse at each time point, addressing hypotheses that abdominal microbes play a key role in decomposition. The approach contrasts with typical forensic studies using human or swine models where only externally accessible sites are sampled. Using a mouse system allowed assessing intra-individual variation of microbiota present in humans and mammals extends to microbial change during decomposition. The number of samples allowed assessing the extent of intra-individual variation. Biotic signatures associated with decomposition stages, such as development of fly larvae, succession of insects, and changes in biochemistry of corpse-soil, can be used to estimate PM. However, no method is successful in every scenario. Using microbial change to track progression may circumvent limitations because microbes are ubiquitous, located on humans before death, and can be quantified using high-throughput sequencing. Microbes play a role in decomposition, contributing to putrefaction by digesting macromolecules, generating metabolic products causing bloat. A shift in communities occurs at end of bloat when body ruptures, altering cavity from anaerobic to aerobic. Future studies are needed to identify most predictive subset taxa. Regression models were evaluated by holding one sample out, fitting remaining, and using to predict PM for held sample, a more rigorous task than fitting to all points. The study demonstrates microbial ecology of decomposing has potential to be developed into a forensic tool for estimation of PM.

The Hox genes define identities along the anterior-posterior axis in bilaterian animals, providing positional information during embryogenesis. H proteins exert their function by interacting with TALE factors. H and TALE are present in some, but not all, non-bilater phyla, raising the question of how H-T interactions evolved to provide positional information. Using proteins from unicellular and multicellular lineages, we showed these networks emerged from an ancestral motif present in H and other protein families. H-T networks experienced additional innovations crucial for differentiating H functions along body plans. Our results highlight homeobox families evolved during eukary evolution to constitute pattering in Eumazoa.

Researchers used super-resolution microscopy to examine the diffusion of AMPA receptors (AMP) at synapses in cultured neurons. They found that 90% of AMPs labeled with small fluorescent dyes or quantum dots (QDs) were confined to post-synaptic densities (PSDs) for 15 minutes or longer, while less than 10% were extrasynaptic and highly mobile. In contrast, 5-10% of Q-tagged AMPs were in PSDs and 90-95 were extrasynaptic. The study suggests that AMPs enter stable nanodomains in PSDs with a lifetime of 15 minutes and do not accumulate in extrasynaptic membranes, contrary to the hypothesis that AMP entry is limited by occupancy of PSD slots. Using QDs, which are photostable for minutes, they observed the stability of AMP nanodom in PSD over 15 minutes, exciting every 5 minutes. Researchers have identified a mechanism by which neurons respond to acute hypoxia, an increase in voltage-gated sodium current (IN). They found that hypia activates the Smallubiquitin Modifier (SUM) pathway in rat cerebellar granule neurons (CG) and SUMylation of Na1.2 channels increases IN. The time-course of SUMylation of Na1.2 channels at the surface and IN coincide and are prevented by mutation of Na1-L38 or application of a deSUMylating enzyme. Within 40 seconds, hypia linkage of SUM1 to channels is complete, shifting the voltage dependence of channel so that depolarizing steps evoke larger sodium currents. Given the role of IN in hypoxic brain damage, the SUM pathway and Na.2 are identified as potential targets for neuroprotective interventions.

In cultured neurons and CHO cells, SUMylation of Na.2 channels at the plasma membrane underlies the increase in IN response to acute hypia. SUMylation increases excitability by suppressing activity of background K channels and voltage-gated K channels that stabilize cells below threshold and restore to baseline after excitatory events. Researchers have developed a mechanistic model for the thermal response of Ross River virus (RRV), a mosquito-borne pathogen found in Australia and potentially emerging worldwide. The model predicts that transmission peaks at moderate temperatures (26.4°C) and declines to zero at thermal limits (17.0 and 31.5°C). This results in year-round endemic transmission in the tropics but seasonal in temperate areas, leading to a nationwide peak in human cases. Climate warming is expected to increase transmission in temperate areas where most Australians live, but decrease in tropical areas where temperatures are near the thermal optimum. The model illustrates the importance of nonlinear models in inferring the role of temperature in disease and predicting climate change.

The study parameterized a temperature-dependent model for RRV with data from two vector species (C. annirostris and Ae. vigil). The optimal temperature for transmission is moderate (26-27°C), determined by the thermal response of mosquito lifespan. Low and high temperatures limit transmission due to low fecundity and survival at all stages, often ignored in models. Temperature explains the geography of year-round versus seasonal disease and predicts seasonality of human cases at the national scale. The model provides a mechanistic link between geographic and seasonal variation in temperature and broad patterns of disease.

The thermal response of RRV matches other mosquito-borne pathogens, but with some differences. The optimal temperature for R (26-27) fits the range of thermal found for other diseases using the same methods: malaria by Anophe spp. The strongest signal of temperature on disease is in temperate regions where temperature varies along the rising portion of the R curve (20-25). Mean temperatures above and below the optimum may mask effects in linear models. Nonlinear model is critical for estimating temperature on transmission as a few degrees can have positive, negligible, or negative on different parts of the curve.

Field evidence for unodal responses in vectororne is rare, but some constraining R transmission: outbreaks were less with days above 35 in Queensland and populations peaked at 25 declined 32 in Victoria. Statistical of R cases may benefit using nonlinear for R as predictor instead of raw temperature. Breeding availability drives mosquito and disease: rainfall linked to abundance R species and cases.
Researchers investigated the relationship between beta oscillations in the motor cortex and movement onset by recording neural activity from macaques performing a novel neurofeedback task. The task involved bringing beta power to an instructed state and then executing an arm reach. Results showed that reaches preceded by a reduction in beta power had faster onset times than those preceded by an increase in power. Additionally, population activity shifted from a movement state during beta oscillations that were neurofeedback or naturally occurring during reaching. This establishes a population basis for slowed onset following beta activity. Beta oscillations have been linked to various aspects of motor behavior, including movement onset, reaction time, angle, and speed. This study ties together behavioral correlates with hypotheses of motor encoding through population-level activity. The presence of beta was tested on unprepared movements, but evidence suggests it may reflect neural states preparing for cues. Results show beta frequencies are a marker of slowed onset, with underlying activity shifting from a threshold when subjects produce or naturally produce them. Mechanisms of beta were discussed to reconcile discrepancies in reported correlates. Shifting activity of motor neurons by beta generation reduces probability of movement for encoding specific plans. To explore beta and spiking, maca were trained on a center-out and neuro-reaching task. Movement-aligned spectrograms showed clearest desynchronization in the 25-40 band, consistent with reports in motor cortex. Beta limits for neurofeedback were set at 25-40. Simultaneously recorded units showed different activity during neuro-induced beta compared to natural oscillations. Population analyses showed beta promotes a movement-slow state in both tasks, matching behavior.
Researchers investigated the role of TRAF2 in the regulation of TNF and NF-κB signaling in keratinocytes and its impact on skin homeostasis. TR2 deficient mice die at birth, so its role in adult tissues is not well-explored. Keratin-specific deletion of TR2, but not Sph1 deficiency, disrupted TN-mediated NF-κ and MAPK signaling, causing epidermal hyperplasia and psoriatic skin inflammation. This inflammation was driven by TN, cell death, non-canonical NF-κ, and adaptive immunity, representing a clinically relevant model of psoriasis. TR2 has tissue-specific functions that do not overlap with S1. Deletion of TN and NF2 prevented the inflammatory phenotype in TR2-deficient keratinocytes. Results highlight TR2's role in protecting keratinocytes from death and downregulating inflammatory responses, supporting the idea that intrinsic defects in keratin can initiate psoriasis-like inflammation.

TR2-deficient keratinocytes produce inflammatory cytokines via non-canonical NF-κ, generating a ps-like phenotype in mice with slower onset. These cytokines recruit immune cells to the skin, including neutrophils and IFγ+ cells. Non-can NF-κ signaling is a driver of inflammation in epithelial cells, relevant to human psoriasis, as TWE and its receptor are elevated in psatic lesions. Inhibition of non-can NF-κ might be beneficial in chronic disease with TN inhibitors.

TR2-deficient keratin have constitutive non-can NF-κ activation, increasing inflammatory cytokine production, including TN. TR-def keratin do not need bacteria to be stimulated to produce TN, TN-induced sets a vicious cycle of inflammation. Deletion of one of NIK prevents lethality of TR2 knock-out. TR2-def keratin have high levels of N and active NF2, hypothesizing inhibiting non-can NF-κ might prevent inflammation in TR2 mice. However, deficiency in Map3 or NF2 did not prevent ps-like disease in TR2 mice. TR2 mice developed inflammation with earlier onset than TR or TR mice. TR mice developed epidermal hyperasia and inflammation in same time as TR mice. Lesions in TR mice were in sub and forehead, while in TR mice were around legs and ventral.

Depletion of TN and NF completely prevented ps-like in TR mice. TR mice developed lesions at early around mouth and anus. It is unclear if defects are due TR epithelial or hematietic cells. TR was specifically targeted in keratin and its role in epider investigated. TR-def keratin developed epidermal hyperasia and severe inflammation, lesions around head and sub by 6 weeks. No inflammation was detected in skin SP-deficient mice. Lack SP did not phenoc skin defects in TR, arguing against essential role in TR function.
A study investigated the mechanism of synaptic vesicle (SV) recycling via bulk endocytosis, a process that compensates for exocytosis after strong stimulation. Dynamin 1 and 3 double-knockout (KO) neurons, where clathrin-mediated endocytosis (CME) is impaired, were used to gain insight into this process. Massive formation of bulkosomes was not defective, but enhanced in the absence of dynamin 1 and 3. The conversion of bulkosomes into SVs was not accompanied by accumulation of clath-coated buds on their surface and proceeded even after clath knockdown, suggesting independence from clath. These findings support the existence of a pathway for SV formation that bypasses the requirement for clath and dynamin 1/3 and operates during intense synaptic activity.

 Synaptic vesicles (SV) are specialized organelles that store and secrete neurotransmitters at synapses. Following exocyt, their membranes are recaptured by endytosis and recycled for generation of new fusion-competent SVs. Strong evidence indicates clath-mediated endyt (C) plays a role in endytic traffic. Endytic vesicles generated by C have small size, same as SV. However, C does not account for all recycling and alternative mechanisms have been debated. Proposed recycling mechanisms include "k-and-run" and bulkocyt, a high pathway of membrane internalization involving patches larger than individual SV. This predominates under intense activity.

The study examined the impact of manipulations that affect dyn-dependent endyt on process using electron microscopy (EM) with endocyt tracers. Rapid, endytic recapture of SV induced by stimulus was not impaired in synapses lacking dyn 1 and 3, where C is severely perturbed. Conversion of bulk into new SV occurred in these syn, less efficiently than wild-type (WT) syn. Impairment was not aggravated by additional clath knockdown, indicating clath-independence. Observations provide evidence for pathway that predominates strong and does not requireath mediated or dyn 1 and 3.
A randomized, crossover, single-blind study was conducted to identify biomarkers of glucocorticoid (GC) action in 10 subjects with primary adrenal insufficiency and no other endocrinopathies. Physiological GC exposure and withdrawal states were generated within the same individual. A transcriptomic profile from peripheral blood mononuclear cells (PBMC) and adipose tissue, plasma miRNome, and serumomics were compared using multi-omics analysis. A transcriptomic derived from two tissues and multiomic cluster was predictive of GC exposure. MicroRNA (mi-122p) correlated with genes and metabolites regulated by GC exposure was identified (p=0.009) and replicated in independent studies with varying GC exposure (0.01≤p≤0.05). A transcript profile under GC regulation was similar in PB and adipose transcriptomes, validated by comparison to published data from other groups. A circulating miRNA, mi-122p, was correlated with transcript and metabolome findings, suggesting a functional role in GC action. Association between mi-122p and GC exposure was replicated in three independent studies. A clinical study was considered appropriate for identifying biomarkers of GC in humans due to Addison's disease, a rare disorder with absent or low endogenous GC production. Subjects with Addison's disease were studied in random order during GC exposure and withdrawal. GC infusion was delivered via isotonic saline pump, and saline alone during withdrawal to prevent sodium and fluid deficiency. This design allowed within-individual comparison of circadian and food intake. Plasma and urinary cortisol, cortis, and serum electrolytes, glucose, body, weight, and blood pressure were similar between interventions, indicating no confounders. Low serum and urinary cortisol cortis during withdrawal may be due adrenal secretion or conversion in liver and adipose. A hypernetwork model of GC based on PB transcript was used to integrate adipose transcript, plasma, mi, and serum data. The interactome derived from PB transcript contained genes with known GC-dependent NR3 receptor elements, supporting specificity. Gene analysis identified pathways associated with GC, including receptor, immunoregulatory, metabolic, and cell cycle.
Researchers investigated the dynamics of mTORC1 activation in response to amino acids using live imaging of the mTOR component RAP and a cell-permeable fluorescent analogue diucine methyl est. They found that translocation to lysosomes is a transient event occurring within 2 minutes of amino addition and peaking within 5 minutes, temporally coupled with leuc appearance in lysosomes and sustained compared to stimulation. Sestr2 and vacu ATPase are negative regulators of mTOR1 activity in this system. Phosphorylation of canonical mTOR targets is delayed compared to lysosomal translocation suggesting a dynamic and transient of mTOR from lysosomal surface targeting substrates.

 The study used RAP-GFP cells to examine the spatiotemporal dynamics of mTOR activation and its dependence on amino presence. They found that translocation is fast and relatively transient, peaking early and returning to baseline, whereas phosphorylation of substrates continues for some time thereafter. The presence of amino analogue in lysosomal lumen is sufficient to induce translocation of R-GFP. The analogue, a diuc methyl ester with fluorescent moiety, produces sustained translocation due to continuous accumulation in lysosomal lumen.

 The study also examined the role of lysosomes in mTOR signalling, finding that they are key organel involved but with modifications to existing model. Lysosomes serve as platforms for interaction between mTOR complex and regulators, but may not terminal destination for activated mTOR. Activated m may exist in cytosol, not exclusively on lysosomal surface, consistent with known substrates ranging from cytos to membrane-bound to nuclear. The study also found that R, essential for mTOR activation, is not concentrated on lysosomes but localised to ER/Gol endemembrane system.

 Researchers tested leuc analogues for mTOR activation, finding diuc methyl est a potent activator, stronger than singleuc est and leuc itself. They synthesised a compound with doubleuc followed fluorophore, a small fluorescent molecule. The data re-emphasise importance lysosomes in mTOR signalling but provide modifications to existing model.
Researchers have discovered that modifying antigens with glycans, specifically the Lewis X (Le) structure, can alter their intracellular routing in dendritic cells (DCs) and promote the priming of T cells. This modification directs the antigen to the C-type lectin receptor M1 (M1), leading to a Th1 skewing of CD4+ T cells and enhanced cross-priming of CD8 T cells. In contrast, cross-presentation of native antigens requires high doses and TLR stimuli, but Le modification reduces the required amount 100-fold and obviates its dependence on TLR signaling. The Le-induced enhancement of T cell cross-priming is M1-dependent, as shown by reduced CD8+ effector cell frequencies in M1-deficient mice. Moreover, M1-mediated cross-presentation of Le-antigens neither T-transport nor Cathein-S and was observed after prolonged intracellular storage in Rab+AMP+ compartments.

 Studies demonstrate that glycosylation of antigens has a major influence on antigen uptake and intracellular compartmentalization, affecting antigen presentation and the type and strength of the immune response. Modification of the model antigen Ovalbumin (OVA) with Le glycans directs OVA to M1, skewing naive CD4 T differentiation towards IFγ-producing Th cells. Targeting O to M1 through conjugation of Le enhances crosspresentation, revealed by increased O-specific CD8 effector cells in vitro and in vivo. Importantly, M1-dependent cross-presentation occurs at low dose and independently of T-signaling. This cross-p pathway does not involve T-transport and Cathein-S. M1 targets antigen to Rab+AMP+ compartment, where it is present for prolonged periods.

The addition of Le glycans to O obviates the need for T signals for induction of crosspresentation and Th priming in vitro, indicating M1 signaling is involved in these processes. CLR, like DC-S, C9, and D1, are known to induce signaling after their natural ligands, but no pathway has been described for M1. DC pulsed with low OLe (7.5μ/ml) induced CD8 cell proliferation, while same native O was hardly cross-presented. Others demonstrated native can only be cross-presented at high dose (0.5-1.0mg) and with T-triggering. Glycan modification associated increased antigen uptake and crosspresentation but not presentation on M-class II, indicating increased uptake is not a requirement for M and presentation.

The glycosylation machinery is subjected to regulation and depends on cell-type and activation. Upon transformation, glycan profiles change dramatically. Le structures are expressed in brain and de novo on cancer cells and pathogens, and glycosylation of antigens in brain, tissue, tumor, or pathogen structure directs CLR uptake and crosspresentation. Two Le glycans were conjugated to O, but multivalent presentation may alter M1 signaling and presentation and induce anti-inflammatory repertoire.

Researchers investigated the role of glutamatergic drive along the septo-temporal axis of the hippocampus in neonatal mice to elucidate its contribution to the emergence of prefrontal oscillations. They selectively optogenetically manipulated glutamateric neurons in the CA1 area of the dorsal or intermediate/ventral hippocampus at neonatal age and found that despite stronger theta and ripples in the dorsal hippocampus, the prefrontal cortex was mainly coupled with the intermediate/ventral hippocampus via phase-locking of neuronal firing via direct axonal projections. Theta-confined activation of pyramidal neurons in the intermediate/ventral but not dorsal CA1 boosted prefront oscillations. The data causally elucidated the cellular origin of long-range coupling in the developing brain. The study built on previous findings that hippocal CA1 drives oscillatory entrainment of PFC at neon age and used a methodological approach to optically manipulate the neon HP along its sept-temp axis. The results showed that theta-specific activation of pyram neurons in CA1 of intermediate and ventral but not dorsal hippocampus elicited broad oscillations in PFC of neon mice via dense axonal projections. The findings provide insights into the mechanisms of early generation of activity and suggest that differences in theta dynamics along the sept-temp axis result from distinct innervation and intrinsic properties of hippocal neurons.
Researchers have identified a new post-translational modification of the C-terminal (CTD) domain of the largest subunit of RNA polymerase II (RNAPI), specifically the methylation of lysine residues at position 7 (K7). This modification is observed during the earliest stages of transcription and precedes or accompanies phosphorylation of serine residues 5 and 7. In contrast, acetylation of K7 is associated with RNAPI elongation, serine phosphorylation, and mRNA expression. A balance between RNAPI K methylation and acetylation at promoters fine-tunes gene expression levels. The CTD is a large, structurally disordered domain composed of heptapeptide repeats with canonical sequence Y-S-P-T-S-P-S-P-S. Extensive remodeling of the CTD occurs during transcription, with phosphorylation of serine 5 and 7 marking the earliest stages, and productive elongation characterized by increase phosphorylation of serine 2 throughout gene bodies. Expansion of the RPB CTD in number and amino diversity has been associated with complexity in organisms, including vertebrates and plants. Non-canonical C repeats have potential to expand postl modifications, resulting in complex signaling through the C. A number of modifications to non-canonical residues have been identified, and importance for expression of specific genes revealed. Methylation of single C7 is involved in expression of snRNAs and snoRNAs, while acetylation C7 is associated with inducible expression of E-regulated genes. In this study, C-K residues are mono and diethylated in mouse and human cells, and invertebrates. Using cell lines with K-to7 substitutions, show simultaneous mono and diethylation of several C-K residues, including single K at repeat 35 of murine C. Conservation of C-K methylation and expansion of number methylated residues inverte to mammals suggests functional modification across taxa. Model characterized K expansion, e.g., Plasium parasites. Immunofluorescence using antibodies shows distribution and levels in K and K cells. Observations suggest mutation C-K residues compatible viability mouse fibroblasts, and ser phosphorylation and localization independent presence or absence K. Acetylation C-K recently identified and associated induible expression.

Infants with defects in the interleukin 10 (IL-10) receptor develop early-onset inflammatory bowel disease, but whether IL-10 regulates lamina propria macrophage function during infancy and whether macrophage-intrinsic IL-10 signaling is required to prevent colitis in infancy is unknown. Here, we show that signs of colitis are absent in IL-10-deficient mice during the first two weeks of life, but intestinal inflammation and macrophage dysfunction begin during the third week of life, concomitant with weaning and diversification of the intestinal microbiota. IL-10 did not directly regulate the microbial ecology during infant development. Macrophage depletion with clodate inhibited the development of colitis, while absence of IL-10 on macrophages sensitized infant mice to development of colitis. These results indicate that IL-10-mediated macrophage function during the postnatal period is indispensable for preventing murine colitis.

Studies demonstrate the critical role of IL-10 signaling in macrophages preventing inflammatory responses in the intestine, but colitis develops in mice completely deficient in IL-10 or lacking IL-10 in CXCR1 macrophages with prolonged latency. Colitis in infants lacking functional IL-10 is aggressive and presents early in life, unclear whether same mechanisms in adult mice lacking IL-10 operate in infancy. We performed time-series experiments using IL-10-deficient mice and littermate controls, evaluating histology, inflammatory expression, cellular composition of LP, and luminal communities from birth to 14 weeks. Clodate depletion investigated the role of colonic macrophages in colonic inflammation in IL-10-deficient mice. IL-10 specifically on macrophages led to development of aggressive colitis in 3-4 week C57/BL/6 Cd1 congenic mice. Treatment with antibiotics suppressed inflammation in 3-4 week mice lacking IL-10, no evidence IL- regulated microbiota composition during infancy. Results support a model where IL-10 signaling on macrophages prevents microbiota-driven response during third life.
Researchers have identified a Tudor domain protein, SIM-1, which plays a crucial role in the RNA interference (RNA) pathway in Caenorhabditis elegans. The RNA pathway regulates transposons and germline genes, and is mediated by the mutator complex, which synthesizes siRNAs through an RNA-dependent RNA polymerase. However, the steps between mRNA recognition by the RNA pathway and siRNA amplification by the mut complex were unknown. SIM-1 acts downstream of RNA production and upstream of the mutator-dependent siRNA biogenesis. It localizes to subcellular foci adjacent to granules and Mutator foci, distinct condensates that are sites of RNA-dependent mRNA recognition and mutator-dependent siRNA amplification. This suggests a role for perinuclear condens in organizing the RNA pathway and promoting regulation by the mut complex.

The study found that SIM-1 is required for the production of secondary siRNAs from pi-target mRNAs, but not primary piRNAs or PR-1 expression. SIM- mutants have a transgenerational sterility phenotype at elevated temperature. SIM- localizes to peruclear foci, adjacent but distinct from Mutator foci, gran, and Pules, which are named SIM foci. This identifies SIM- as a factor that acts downstream PR- to mediate the production of siRNAs by the mut complex and suggests a role for multiple peruclear condens to promote regulation by the RNA pathway and mut complex.

SIM- localizes to cytoplasmic foci near gran, Zules, and Mut foci, implicating distinct peruclear condens in regulation of mRN by the RNA and mut complex. Researchers have investigated the properties of Purkin neurons in larval zebraf (Danio rerio) using intracellular recordings. Purkin neurons are central to cerebellar function and show bistability in vitro and in vivo under anesthesia, but the existence of bist in vivo in awake animals is disputed. The study demonstrates bistability in Purkin neurons in unanesthetized larval zraf. Tonic firing was seen in depolarized regimes and bursting at hyperpolar membrane potentials. Purkin neurons could switch from one state to another spontaneously or with current injection. GABA or NMD were not required for bursting, but activation of AMPs by climbing fibers (CFs) was sufficient to trigger bursts. Recording Purkin membrane potential intracellular and motor spikes extracellular showed initiation of motor spiking correlated with increased CFSP and depolarization. Developmentally bist was observed after Purkin neuron specification and persists at least late larval stages. 

Purkin neurons of zraf have unique properties compared to mammalian Purkin. Unlike mammalian Purkin, zraf Purkin do not exhibit spikes, but show large all-or CFSP and broad spikes. Despite differences, similarities have been conserved. Spontaneous firing is intrinsic to zraf Purkin as they continue to fire at high rates when synaptic transmission is blocked. Two glutamateric excitation types are observed: strong from CF and weak from PF, mediated by AMP receptors. Bistability in Purkin has been reported in vitro and in vivo under anesthesia, but not in awake conditions. The zraf preparation allows intracellular recordings without anesthesia, observing bist and toggling between states. Based on cerebell conservation, bist is conserved from fish to mammals. Purkin can be tonic or bursting and toggle spontaneously. AMP-mediated CF is sufficient to trigger bursts. GABA inhibition or NMD not critical for bursts. Hyperpolar potential and AMP-activation required for bursts, suggesting CF-EPs activate conductances in Pur to trigger. Three elements of activity in zraf Pur, spikes, calcium, and CFEPs, present after differentiation and persist until larval stages. Bist also appears after Pur specification and persists until 19dp.
Researchers investigated the principles of self-organization and adaptation of the actin cytoskeleton during clathrin-mediated endocytosis. They found that a minimal actin network composed of actin, Arp2/3 complex, and capping protein with attachments to the clath coat is sufficient to generate force for internalization of endytic pits against mammalian plasma membrane tension. Approximately 200 Ar2/3 complexes assemble at sites of endocyt in human induced pluripotent stem cells. Endytic actin filaments selforganize into a radial array with filaments growing toward the base of the pit. Filaments bend and store elastic energy, which supports internalization. The endytic act network adapts to changes in membrane tension by driving filaments to the base of the pit and increasing bending, which supports a higher load and nucleates more actin filaments. Four lines of evidence support the model, including published data and experiments conducted in this study. Experiments showed that knocking down Hip1 in HeLa cells inhibited endytosis, consistent with simulations that a threshold of actin linkers such as Hip1 is necessary for endytic internalization. Results show a branched actin is sufficient for sustained internalization of endytic pits against physiological tension. Actin selforganization and bending allow the act network to adapt to changing loads. The mechanistic insights gained for act in mammalian endyt may apply to local membrane-bending processes carried out by bran act throughout the cell.
Researchers have investigated the formation of macropocytic cups in Dictyostelium, a process that involves the uptake of extracellular liquid into large vesicles. They found that macropocytic cups are organized around intense patches of P3, active Ras, and Rac, and that these patches are associated with a ring of active SCAR at their periphery. The formation of these patches does not depend on the F-act ring and becomes enlarged when RasAP1 is mutated, showing Ras plays an instructive role. Macropocytic cups predominantly form by splitting existing ones. The researchers propose that cup-shaped plasma structures form from self-organizing patches of active Ras/3, which recruit a ring of actinators to their periphery. Macropomes develop from cup projections of the plasma membrane whose walls are driven by actin polymerization and contain a central patch of activated Ras and P3 throughout their life. In Dictyostelium, this patch is invariably associated with a ring active SC at its edge. The ring of active SC is recruited by signalling and drives a ring of F-act to extend the walls of the macinocytic cup. A possible mechanism comes from immune cells, which make linear ruffles that occasionally fold to form circular ruffles, described as diffusion traps that intensify signalling within, leading to formation of a patch of active and P3. However, this does not easily extend to Dictostel, where ruffles are less common and central P3 patch of macinocytic can form when ruffle is inhibited. It remains possible a diffusion forms by r-independent mechanism, for example septin molecules or crossing components within patch. Further, Dictostel patches become larger when signalling is increased by NF1 inactivation, showing Ras plays instruct part in formation. Researchers have determined the structure of the S4 protein bound to a fragment of S2 and found that S4 determines cohes localization through a conserved patch on its surface. This finding shows that S4 targets cohes loading to a specific genomic location, which is separable from its role in establishing sister chromatid cohesion across the genome. The conserved patch on S4 is required to recruit S2/4 to centromeres and build pericenteric cohesion. Cohesin targeting to specific locations is required in all species for centromeric cohesion and tissue-specific transcription programs in multicellular organisms. DNA sequences do not drive cohes loading; instead, it depends on interactions between the loading complex and chromatin factors. The S4-dependent cohes targeting pathway is a feature of the control of cohes loading. The centrom enrichment of cohes loading is not essential for viability, and arm cohesion is unaffected in strains in which this pathway is compromised. There are two modes of cohes loading: one depends on conserved of S4 and happens centrom, and a second opportunistic mode that happens on chromosome and is sufficient to support viability in the absence of the first. S4-conserved mutations would eliminate cohes loading but redistribute events, resulting in cohes levels centrom and chromosome arms.
