This study investigated the role of ADAR (adenosine deaminase) enzymes in regulating gene expression in Caenorhabditis elegans, a nematode worm. ADs catalyze adenosine to inosine (A-to-I) editing in RNA and bind to regulatory elements in target RNAs. Loss of ADs affects neuronal function in all studied animals to date. C elegans lacking ADs exhibit reduced chemotaxis, but the responsible targets remain unknown. To identify critical AD targets in C eleg, an unbiased assessment of ADR-2, the A-to editing enzyme in C, was performed on the neural transcriptome. Development of publicly available software, SAIL, identified 736 A-I events across the neural transcript. Intersecting neural with AD- associated expression revealed edited cle-41, whose expression is dependent on deamination. Restoring cle-41 in AD deficient cells rescued chemaxis, providing evidence that phenotypes of AD mutants can be caused by altered expression.

 The study analyzed tissue-specific role of ADR, both A-to editing and regulating gene in C eleg. Combining neural isolation with RNA sequencing and editing detection, identified 730 A sites in the C neural transcript, including 104 edited genes. In addition to editing, RNA data revealed 169 genes misregulated in worms. Intersection led to identification of cle-41, a gene expressed lower in AD cells with multiple 3' U editing. Transgenic expression of cle-41 in cells rescued aberrant chemaxis of AD deficient worms and is required for cle- expression and chemaxis. Although ADs play roles in editing codons for neuronal diversity, data indicate aberrant phenotypes caused by lack C eleg AD are result altered expression rather than recoding events. Consistent with previous studies, loss components of RNA interference (RNAi) pathway restored chemaxis to mutant worms. However, attempts to identify regulated by ADs were unable to pinpoint transcripts underlying behavior. Transcript-wide studies of expression in RNA mutants identified elevated cle- in mutants.

 The study linked phenotype with loss C AD enzymes to editing regulation of specific gene. This is consistent with previous data indicating similar chemaxis worms lacking AD or both AD. Despite similarity phenotypes known decade, this is first to identify target whose expression regulated by C eleg ADs. The study highlights need to assess editing and expression at tissue level in C, as molecular not observed in isolated worms. Gene indicates both C ADs important for cle- expression and previous genome-wide suggested cle- important locomotion/chemaxis. To assess role, a line expressing cle- under pan-neural promoter was generated. Transgenic wild-type generated microinjected to C AD mutant worms. Expression cle- from transgenic in both wild and AD worms confirmed by qRT on isolated cells. Data consistent with coding events isolated whole-worms, identified nearly same transcripts, including heavily edited histone genes. This indicates recoding events enriched in C nervous system and role C ADs not generate proteomic but fine-tune expression.
Influenza A virus (IAV) employs a host shutoff strategy to repress cellular translation and concomantly allow efficient translation of viral mRNAs. Using RNA-sequencing and ribosome profiling, researchers explored the mechanisms utilized by IAV to induce shutoff. Results show that viral mRNAs are preferentially translated and decline in cellular synthesis is mediated by viral takeover of the mRNA pool. Measurements uncover variability in levels of cellular transcripts, revealing short mRNAs less affected by IAV. mRNAs refractory to IAV are enriched in maintenance processes such as oxidative phosphorylation. Continuous phosphorylation activity is important for viral propagation. IAV shutoff is achieved by reduction in cellular mRN levels, with viral mRN taking over 53.8% of translation activity at 8 hours post-infection. PA endonuclease activity plays a dominant role in shutoff during IAV infection. Transcripts are differently affected by IAV, with length and GC content related to reduction. mRN encoding oxidative phosphorylation proteins remain stable throughout infection, supporting energetic demands for replication. Oxidative phosphorylation components and ribosomal proteins are barely affected by IAV, while DNA repair and cell cycle genes are drastically downregulated. Inhibition of oxidative phosphorylation impairs viral replication, suggesting continuous expression is important for propagation.
Researchers investigated the regulation of FGF (fibroblast growth factor) gradient formation in the Drosophila larval wing imaginal disc. They found that FGF produced in the wing disc diffuses to the air sac-primord (ASP) through FG-containing cyton that extend from the ASP to contact the wing disc. The number of F-receiving cyton extended by ASP cells decreases with distance from the source, generating a tissue-specific F gradient. F acts as a morphogen in ASP, activating concentration-dependent gene expression of pointed-P at higher and cut at lower levels. Pointed-P and Cut antagonize each other, differentially regulating formation of FG-containing cyton, creating regions with higher or lower numbers of F-receiving cyton. These results reveal a mechanism where morphogens self-generate tissue-specific gradients through feedback regulation of cyton-mediated dispersion.

The study focused on the Doph larval ASP due to its unique features: the wing disc-source is spatially separated from recipient epithelium, B signals exclusively to ASP, and wing cells between disc and ASP do not express B receptor. Genome-ed B:FP and B:Cherry constructs revealed B distribution with high sensitivity, direct visualization of endogenous and signaling gradient. A regulatory loop controls generation and reinforcement of different cyton and B levels, making contours of B gradient robust, tissue-specific and self-sust. Roles of target genes explain variable shapes B gradients sculpted with growth and development recipient tissues.

A morph model proposes gradients generated and scaled by ratio of diffusible molecules, morph and contractor, emanating from tissue poles, eliciting non-autonomous feedback control each other's distribution. Cut and P are intracellular factors, counteracting feedback, regulating signal cyton as function of levels, self-generate gradients adopting recipient shape. This mechanism implies feedback shape result growth patterning recipient, which controls. An ectopic B source near trache branch initiates local gradient cyton, polarized signaling, and neo-organization.

The study demonstrates inductive B gradient for branching morphogenesis, dynamic, adaptable, and receptor-bound within tissue. B self-generates variable branch-specific shapes regulating cyton-mediated transport. Self-generation tissue-specific may be common strategy sustaining robust, variable shapes migrating tissues. Consistent view, chemine Ccl12 forms self-generating zebraf lateral.
Researchers have discovered a new type of protein structure called the α/β coiled, which is formed by inserting two or six residues into the hept repeat of a parallel trimer coiled coil. This insertion strains the supercoil, causing the formation of short β-strands that move the path of the chain by 120° around the trimer axis. The result is an α/β coiled that retains one backbone bond per unit from the parent coiled. The study shows that a novel structure is possible within allowed regions of Ramachand space with minor mutations to known fold. The α/ coiled is built of nonads and contains six residues in the α region of Ramand plot, retaining one hydrogen bond characteristic of α-hel structure. A minimal α/ co would be built of hexads with three in β and three in α of Ramand. Researchers have not detected co-coil proteins with β-layers in hex spacing nor constructed such structure by fusion of MATDD repeats between GC4-NV adaptors. However, a tail-fiber from Streptococcus pyes prophage 2C3 contains α/ co with four-layers, two in hex spacing. The study identified non in stalks of TA and N-terminal coiled of proary endonucleases in Pfam PD (D) XK, specifically crenaeal representatives lacking coiled-coil stalk. Bacteria contain coiled proteins lacking endon domain but similar to coiled crenae proteins, Therminus T076 belongs to this group. α-hel co are ubiquitous, first described in detail, and only backbone can be computed parametric, making them forefront of design efforts. They consist of at least two-hel, superhel bundles held by hydrophobic core, mostly. Most follow hept sequence pattern, seven labeled a-g, a and d towards core, mostly hydrophobic. Beyond hept, range of periodic accessible, restrained by unpert αelix periodicity. Ideal α has 3.63 residues turn, hept 3.5, hend 11/3 3.67, pentec 15/3.75. Many coiled contain transitions between periodicities. Insertions 3 into hept lead to largest strain, accommodated by 310 helix. Further insertions 2 or 3 lead to loss helical, formation short-strands that form triangular plane, moving path by 120 around trimer axis.
Researchers investigated the function of epsin, a protein involved in clath-mediated endocytosis, by generating embryonic fibroblasts from conditional eps triple KO mice. Triple KO cells displayed a cell division defect and impairment in clath-mediated endytosis, with accumulation of early and U-shaped pits. This correlated with perturbation of the coupling between the clath coat and actin cytoskeleton, confirmed in a cell-free assay of endytosis. Results indicate a key conserved function of eps, in addition to other roles, is a low interaction with SNAREs to generate force for invagination and fission of clath-coated pits. Epsin acts in concert with HipR, but differs from yeast to mammals. An additional function of eps is a low interaction of its EN domain with synobrev 2/V2 that may ensure presence of vesicular SN in budding. Clrin endytosis involves complex factors besides clath and classical adaptors, coordinating nucleation, cargo, invagination, and fission. Eps, a family of evolutionarily conserved clrin-associated proteins, is represented by three isoforms: 1, 2, and 3, encoded by Ep1, Ep2, and Ep3 genes. Eps was identified as major interactor of E15, another clrin coat protein, comprising ENTH domain, ubiqu-interacting motifs, and long tail. Core of EN domain is preceded by sequence that unfolds into amphip α-helix upon binding PI4,5P. Hydrophobic of helix penetrates bilayer, conferring curvature and sensing to protein. Eps binds ubiquitin and interacts enzymes of ubiqu metabolism. Generated cells lacking all three eps by conditional KO, main conclusion is occurrence of coupling between eps and actin dynamics. A case-control study was conducted among 555 children aged 5-17 in Uganda to investigate the risk factors for asthma. Asthma was diagnosed by clinicians. The main risk factors for asthma were: tertiary education for fathers (OR 2.32, 95% CI 1.71-3.16) and mothers (1.85, 1.38-2.48); area of residence at birth, with children born in a town or city having increased risk compared to those born in rural (2.16,1.60-2.92) and (2.79,1.79-4.35) respectively; father and mother's history of asthma; children's allergic conditions atopy; and cooking with gas/electricity. Asthma was associated with a rural-city risk gradient, higher socio-economic status and urbanicity. This study provides basis for future studies to identify environmental/lifestyle factors increasing risk among children in urban LMIC.

The risk among children in urban Uganda is predicted by area residence in life, particularly birth, with highest in towns and cities. This increases with other risk factors such as parental disease, children atopy, socio-economic status and urbanicity. Rapid urbanization in Africa and LMIC, asthma prevalence is likely to increase. This study provides basis for investigating environmental and lifestyle factors increasing risk in urban LMIC.

Asthma affects 235 million globally, most non-communicable among children (WHO, 2017). In Africa, prevalence increasing (Ash,2006;Add-Yobo,2007;Gemert,201;Zar,2007;Lawson,201), particularly urban (Add-Yobo,200;Morgan,201) but causes not fully understood. Asthma phenotypes may have aetiologies(Ltv,201). Asthma factors vary internationally, HIC and LM(DouesPearce,200). Little data on Africa, few suggested residence urban associated higher risk than rural (Both,201;Add-Y,200) and LM(Robinson,201;avi,201). The study investigated the evolution of olfactory receptors in insects, specifically in the primary wingless bristail Lepachysignata (Archaeognatha), the firebrat Thermobia domestica (Zygentoma), and the neopter leaf insect Phylliumsifolium (phasoda). The results suggest that olfactory receptors (ORs) did not arise as an adaptation to a terrestrial lifestyle, but evolved later in insect evolution, with the co-receptor (Or) present before the appearance of ORs. The transition from sea to land meant molecules needed to be detected in gas phase instead of aquatic, and the olfactory system of hexapod ancestor had to adapt to terrestrial detection of volatile air-borne chemicals. One hypothesis was that Or and OR of insect are an adaptation to terrestrial lifestyle. To reconstruct an evolutionary scenario for OR, species belonging to different orders were investigated using morphological, electrophysiological, and molecular techniques. Insects provide an opportunity to study ancestral characteristics and building blocks of olfaction evolved in both insects and animals. Selected species at crucial positions of phylogenetic with functional olfactory adapted to terrestrial detection of chemicals. The collection provides model to study early evolution of insect olfactory. Addressing receptors involved in odor in insects and basal in general, several approaches were applied. Transcript data suggest stepwise evolution of Or/OR complex with Orco evolved in lineage Diconylia (Zygent+Pgota) and functional ofco and ORs within ptery insects. Although impossible to rule out presence of OR, none experiments led to identification either OR or Or in bristail. However, a number of ionotropic receptors (IR), including IRa, IRa, and IRb, were identified in L. yata antennal transcript. FISH allowed visualization of IR-receptors in OS associated with basicon sens. Based on results, propose olfactory of L.ata not based on OR. Insects sens types house OS typically responding different odors. All organisms detect chemicals in environment, sensitivity and range of olfactory is remarkable, enabling to detect and discriminate thousands of odor. Molecular identity of receptors first unraveled in vertebrates. In mammals, many G-protein-coupled receptors considered employed olfactory. Similar number chemoreceptors hypothesized Caenorhabditis.
Researchers conducted a study to investigate the potential herd effect of mass antimal drug administration (M) on Plasmodium falciparum infections in four villages in Myanmar. The study found that individuals living in neighborhoods with low M adherence to M had 2.85 times the odds of having a malaria episode post-M compared to those in high-adherence neighborhoods, suggesting a herd effect. High mosquito biting rates, living with someone with malaria, or asymptomatic infection were also predictors of clinical episodes. Spatial clustering of non-adherence to M, even in villages with high participation, may frustrate elimination efforts.

The study found a group effect of M adherence, evident from three lines of evidence: clinical episodes decreased among groups for longer than the prophylactic effect (approximately 1 month) of administered antimalials, group protective from M in the rainy season following M, and a lag of 1 month between onset clinical episodes in neighborhoods with low and high M adherence. Neighborhoods with high M adherence experienced lower levels of infection than those with or low M. Individuals who participated in M but lived in low-adherence had highest risk of clinical episode, whereas those did not participate in M but lived in high had lowest risk.

The study investigated associations between individual and group participation M, subclinical infections, and clinical episodes post-M. The results suggest living in a neighborhood with proportion of people not adhering to M was a risk factor for acquiring clinical episode, adjusting for individual M and other predictors. The study points to the need considering scale in M and M adherence, as current programs often target a single village or community.

Mass administration can reduce parasite reservoirs and act as catalyst for regional elimination. The concept of herd applies to communicable diseases, if sufficient population participates, transmission cannot be sustained, leading to reduction morbidity and mortality. The aim of M is to interrupt transmission, notion of herd providing additional protection plausible but not examined empirically. The study describes geographic and epidemiological patterns of clinical and subclinical P. falcum malaria in villages undergoing M, the first documentation of herd conferred by M. The increase post-M episodes corresponded an increase in mosquito biting rates, which peaked in one village at same time as another, but in absence detectable reservoir and did not persist.

The study has limitations, individuals did not participate M did not participate screenings after M.

The analysis revealed that TF specificities are highly conserved between Dophila and mammals, and for ortholog TFs, the similarity extends to the level of subtle dinucleotide binding preferences. The human TFs with divergent specificities in cell types not found in fruit flies suggest that evolution TF specificities contribute to emergence of novel types of differentiated cells. TF were expressed in E. coli and specificity using HT-LEX and compared to existing human and mouse-LEX data. A striking level of conservation of TF specificity was observed, with fruit having almost as complex repertoire of binding motifs as humans. Conservation extended to secondary modes of binding and subtle dinucle preferences, suggesting TF specificities not subject to substantial drift. The study reported high-resolution DNA-binding for large fraction Doph TFs using HTLEX, same method used to determine mammalian TF specificities. Researchers have developed a novel strategy to model human cardio-pulmonary co-development using multilineage differentiation of human-induced pluripotent stem cells (hiPSCs). The approach involves simultaneous induction of mesod-derived cardiac and endod-derived lung epithelial lineages within a single differentiation of hiP via specific tuning of WNT and Nodal signaling in the absence of exogenous growth factors. Using 3D culture, concentric-pulmonary microissues (μTs) were established, and alveolar maturation was expedited in the presence of cardiac accompaniment. Upon withdrawal of W agonist, the cardiac and pulmonary components within dual-line μ effectively segregated from each other with initiation of cardiac contraction. This multiline differentiation model offers an experimentally tractable system for investigating cardio-pul interaction and tissue formation during embryogenesis.

The study focused on co-induction of cardio-p progenitors, establishing foundation for future investigations of cstalk between these organ lineages and their maturation. Shared signaling events drove simultaneous cardiac and pulmonary specification from respective germ progenitors. Transitioning co-induced cardiac pulmonary progenitors to 3D culture, alve maturation was observed within 3 days, supported by accompanying lineage. In 3 culture, cardio-p μ effectively segregated into cardiac and pulmonary μ, partially inhibited by W activation. This model delivers in vitro for studying mechanistic interplay between heart and lung during human embry.

Cardio-p interaction during organogenesis has been documented in mouse models; however, translatability to human remains elusive due to lack proper systems. Pluripotent cell differentiation offered means for recapulating investigating organ, and progress toward cardiac or pulmonary specification has been made. This study delivers effective model for studying mechanistic interplay between developing heart and lung during human embry.
Researchers have demonstrated an unexpected flexibility in the mutation rate of Escherichia coli as a response to changes in selective pressure. They found that hypermutation evolves when cells adapt to high stress and declines once cells are adapted. The study used a collection of mutators with distinct mutation rates to identify optimal rates for growth under ethanol stress. Experimental evolution revealed an essential role for hypermutation in de novo adaptation to high stress. Hypermutation rapidly arose with increases in ethanol concentrations and declined once cells were adapted. Cellular mortality was identified as the major force driving the evolution of mutation rates. The findings show how organisms balance robustness and evolvability help the prevalence of hypermutation in various settings, such as emergence of antibiotic resistance and cancer relapse.

The study used E coli exposed to high stress as a model. Multiple mutations interact and diverse trajectories lead to adaptation to high concentrations. A collection of mutators with distinct rates was used to identify optimal rates for growth under ethanol. Experimental evolution revealed an essential role for hypermutation de novo to adaptation high stress. Hypermutation quickly arose with increases in ethanol and rapidly declined once cells were adapted. Mortality was identified as the major force driving the evolution of mutation. The findings show how organisms balance robust and evolvability help the prevalence of hypermutation in various settings, ranging from emergence of antibiotic in microbes to cancer relapse upon chemotherapy.

A collection of E coli displaying a range of mutation rates was grown in 5 EtOH. The concentration completely inhibits growth and reduces the carrying capacity of wild-type, indicating extreme stress. Growth and lag reflect fitness in a specific environment. Parameters are contingent on initial size. The effect of beneficial on growth and lag is mitigated by large population and amplified by small. Tested wild and mut strains in presence 5 Et for small and large population. The latter showed overlapping curves, while small led to dispersed, indicating contribution mutation to adaptive under stress. Surprisingly, large led to lower yield than small. Growth from small is driven by mutations, while effect beneficial might be mitigated starting large.

Metabolic adjustment to changing conditions, particularly balancing growth and defense, is crucial for organisms to survive. The conserved AMPK/Sn1/SRK kinases are well-known metabolic regulators in low-energy response in animals and plants. They act at two levels: modulating activity of key enzymes and transcriptional reprogramming. While the first is established, the latter is partially understood in animals and not in plants. This study identified Arabidopsis transcription factor bZIP as regulator of starvation and direct target of Sn1 kinase. Phosphorylation of bZIP by Sn1 changed its dimer preference, affecting gene expression and ultimately metabolism. b63 knock-out exhibited starvation-related phenotypes, which could be functionally complemented by wild bZIP, not by version harboring mutations in identified Sn1 sites.

Flexibility in regulation of expression is crucial for organisms to adjust metabolism to changing conditions. Under stress, energy need to be balanced between defense and growth. Sn1 in plants and its orths sucrose-nonferment (Sn1) in yeast and AMP protein (AMP) in mammals are crucial regulators of homeostasis. Sn is involved in regulation, development, and response, and required for fermentative to oxidative in absence of glucose, and regulates glucose, lipid, protein, and biogenesis in animals. They are activated under starvation and trigger reprogram to slow processes and on pathways in order to survive conditions. This happens two ways: direct modulation of key enzymes and transcriptional reprogramming. In plants, the latter regulation is poorly understood. Arabidopsis prots overexpression of AK10, catalytic of Sn complex, resulted in transcription profile and led to identification of putative Sn target.

Researchers have identified Ring protein 10 (RNF10) as a novel synapse-to-nucle messenger that encodes NMDA receptors in the hippocampus. NF10 is activated by calcium signals at the postsynaptic compartment and elicits changes at the transcriptional level. NF is enriched at excitatory synapses where it associates with the Glu2 subunit of NMD receptors (NARs). Activation of Glu2-containing NAR and induction of long-term potentiation (LTP) lead to translocation of NF from dendritic segments and dendrites to the nucleus. Importantly, evidence is provided for import-dependent long-distance transport from syn-to-dend compartments to the nucleus. NF silencing prevents the maintenance of LTP as well as L-dependent modifications of dendritic spines.

 NF is highly expressed in the nucleus as well as at synapses where it is part of the NAR complex and directly interacts with the cytoplasmic tail of Glu2 subunit NAR. NF dissociates from NAR in activity-dependent and evidence is provided for import-dependent long transport from syn-toend compartments to the nucleus. The intracellular domain of Gl2 contains sequences for signaling and scaffolding molecules. NF and Ca share the same Gl2 binding region. Results show preferential formation of NF/Glu2 complex in resting conditions is disrupted in favor of Ca/Glu2 complex following synaptic-dependent calcium influx. NF binding to Gl2 plays a role for NF anchoring at excitatory synapse but interferes with formation of Ca/NAR complex. NF presence in proximity to receptor allows rapid dissociation NF/Glu complex following NAR activation and formation NF/import complex. Interaction of NF with import represents key for nuclearlocation. NF can interact with majority import isoforms including import previously associated with NAR.

 NF silencing leads to significant decrease in spine density fully rescued by co-transfection shRNA NF construct. Direct consequence is reduction in expression proteins localized at excitatory PSD such as PSD, AMPAR Gl subunit and NAR Gl2 sub. Overexpression NF construct unable translocate to nucleus leads significant reduction PSD puncta along dendrites indicating impaired NF-mediated synucle signaling not reduction NF levels responsible reduction dendritic density following NF silencing.

The data show that Mot1 reduces DNA:NC interactions and unbends DNA compared to the TBP:DNA:NC state, suggesting that Mot primes TBP:NC displacement in an ATP-independent manner. Electron microscopy and cross-linking data suggest that the Swi/Snf domain of Mot associates with upstream DNA and histone fold of NC, revealing parallels to some nucleosome remodelers. This study provides a structural framework for how Sw/Snf ATP interacts with its DNA:protein complex. Researchers have designed and synthesized variants of the natural product ternatin, a cytotoxic and adipogenic compound with an unknown mode of action. The new variants are cytotoxic to cancer cells with up to 500-fold greater potency than ternatin. Using a photo-affinity probe, they identified the elongation factor-1 ternary complex (EF1·G·aminoacy-tRNA) as a specific target and demonstrated competitive binding by unrelated products didnin and cyrien. Mutations in domain III of EF1 prevent tern binding and confer resistance to cytotoxic effects, implicating the hydrophobic surface as a functional spot for EF1 modulation. They conclude that the eukotic elongation factor-1 and ternary complex with G and amino-tRNA are common targets for cytotoxic natural products.

The study aimed to identify the target of the cytotoxic natural product tern, characterized as a potent adipogenesis inhibitor. Inspired by the partially elucidated structure and cyt activity of cyclic peptide A, compounds 3 and 4 were designed, up to 500 more potent than tern. Tern inhibit protein synthesis with potencies correlate with ability to block proliferation and photoatin identified EF1 as a plausible. Surprisingly, photoatin cross-linked purified EF only in the context ternary with G and amino-tRNA. To knowledge, specific binding a molecule to EF ternary has not been reported. Two unrelated products, didnin and ansenin, compete for binding the EF ternary complex with the finding that nystin targets domain III of EF (Krast et al, 201) suggests four products have evolved to target a shared surface on EF.

Based on the similarity between A and tern structures, hypothesized replacement of N-Al- and Leu in tern with A-specific residues piolic and de-hydrooleine would confer increased potency. This led to design peptides 3 and 4 as hybrids of tern and A. Compound differs tern only at 6, whereas 4 has dehydroole at 4. They demonstrate tern and variants inhibit protein synthesis with 4 showing greatest. The elongation factor-1 ternary (EF·G·amacy-t) is a target of tern-related peptides, a new class of macro cyclic factor-1 inhibitors.

This study investigated the regulation of vestibular balance mechanisms during transitions between quiet standing and locomotion and between standing postures. Healthy subjects initiated and terminated locomotion while exposed to electrical vestibular stimuli (EV) and the relationship between EV and ground reaction forces was quantified using time-frequency analysis. Discontinities corresponding to coherence periods were observed preceding the onset of movement and during locomotion termination. These results show that vestibular balance mechanisms transition between motor states, suggesting a discrete change in motor control as predicted by optimal theory. Prior to transitions, vestibular influence on whole-body responses is suspended, with suspension of vestib-evoked responses during movement initiation and termination. Changes in vestibulo-motor control occur before biomechanical transition markers. Inhibition of ankle flexor muscular activity during locomotion occurs 150ms before first change in ground reaction force, suggesting balance responses regulated 100-300ms before step-related activity. Results confirm and extend previous work on vestibular decrease at locomotion initiation and increase at termination. A period of null coherence preceding transition was observed in all subjects, providing insight into neural stabilizing mechanisms enabling motion from posture. From an optimal control perspective, distinct processes govern posture and movement. Transition from quiet to movement or posture requires disengagement of current policy to implement new. Locomotor transition disengs standing policy to implement locomotor, and posture-posture diseng balance to posture. Periods of coherence illustrate brain diseng current policy. Vestibular diseng solves classic problem of opposing stabilizing reflexes initiating motion. Biomechanical diseng of vestib influence whole responses necessary for transition and incorporated into planning. Disruption vestib contribution appears to contradict prediction of refer control theory, monotonic shift in body orientation during postures. Data show intermittent use of vestibular during transitions, unclear if shift in body configuration associated. Suspension of vestib-evoked related different sensory gains control quiet and locomotor policies. Vestibular encodes motion in head space, vital for stability standing and locomotion. Influence activation of muscles in posture depends on engagement and alignment with plane and direction of disturbance. Context-dependent responses observed quiet and locom, magnitude modulated during gait phase. Vestib control during posture-posture transitions monitored by quantifying vestib-evoked responses over transition.
Researchers investigated the role of the transcription factor Rpa in the cyanobacterial circadian pathway, which regulates genome-wide oscillations in mRNA abundance. They found that deletion of rpa has no effect on viability in constant light conditions but renders cells inviable in light/dark conditions. The rpa- strain cannot maintain energy status at night, does not accumulate carbon reserves during the day, and is defective in transcription of genes for carbohydrate utilization at night. Reconstruction of carbon pathways with an external carbon source restores energy and viability of the rpa- strain in lightark conditions. The study highlights the circadian pathway's control of temporally coordinating carbon metabolism to maximize fitness in cells facing periodic energy limitations. The circadian program directs expression of anabolic and catabolic pathways in constant light, with genes for carbon catabol peaking at dusk. In the rpa- strain, genes for glycogen breakdown, glycolysis, and oxidative pentose pathway are defective, key for carbon and energy production in darkness. Deletion of these genes results in impaired viability in lightark but not constant conditions. The phosphorylated form of Rpa, regulated by the circ clock, is critical for coordination of anabol and catab and cell viability in lightark. The r- strain prevents glycogen accumulation and cannot utilize existing glycogen at dusk, leading to energy alterations and reduced fitness.
This study presents the first high-resolution structure of an archetypal P-4-ATPase, CoT from Sulfitobacter NAS-14, and provides insights into the mechanism and diversity of heavy-metal transporters. The P-4-ATPases are crucial for homeostasis, conferring detoxification and redistribution of transition metals across cellular membranes, but their topology remains elusive due to lack of structural information. The P-4-ATases have broad cargo scope, transporting ions such as Zn2+, Ni+, Cu+, and Ca+, and are virulence factors in pathogens, making them attractive targets for antibiotics. The P-4-ATases are referred to as cobalt transporters, but metal specificity remains elusive, with some having cobalt specificity and others transporting other ions. The P-4-ATases serve as multifunctional pumps under environmental stress to maintain heavy-metal homeostasis.

 The P-ATases belong to the P-type superfamily, exploiting ATP hydrolysis for transport across membranes. The Post-Albers cycle describes four states, E1-E-P-E-P-E, alternating access and affinity for ions and counterions. P-ATases are subdivided into groups based on conserved motifs and selectivity towards transition ions. This study reveals novel P-AT features, including dual heavy-metal release and internal counterion histidine. The turnover of P-AT is independent of potassium, contrasting other P-ATases. Inhibitory compounds open efforts for drug discovery, as P-AT function virulence in pathogens.
Researchers have identified a chromatin signature that distinguishes genes with mitotically stable allelic expression (MA) in human cells. This signature consists of chromatin marks associated with active (H3K36me3) and silencing (H3me3) occurring in the gene body. The MA signature is present in ~20% of ubiquitously expressed genes and ~30% of tissue-specific genes across cell types. Notably, it is enriched among developmental genes with bivalent chromatin in pluripotent cells. The results open a new approach to study MA that is independent of polymorphisms and suggest MA is linked to cell differentiation.

A machine learning approach identified and experimentally validated a specific and sensitive signature for MA: a gene-body overlap chrom marks for transcription (H3me) and silencing (3). The signal carried little additional information relative to gene features. Applying this approach to various cell types, ~20% of ubiquitously genes and ~1/3 of tissue genes showed the chromatin of MA. Detection does not rely on SN or sample clonality, making it feasible for analysis in primary samples, including comparisons normal and diseased tissue. Findings fill gaps in understanding MA: identified a molecular correlate with predictive power, mapped genome-wide in multiple types, and uncovered relationship between MA and drivers, bivalent, implying functional role in process. Overlap MA and bivalent poses a mechanistic puzzle.

In monoclonal cell lines, predicted MA genes were spread throughout autosomes, interspersed with biallic genes and showed various expression. Transcriptional bias in MA genes was tested using mRNA-seq in two lines with different X-inactivation. Of 48 X-linked genes with SN, ~90 showed evidence of clone-specific bias; rest inconclusive evidence in X-linked genes. Same ontology categories were over or underrepresented in other lines, suggesting different MA affects genes in same type. A group consists of bivalent, associated with overlapping modifications. In embryonic cells, bivalent are transcriptionally silent and carry peaks of H3me and Hme. These play role in determining fate during differentiation; upon commitment, 'po' chrom allows rapidly resolve into active or inactive. A large fraction of bivalent acquire MA chromatin at least one differentiated type, higher compared to silent in ES. Observations indicate upon activation, master genes resolve into state with characteristic MA chromatin.

Histone modifications offer combinator possibilities controlling transcription. To identify specific to MA genes, compared marks associated with MA against biallically expressed (BA). A correspondence would be more pronounced comparing data from same type, not exactly cells. Largest sets MA and BA were in humanoblast. Used these to compare histone data for GM78oblast deposited by EN project. Focused eight investigated marks: Hme, Hme, H, H, H, H, H, H, H, H. Researchers have discovered a new mechanism of control for the AAA+ chaper ClC, a protein essential for bacterial virulence and stress resistance. ClC forms a proteolytic machinery with the peptase ClP in Gram-positive bacteria, and its activity relies on the adaptor protein MecA, which targets substrates and stimulates ClP ATPase activity. Cryo-EM structures of Cl in the absence and presence of Mec show that Cl is repressed in a resting state by forming two-helical assemblies via head-to-head contacts between coiled-coil domains (MDs). Mec converts this state to an active planar ring by binding to MD sites. Loss of Cl repression in MD mutants causes constitutive activation and severe cellular toxicity. The findings reveal an unexpected regulatory concept by coiled-coils controlling AAA+ chaper activity. ClC is a central AAA+ chaper distributed among Gram-positive bacteria, and coiled MDs control its activity by sequestering Cl in an inactive state. This extends the role of coiled MDs as regulatory devices controlling AAA+protein activity. The MD of ClB/H104 function as molecular toggles, crucial for AAA repression in the state and activation by Hsp chaper. Repression by ClB/H104 MD relies on formation of a reing belt around a canonical+ ring by interacting AAA1 and neighboring domains. Intermolecular head-tail contacts between MD are crucial to keep in a reing conformation. Hsp binding breaks interactions and leads to Cl activation. Cl MD cannot function in the same manner due to reduced size, which is too short to span distance between subunits in hexamer. Instead, Cl form intermolecular head-head contacts allowing two Cl layers arranged in helical conformation. Formation involves ATP binding AAA domains. The Cl assembly is defined as resting, restricting binding substrates, ClP and proteins, and not allowing efficient hydrolysis. Cl MD function as switches, similar to ClB104, ensuring repression in ground and activation presence of adaptors.
Researchers used a Drosophila model to study the behavior of adult muscle precursors (AMP), the fruit fly's muscle stem cells. They found that AMPs send out filopodia that make contact with neighboring muscles and keep their filopodia association with muscles throughout their dormant state, suggesting muscles play a role in AMP reactivation. Genetic analyses indicate muscles send inductive dIl6 signals that switch the insulin pathway on in associated AMP, leading to activation of Notch, which regulates AMP proliferation via Myc. The study shows that Doph AMP display homing behavior to muscle and the niche-driven insulin-Not-My cascade plays a role in AMP reactivation.

The researchers also found that Myc, a transcription factor involved in vertebrate myoblast proliferation, also regulates AMP proliferation in Doph. My acts as a positive regulator of AMP reactivation, providing evidence that proliferation of muscle cells is regulated by the same genes in both Doph and vertebrates. To understand the link between insulin, Not, and My in AMP behavior, they analyzed activity in reactivated AMP. Activation of insulin was correlated with AMP proliferation, and proliferating AMP display high levels of intracellular Not and nuclear My.

The study shows that AMP, in addition to cellular projections, also send filopodia that link to muscles, behaving as AMP niche. Genetic evidence that muscles via dIl6 switch insulin on AMP and initiate reactivation, leading to non-canonical activation of Not, which positively regulates AMP via My. The study highlights the importance of non-canonical Not activation and provides evidence for its role in stem-niche interactions.
Researchers investigated the molecular basis of spontaneous firing in substantia nigra pars reticata (SN) neurons, a major output of the basal ganglia, which provide tonic inhibition to downstream brain areas. They used single-cell RNA sequencing to determine the transcriptome of individual SN neurons and found that they express the sodium channel NAL. SN neurons lacking NAL had impaired firing. NAL is involved in modulation of excitability by changes in glycolysis and activation of muscarinic acetylcholine receptors. Findings suggest disruption of NAL could impair basal gang circuit, underlying severe motor deficits in humans with NAL mutations. NAL is also expressed in other brain neurons, indicating findings may be broadly applicable. Results identify cellular responses impaired in absence of NAL, have therapeutic value in humans with NAL mutations leading to motor and cognitive deficits. Many neurons display intrinsically-regulated spontaneous firing, crucial for circuit function, but molecular machinery sustaining firing not fully understood. 'leak' current describes depolarizing component of spontaneous, but molecular identity remains undefined. SN neurons have sodium-dependent, T-insensitive depolar current, attributed to nonselective cation channel, but molecular undefined. Identifying basis important for detailed investigation of properties and regulation of SN inhibition of basal targets. To address, researchers performed transcript sequencing of SN and detected expression of nonselective cation channels. Discovered SN express NAL, prime candidate for carrying depolarizing current. Using pharmacological and genetic techniques, showed NAL functionally active in SN, important for maintaining firing, and required for sensitivity to glycol and muscarinic receptor stimulation.
Researchers have developed a droplet-based microfluidic system to cultivate and screen for antibiotic-producing bacteria from complex microbial communities. The system consists of three phases: droplet generation, incubation, and isolation. During droplet generation, single cells are encapsulated in droplets, which are then incubated together in a vessel under dynamic conditions. After incubation, microbial cultures are isolated from droplets by depositing them on agar plates. The system allows for the cultivation of uncultured species and increased bacterial diversity compared to standard plate cultivation. Strains able to inhibit reporter strains were isolated from the system, and a variety of antimicrobial compounds were detected from selected producers.

The droplets can be used to create multispecies communities, which support the growth of dependent species or exert a change in product synthesis. Strains can be isolated after one incubation round, and the emulsion can be broken to start new droplet cultivation from pooled suspension. By passaging community through droplets, replicating organisms become enriched and domestication chance increases. The cultivation and screening strategy is adaptable to fastidious microorganisms, representing sources for natural products. The diversity of natural habitats, especially limited access samples, will be explored with the strategy.

The system combines strain discovery in-dro cultivation with selection of auspicious candidates for product through robust assay, streamlining efforts towards new antibiotics. Microfluid droplets provide genotype-phenotype linkage for secreted products, confined with cell origin. Droplets can be stored, generate incubate large populations under aerobic conditions. A high-throughput screening for antimicrobial using reporter strains in droplets was implemented. Cultivation and screening was verified by culturing complex community from soil sample, compared to agar cultivation. Isolates showed antimicrobial activity in dro screening, and secreted metabolites identified.

The system increased relevance by combining with step to detect and select dro containing cells producing compounds activity. Although axenic species not maintained standard after isolation, cells used for high-quality information. The method assists strain prioritization during mining or future campaigns. Cloning heterologous biosynthetic clusters non-canonical architectures pave novel antibiotics undetected. To increase taxa in droplets, perfluor oil dissolved gases during incubation, microaerobic or anaobic enabled.
Tuberculous meningitis (TBM) is a severe form of tuberculosis with high mortality, particularly in HIV-positive individuals. Adjunctive anti-inflammatory corticosteroids, such as dexamethasone, reduce mortality by 30% in HIV-negative patients, suggesting that inflammatory pathophysiology causes only a subset of deaths. In Vietnam, the benefit of dexamethasone was most pronounced in patients with the C/T variant of the leukotri A hydrolase (L4) gene, which regulates inflammatory eicosoids. L4 TT patients with increased expression had survival, consistent with corticoids benefiting individuals with hyper-inflammatory responses. However, an Indonesia study did not find an L4 TT survival benefit. Using Bayesian methods to analyze both studies, we find L4 TT confers survival that begins early and continues long-term in both populations, nullified in severe cases with high mortality. L4 genotyping with disease assessment may target glucocoid therapy to most likely benefit.

Two studies of L4 association with TBM in HIV-negative patients were performed in Vietnam and Indonesia. Glucocoid adjunct therapy became standard care due to benefit in a randomized trial, all patients received in both studies. The prediction that TT mortality is less than CC+CT was tested, confirmed in Vietnam but not Indonesia. Vietnam had 18.8% mortality, similar to literature. Indonesia had more than two-fold mortality, with most deaths early, median 8 days versus 50 in Vietnam. Researchers investigated the mechanism of axon guidance in response to netrin-1 gradients, a protein that guides axonal projection in the developing brain. They found that knockout of shootin, a protein associated with netrin, resulted in abnormal axonal projection, similar to netrin knockout mice. Netrin-1 elicited polarized phosphorylation of shootin within growth cones, which promoted interaction with L-CAM, a cell adhesion molecule, and generated force for growth migration. Spatially regulated phosphorylation of shootin within cones was required for ax turning in response to netrin gradients. The study defines a mechano-effector for netrin-1-induced axon guidance and demonstrates shootin phosphorylation as a readout for netrin gradients resulting in directional ax guidance.

The study used gene knockout, protein assays, microscopy, and microfluidics to define a gradient-reading and mech-eff machinery for netrin-1-induced axon guidance. Shootin was expressed in high levels in forebrain commiss axons and knockout mice displayed abnormal guidance, a phenotype similar to netrin knockout. Small differences in netrin concentration elicited highly directional phosphorylation of shoot within cones. Netrin-elicited shoot phosphorylation promoted interaction between shoot and L-C, generating force for growth motility. Disturbance of spatially regulated shoot phosphorylation within cones inhibited ax turning but not outgrowth induced by netrin gradients.

Previous studies proposed mechanical coupling between F-act flow and adhesion transmits force onto substrates for growth migration. Netrin-1 induces dimer of its receptor, DCC, activating molecules including N1, F, and FYN. This induces C42 and Rac1, and downstream Pak. Activation of Pak via these pathways, shoot phosphorylation enhances shoot-adhesion and shoot-Fact interactions, leading to increased- adhesion. The study demonstrates a highly sensitive gradient-reading machinery for ax guidance, with 0.4% difference in netrin concentration inducing 71 difference in shoot phosphorylation within cones.

The interaction between shoot and L-C mediates netrin-induced ax guidance. Lins are used substrates for ax assays and interact with lamin on substrate. The study performed ax assay on lamin and found growth turned in response to netrin gradients, similar to L-C. Overexpression shoot increased retrograde flow on lamin, indicating inhibition of shoot-L interaction disrupts adhesion in growth on lamin. Uncoupling of adhesion by shoot inhibited netrin-induced outgrowth and turning on lamin. The role of polarized phosphorylation within cones elicited by netrin gradients was examined, with majority migrating toward netrin source. Repression shoot reduced outgrowth and inhibited turning toward netrin source.
Researchers studied the acoustic and aerodynamic forces generated by freely hovering hummingbirds to understand the origin of their distinctive hum. They used a 217-microphone array to record the near acoustic field and a new aerodynamic platform to measure 3D forces, correlating the measurements with an idealized model that integrates wing kinematics and aerodynamics. The model shows the hum arises from oscillating lift and drag forces on each wing. Comparing birds and insects, the characteristic timbre and radiated power of flapping wings stem from higher harmonics in aerodynamic forces supporting body weight. The analysis across species reveals larger birds are quieter, elongated flies louder, and clarifies bioacoustic behavior. 

The researchers developed a new aerodynamic platform to measure 3D forces generated by freely hovering birds, integrating data into a model predicting sound due to oscillating forces. The model is ideal, assuming rigid wings, neglecting effects like wingtip, feather, and turbulent dynamics. Nearfield recordings of six hummingbirds corroborate the model's predictive power. Using the model, they determined how flapping hum depends on frequency content across mosquitoes, flies, hawkmoths, hummingbirds, and parrots in slow flight, and how it scales with mass and frequency across 170 species. The findings point to the role of oscillating aerodynamic forces in generating hum. Numerical simulation of the Fow Williams and Hawk aeroacoustic equation showed far hum is driven by aerodynamic fluctuation. Vivo near measurements are lacking, and no model can integrate wing kinematics and aerodynamics without computationally expensive simulations. Hummingbirds are ideal for developing a model due to repeatable kinematics and aerodynamics and similarities with birds and insects. 

The researchers combined aerodynamic platform and microphone recordings in vivo to determine flapping forces and acoustic signature. The aerodynamic platform measures 3 forces up three times wing frequency, microphone measures unsteady up 100 times. The two relate mechanistically if acoustic field originates from oscillating lift and drag. Oscillating 3D forces were recorded with wing kinematics, decomposing lift and drag throughout wing. The lift consists of peak downstroke, corresponding first harmonic, drag of up and downstroke, second. The 3D contact on artificial flower is negligible. The 3 acoustic field was reconstructed from four arrays, showing pressure throughout single wing, fluctuations in spectrum. Peaks at fundamental and harmonics characterize humming. A simple aerooustics model based on governing equations predicts acoustic field, depending on air, kinematics, and oscillating forces. The model shows radial distribution can be represented by net force at center pressure, analogous to propeller theory.
Researchers investigated the roles of chance and necessity in protein evolution by reconstructing ancestral B-cell lymphoma-2 (BCL-2) proteins and evolving them to acquire protein-protein interactions (PPI) specificities that occurred during animal evolution. They replicated evolutionary trajectories from ancestral proteins and found that contingency generated over historical timescales erased necessity and chance as the primary cause of sequence variation. Trajectories from phylogenetically distant proteins yielded no common mutations even under strong selection pressures. Chance arose many mutations could alter specificity at any point; contingency arose historical substitutions changed these sets. Results suggest BCL-2 sequences and other proteins are idiosyncratic products of particular and unpredictable historical events.

The study used a high-throughput phage-assisted continuous evolution (P) system to select for and against PIs. Mutations rose to frequency during evolution were different among trajectories initiated from starting points separated by phylogen distances. A strong role for chance was observed because trajectories from the same point evolved differences; a greater effect of contingency because trajectories from different starting points evolved greater differences. Combined, chance and contingency erased traces between trajectories initiated distantly starting. Predictable states were those unchanged from the point, presumably unconditionally necessary for PIs and conserved by purifying selection.

The study quantified chance and contingency by calculating variance among replicates from the same point and pooled replicates from different points. The effect of chance was quantified by comparing outcomes from the same point; contingency by outcomes from different points. This design allowed characterizing factors over phylogen time and dissecting genetic basis they emerged. Contingency's strong effect on evolution and acquisition of new functions suggests directed evolution will be effective and lead diverse outcomes using different sequences starting points. Ancestral proteins provide routes inaccessible functions from extant.

The study has implications for understanding protein evolution and natural variation. Observations suggest sequence-structure associations in alignments result contingent constraints imposed by events during history. Evolution explanations diversity conservation must consider historical trajectories evolved, not history-free dominant thought. Present-day B-2 proteins and others are anecdotes particular histories reflecting accumulated chance and necessity.
A genome-wide haplotype analysis of 48 sub-Saharan African groups reveals that coastal populations experienced an influx of Eurasian haplotypes over the last 700 years and Eastern and Southern Niger-C speaking groups share ancestry with Central Africans as a result of recent expansions. Most sub-Saharan populations share ancestry with groups from outside their current region due to gene-flow within the last 400 years. The analysis provides insight into haplotype sharing across ethno-linguistic groups and movement of alleles into new environments, relevant to genetic epidemiology studies. The study uses latest tools to characterise population mixtures of haplotype and estimate admixture events, providing a quantitative measure of co-ancestry between groups and identifying dominant events shaping genetic diversity in sub-Saharan Africa. The sample includes 23 groups from eight sub-Saharan countries and the 1000 Genomes Project, with Eurasian groups to understand genetic contribution outside the continent. A dataset of 328,000 high-quality SNPs provides a view of historical gene-flow, and statistical approaches phasing to obtain haplotype for each individual, representing as a mosaic of other haplotype in the sample. A model-based approach groups individuals using haplotype alone, reflecting broad geographic patterns, and finer analysis reveals smaller groups consistent with self-reported ancestry. Gene-flow between populations relate to historical movements into sub-Saharan. The analysis supports the late hypothesis that agriculturist Bantu migrated south the Congoforest before east, and haplotype analyses a complex picture of recent gene in Africa.
To understand how the mitotic spindle adapts to changing cell size during development, researchers created an in vitro system using cytoplasm extracts from Xenopus lavis embryos that recapitulates spindle size differences between stage 3 and 8. They identified kines-13 kif2 as a driver of spindle scaling whose microtub-destabilizing activity is inhibited in stage 3 sples by transportin α and activated in stage 8 when import α partitions to membrane. Altering spindle size in embryos impaired spindle orientation during metaphase but chromosome segregation remained robust. Spindle size in Xenopus is coupled to cell size through a rati mechanism controlling microtub destabilization.

The spindle is a dynamic structure composed of microtules and proteins that must adjust to cell size to segregate chromosomes and specify cleavage orientation. However, mechanisms coordinating spindle and size are poorly understood and altering them during vertebrate development is unknown. Xenopus lavis development typifies the scaling problem, with cell and spindle sizes measured. Following fertilization, the egg synchronously divides 12 times, leading to a 100-fold decrease in cell volume and 20-fold in diameter by midula stage 8.5. Initially, the cortically meiosis spindle segregates a short, generating a polar that preserves cytoplasm. During early cleages, spindle is small compared to cell, and cytoplasm mechanisms operate in vivo size is maintained in extracts from eggs or two-cell embryos. As cell and spindle converge, a ratio is established and spindle reduces by end of synchronous divisions.

The balance of nucleation, transport, and destabil are critical for spindle in extracts. Comparing Xen species, meiotic size is controlled by the sever protein katan. However, genetically differences cannot play in early development with a single background and transcription repressed. In Caenorhabitis development, spindle correlates with centome and decay of TP-1 along MTs. In contrast, assembly does not require centomes and depends on chrom-centered RanG, releasing factors from transportin and its α binds nuclear sequence-containing cargos. Gradients and centomes expected spindle architecture and size, influenced by availability.
Researchers have discovered riboswitch aptamers for phosphoribosylrophosphate (PR) that exist in either single arrangements or tandem with guanine apters in bacteria. The guanine-PR apters can bind ligands independently or in combination, approximating an IMPLY logic gate to regulate transcription of messenger RNAs for de novo purine biosynthesis. This discovery supports the hypothesis that RNA organisms could manage complex metabolic states without protein factors. The IM logic table indicates gene expression should be on when neither ligand is present, when PR is bound by RNA, and when guanine is available, and off when guanine is bound and PR is not. The tandem arrangement forms an antiterator stem by base pairing between apters, similar to alloster-regulated ribwitchribozymes. Alternatively, apters compete due to conserved sequences and structures. The precise mechanism is unknown, but unoccupied PR apt permits stem formation, while occupied guanine destabilizes PR.

The PR is essential for RNA biosynthesis in all domains of life and likely important in early evolution. Representatives of PR ribwitches were identified over a decade ago but were difficult to validate due to similarity to guidine and pp ribwitches. Members of other classes have altered ligand specificities by nucleotide mutations. Gu ribwitches changed to adenine and 2-deoxyguine, FM ribwitch to closely related ligand, and Mo ribwitch to tungsten cofactor. The gu-PP ribwitch promotes synthesis but not GDP or GTP synthesis inhibited by pp. The gu-PR tandem arrangement approximates IM logic, joining NOR gate by SAM and Ado ribwitch and AND by T and pp ribRNAs. Tandoswitchribozyme senses c-G and guanine or derivatives to AND gate.

A 106 nucleotide singlet PR aptamer was examined by in-line probing, showing structural modulation at conserved nucleides upon PR addition. The changes occur in regions implicated in lig binding guidine and pp ribes. PR extensively degrades under alkaline and Mg+ conditions, so dissociation constants were not accurate. Single-turnover in vitro termination experiments showed gu addition PR reduces RNA cleavage at conserved nucleides, while gu increases. The gu apters are adjacent conserved nucleides at 5 terminus PR in tandem, with 5-7 nucleides separating P stems. Addition PR causes reduction in cleavage, gu causes increase. Results suggest gu binding destabilizes structural necessary for PR binding. Experiments need to evaluate mechanistic hypothesis. The gu-PR system was subjected to in vitro termination assays, consistent with results. Addition PR increases fraction of transcripts, gu increases terminated. Both gu and PR override, fraction approaches zero. Mutations confirm OFF and ON of gu and PR apters.
Researchers have investigated the variability in the number of neurotransmitter-filled vesicles released into the synaptic cleft with each action potential, which dictates the reliability of synaptic transmission. Using mouse brain slices, they demonstrate that the number of vesicles released is regulated by the size of the readily-releasable pool, upstream of vesicle probability. The results point to a model wherein protein kinase A and its ves-associated, synin, dynamically control release occupancy to dictate the number of vesicles without altering probability. These findings define molecular mechanisms that control multivesicular release and functional diversity of synaptic signaling.

Excitatory glutamatergic synapses mediate the majority of fast communication between neurons in the brain. While mechanisms underlying transmission are well-conserved, there is diversity in parameters that dictate reliability and fidelity between regions and species. The number of vesicles released is the product of vesicle probability and number available. Multives release typically correlates with probability, but evidence supporting sole determinant is mixed. Suppression of multives by PK inhibition was absent in synin KO mice. Data suggest synins may be principal of PK at synapses and required for bidirectional regulation of multives via regulation of the readily releasable pool.

Results show PK-dependent regulation of the pool bidirectionally transforms release at synapses (UVRM) without affecting probability. The simplest explanation is the pool a measure of number release-competent vesicles per zone, under control of synins, constraining number released. Surprisingly, this parameter not explored, leading focus on probability as principal of UVRM. To understand pool affects multives across range, a model incorporates facilitation and depression to estimate release and added step for interaction vesicles docking. Size of pool dependent on synin may function assist priming already-docked ves. Phosphorylation of synin critical controlling occupancy and pool upstream probability ves fusion. Ves in pool subject calcium reactions drive facilitation and depression, dictating short-term plasticity.

Cyclic-dependent protein kinase (PKA) increases multives and bidirectionally modifies release via regulation of the pool. Show PK regulation can dissociated from effects on probability and requires synins, family controlling pool. Results support model release controlled occupancy upstream probability, explaining paradox reports multives in high and low. By dissociating requirement high from multives, provide insight mechanisms control and illustrate diverse transmission from common pathways. Model incorporates PK-mediated pool sufficient recapitulate results and reconcile other synapses. PK regulation pool sufficient generate bi-direction shift release (MVRU or UVR) without changing probability, illustrating not required at syn where mode predominates. Changes pool contribute heterogeneity mult across synapses.
Researchers have reconstituted the selective packaging of HIV-1 RNA in a minimal system consisting of purified RNAs, recombinant HIV-1 G and giant unilellar vesicles (GUVs). They found that HIV-1 G selectively packages the 5 untranslated region of the HIV-1 genome in the presence of excess RNA. Mutations in the CACT domain of G, which affect self-assembly of G, abrogated selectivity. The ability of HIV-1 G to package its genome and self-assembly on membranes are interdependent. G-tRNA interactions may serve to increase G fidelity for viral RNA. In the absence of tRNA, any RNA caused a slight increase in G membrane association, but in the presence of 20µM tRNA, 5UT and a longer 194 nt of HIV-1 increased G association more than other RNAs. A model for HIV-1 selection is proposed where the NC of G is necessary but not sufficient for selective packaging of the HIV-1 RNA. Lattice conferring G ability to multimer on membrane is crucial for selective packaging viral RNA. The 5UT is recruited membranes by multized G without changes to secondary structure. Association of G with tRNA serves to inhibit assembly. The fraction of cyolic G which binds RNA has a preference for 5UT but also binds the Rev element, whose function is thought to be in nuclear export. On the other hand, G in membrane has lost t association and is enriched for viral, interacting with equal probability all of genomic RNA. The researchers used SHA analysis to find recruitment of 5UT by G does not lead to changes in secondary, and demonstrated association soluble G with t confers RNA selectivity.
